Sawm — Fasting
beginner
6 min
Reviving the Nights of Dhul Hijjah and Fasting on Arafah and Ashura
States the recommendation, provides proofs, and includes exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on reviving the nights of the first ten days of Dhul Hijjah, fasting on Arafah, and fasting on Ashura, and identify the exception for pilgrims on the day of Arafah.

Core Idea
Revive the nights and fast the days of Dhul Hijjah — immense reward, but pilgrims break their fast on Arafah.

The first ten days of Dhul Hijjah are the most blessed days of the year. The Hanafi school recommends reviving these nights with prayer and fasting during the day, as supported by Prophetic hadiths. However, special rules apply for pilgrims on the day of Arafah, as fasting is discouraged for those standing at Arafat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended acts
The text recommends three specific acts: reviving the nights of the ten days of Dhul Hijjah (i.e., engaging in worship like prayer and dhikr), fasting on the day of Arafah (9th Dhul Hijjah), and fasting on Ashura (10th Muharram).
These are sunnah muakkadah, not obligatory.
2
Locate the primary proof for the ten days
The hadith from al-Tirmidhi states: 'There are no days more beloved to Allah in which worship is done to Him than the ten days of Dhul Hijjah; the fast of each day equals a year's fast, and the standing of each night equals the standing of Laylat al-Qadr.' This establishes the immense virtue and recommendation.
The reward comparison highlights the importance.
3
Understand the specific virtues of Arafah and Ashura
The hadith from Abu Qatadah specifies that fasting on Arafah expiates two years of minor sins, and fasting on Ashura expiates one year. These are distinct merits within the ten days.
Arafah fasting is only for non-pilgrims.
4
Identify the exception for pilgrims
The hadith from Abu Hurairah forbids fasting on the day of Arafah for those who are present at Arafat (i.e., performing Hajj). This is an exception to the general recommendation.
Pilgrims should not fast on Arafah to conserve strength for worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحب إحياء ليالي عشر ذي الحجة، لقوله: «ما من أيام أحب إلى الله أن يُتعبد له فيها من عشر ذي الحجة، يعدل صيام كل يوم منها بصيام سنة، وقيام كل ليلة منها بقيام [ليلة] القدر» رواه الترمذي وغيره. وفي صحيح ابن حبان عن جابر مرفوعاً: «ما من أيام أفضل عند الله من أيام ذي الحجة». وعن أبي قتادة قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «صوم يوم عرفة يكفر سنتين: ماضية ومستقبلة، وصوم عاشوراء يكفر سنة ماضية» رواه الجماعة إلا البخاري والترمذي. وعن أبي هريرة قال: «نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن صوم يوم عرفة بعرفات» رواه أحمد.
And it is recommended to revive the nights of the ten (days) of Dhul Hijjah, because of his (peace be upon him) saying: 'There are no days more beloved to Allah in which worship is done to Him than the ten days of Dhul Hijjah; the fast of each day of them is equivalent to the fast of a year, and the standing (in prayer) of each night of them is equivalent to the standing of [the night of] Qadr.' Reported by al-Tirmidhi and others. And in Sahih Ibn Hibban from Jabir, raised (to the Prophet): 'There are no days more virtuous before Allah than the days of Dhul Hijjah.' And from Abu Qatadah, he said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'The fast of the day of Arafah expiates two years: a past one and a future one, and the fast of Ashura expiates a past year.' Reported by the group except al-Bukhari and al-Tirmidhi. And from Abu Hurairah, he said: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade fasting on the day of Arafah at Arafat.' Reported by Ahmad.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage presents the third recommendation: reviving the nights of the first ten days of Dhul Hijjah. The proof is a hadith from al-Tirmidhi stating that these are the most beloved days for worship, with immense rewards: fasting a day equals a year's fast, and praying at night equals standing on Laylat al-Qadr. Additional hadiths from Ibn Hibban and Abu Qatadah confirm the superiority of these days and the specific merits of fasting on Arafah (9th Dhul Hijjah) and Ashura (10th Muharram). The text also includes a qualification: fasting on Arafah is prohibited for those who are present at Arafat (i.e., performing Hajj), as reported from Abu Hurairah. This highlights that while these acts are generally recommended, there are exceptions based on the situation.

Key Terms
عشر ذي الحجة (ashr dhi al-hijjah): The first ten days of the Islamic month of Dhul Hijjah, considered the most blessed days for worship.
عرفة (Arafah): The 9th day of Dhul Hijjah, the day when pilgrims stand at the plain of Arafat. Fasting on this day is recommended for non-pilgrims.
عاشوراء (Ashura): The 10th day of Muharram, fasting on which is recommended and expiates sins of the past year.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who should NOT fast on the day of Arafah according to the text?
A) Anyone who is not a pilgrim.
B) Pilgrims who are standing at Arafat.
C) Those who have not made intention to fast.
D) Those who are traveling.

Key Takeaway
Reviving the nights and fasting during the ten days of Dhul Hijjah (especially Arafah and Ashura) are highly recommended, but pilgrims must avoid fasting on Arafah.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner
6 min
Fidya for Missed Fasts of the Deceased
Hadith-based ruling for missed fasts of deceased

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fidya obligation for a deceased person's missed fasts, quote the hadith evidence, and identify the amount of food required.

Core Idea
The Prophet's command to feed a poor person for each missed fast day establishes fidya for the deceased.

When a person dies with unfulfilled fasts, the estate must compensate by feeding the poor. This lesson examines the primary textual evidence from a hadith narrated by Ibn Majah and the Hanafi ruling on the amount.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is paying fidya for deceased's missed fasts, and the ruling is wajib (obligatory) based on the hadith.
The hadith provides direct evidence; no qiyas needed for fasts.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith cited: 'Whoever dies while having a month of fast, then let food be given on his behalf in place of each day to a needy person.'
The hadith is from Sunan Ibn Majah with a good chain according to al-Qurtubi.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative verb 'فليطعم' (let him feed) indicates a command, which in Hanafi usul gives obligation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is no intra-Hanafi dispute on fidya for fasts; all agree. The Shafi'i school also accepts fidya for fasts, so it is muttafaq alayh.
The dispute arises only for prayers, not fasts.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that for each missed fast day, half a sa' of wheat (or its value) is given to a poor person.
The text also permits one sa' of dates, raisins, or barley, but half sa' of wheat is standard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللبث في المسجد، قاله الكمال. وقد لزمه بنذره وهو صحيح ولم يعتكف حتى مات، لزمه أو يوصي لصوم اعتكاف كل يوم بنصف صاع من ثلث ماله، وإن كان مريضاً وقت الإيجاب ولم يبرأ حتى مات فلا شيء عليه. فإذا لم يف الثلث توقف الزائد على الإجازة فيعطي الصوم كل يوم طعام مسكين لقوله : « مَنْ مَاتَ وَعَلَيْهِ صَوْمُ شَهْرٍ فَلْيُطْعَمْ عَنْهُ مَكَانَ كُلِّ يَوْمٍ مِسكين » قال القرطبي: إسناده حسن رواه ابن ماجه أيضاً، وكذا يخرج الصلاة كلّ وقت فروض اليوم والليلة، حتى الوتر لأنه فرض عملي عند الإمام الأعظم. وقد ورد النص في الصوم، والصلاة كالصوم باستحسان المشايخ لكونها أهم، واعتبار كل صلاة بصوم يوم هو الصحيح، وقيل فدية جميع صلوات اليوم الواحد كفدية صوم يوم، وعلى الصحيح أنه لكل صلاة فدية هي نصف صاع من بر أو دقيقه أو سويقه، أو صاع تمر أو زبيب أو شعير أو قيمته وهي أفضل عندنا لإسراعها بسد حاجة الفقير.
Staying in the mosque, said by al-Kamal. And if he is obligated by his vow while healthy and did not perform i'tikaf until he died, it is incumbent upon him to will for the fast of i'tikaf each day half a sa' from one-third of his wealth. If he was sick at the time of obligation and did not recover until death, nothing is upon him. If the one-third does not suffice, the excess depends on permission, so he gives for the fast of each day food to a needy person due to his saying: 'Whoever dies while having a month of fast, then let food be given on his behalf in place of each day to a needy person.' Al-Qurtubi said: its chain is good; Ibn Majah also narrated it. Similarly, he gives for prayer each time, the obligatory prayers of the day and night, even Witr, because it is a practical obligation according to Imam al-A'zam. And the text has been transmitted regarding fasting, and prayer is like fasting by istihsan of the scholars because it is more important. And considering each prayer equal to a day's fast is the correct view. It is said that the fidya of all prayers of one day is like the fidya of one day's fast. And the correct view is that each prayer has a fidya: half a sa' of wheat or its flour or its sawiq, or a sa' of dates, raisins, or barley, or its value, and it is better in our school because it quickly meets the need of the poor.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the fidya (compensation) for missed religious obligations of a deceased person. The discussion covers three areas: i'tikaf (spiritual retreat), fasting, and prayer. For i'tikaf, if a person made a vow while healthy and died without fulfilling it, his estate must pay fidya from one-third of his wealth—half a sa' of wheat per day. If he was sick at the time of the vow and died without recovery, no compensation is due. If the one-third is insufficient, the excess requires the heirs' permission. For fasting, the proof is a hadith: the Prophet ﷺ commanded feeding a poor person for each missed fast day. This hadith is narrated by Ibn Majah with a good chain according to al-Qurtubi. For prayers, the ruling is analogized to fasting by istihsan (juristic preference) because prayer is more important. There is a difference of opinion: some say all prayers of a day require only one fidya (like one day of fasting), but the correct view (sahih) is that each prayer requires its own fidya. The amount is half a sa' of wheat (or its flour or sawiq) or one sa' of dates, raisins, or barley, or its monetary value, with the latter being recommended. The passage establishes that fidya is a valid substitute for these missed acts of worship, based on transmitted texts and analogical reasoning.

Key Terms
فدية (fidya): Compensation paid for missed religious obligations of a deceased person, in the form of feeding the poor.
صاع (sa'): A dry measure equivalent to approximately 2.4 kg of wheat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the fidya for each missed fast of a deceased person?
A) Half a sa' of wheat or its value
B) One sa' of wheat
C) One full meal
D) No fidya is required

Key Takeaway
Fidya for missed fasts of the deceased is wajib, proven by a direct hadith, and the standard amount is half a sa' of wheat per missed day.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
6 min
Fidya for Missed Prayers of the Deceased
Qiyas-based extension of fidya to missed prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires fidya for missed prayers via istihsan, and distinguish between the views on whether it is per prayer or per day.

Core Idea
Prayer is like fasting in importance; the Hanafi school uses istihsan to extend the fidya ruling to each missed prayer.

After establishing fidya for fasts, the Hanafi scholars analogized prayers to fasts because both are inalienable obligations. This lesson explores the qiyas khafi and the intra-school disagreement on the unit of fidya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the far' (new case)
The far' is the missed prayers of a deceased person – each obligatory prayer (including witr) requires fidya.
The passage mentions all prayers of the day and night, including witr.
2
Identify the asl (original case)
The asl is missed fasts of the deceased, for which fidya is established by hadith.
3
Identify the illa (effective cause)
The illa is that both fasts and prayers are religious obligations that cannot be performed by another after death.
This is a khafiyyah (hidden) cause; istihsan is used to extend the ruling.
4
Note the istihsan justification
The scholars used istihsan because prayer is more important than fasting; if fidya is given for fasts, it should certainly be given for prayers.
5
Evaluate the ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars say all prayers of one day count as one fidya, but the correct (sahih) view is that each prayer has its own fidya.
The mufta bihi is per prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللبث في المسجد، قاله الكمال. وقد لزمه بنذره وهو صحيح ولم يعتكف حتى مات، لزمه أو يوصي لصوم اعتكاف كل يوم بنصف صاع من ثلث ماله، وإن كان مريضاً وقت الإيجاب ولم يبرأ حتى مات فلا شيء عليه. فإذا لم يف الثلث توقف الزائد على الإجازة فيعطي الصوم كل يوم طعام مسكين لقوله : « مَنْ مَاتَ وَعَلَيْهِ صَوْمُ شَهْرٍ فَلْيُطْعَمْ عَنْهُ مَكَانَ كُلِّ يَوْمٍ مِسكين » قال القرطبي: إسناده حسن رواه ابن ماجه أيضاً، وكذا يخرج الصلاة كلّ وقت فروض اليوم والليلة، حتى الوتر لأنه فرض عملي عند الإمام الأعظم. وقد ورد النص في الصوم، والصلاة كالصوم باستحسان المشايخ لكونها أهم، واعتبار كل صلاة بصوم يوم هو الصحيح، وقيل فدية جميع صلوات اليوم الواحد كفدية صوم يوم، وعلى الصحيح أنه لكل صلاة فدية هي نصف صاع من بر أو دقيقه أو سويقه، أو صاع تمر أو زبيب أو شعير أو قيمته وهي أفضل عندنا لإسراعها بسد حاجة الفقير.
Staying in the mosque, said by al-Kamal. And if he is obligated by his vow while healthy and did not perform i'tikaf until he died, it is incumbent upon him to will for the fast of i'tikaf each day half a sa' from one-third of his wealth. If he was sick at the time of obligation and did not recover until death, nothing is upon him. If the one-third does not suffice, the excess depends on permission, so he gives for the fast of each day food to a needy person due to his saying: 'Whoever dies while having a month of fast, then let food be given on his behalf in place of each day to a needy person.' Al-Qurtubi said: its chain is good; Ibn Majah also narrated it. Similarly, he gives for prayer each time, the obligatory prayers of the day and night, even Witr, because it is a practical obligation according to Imam al-A'zam. And the text has been transmitted regarding fasting, and prayer is like fasting by istihsan of the scholars because it is more important. And considering each prayer equal to a day's fast is the correct view. It is said that the fidya of all prayers of one day is like the fidya of one day's fast. And the correct view is that each prayer has a fidya: half a sa' of wheat or its flour or its sawiq, or a sa' of dates, raisins, or barley, or its value, and it is better in our school because it quickly meets the need of the poor.
Source pages p. 457

Key Terms
فدية (fidya): Compensation paid for missed religious obligations of a deceased person, in the form of feeding the poor.
صاع (sa'): A dry measure equivalent to approximately 2.4 kg of wheat.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of deriving rulings by preferring a stronger public interest or removing hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view, how much fidya is required for a deceased person's missed prayers?
A) Half a sa' of wheat per prayer
B) Half a sa' of wheat per day of prayers
C) One sa' per prayer
D) No fidya is required

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires fidya for each missed prayer of the deceased using istihsan, and the correct view is per prayer, not per day.

Fidya for a Vowed I'tikaf Not Fulfilled
Specific conditions for fidya of vowed i'tikaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which fidya is due for a deceased person's unfulfilled i'tikaf vow and how it is paid from the estate.

Core Idea
A vowed i'tikaf is binding; if death occurs before fulfillment, the estate must pay fidya from one-third of wealth, subject to conditions.

The lesson covers the specific ruling for i'tikaf nathr: the vow must have been made while healthy, and the deceased must not have recovered from illness. The financial limitation of one-third is also discussed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation
A person made a vow (nadhr) to perform i'tikaf (retreat in mosque) for a certain number of days, while healthy.
I'tikaf becomes wajib when vowed.
2
Determine if fidya is due
If the person died before fulfilling the vow, fidya is wajib (he must will for it). Exception: if he was sick at the time of the vow and died without recovering, no fidya.
The exception applies only if he was sick at the time of making the vow and remained sick until death.
3
Identify the source of payment
Fidya is to be paid from one-third of the deceased's wealth. If the amount exceeds one-third, the excess requires the permission of the heirs.
This is a general rule for bequests (wasiyya).
4
Calculate the amount
For each day of i'tikaf, half a sa' of wheat is given to a poor person.
This is the same measure as for fasts and prayers.
5
Summarize the ruling
If the deceased vowed i'tikaf while healthy and died without fulfilling it: fidya wajib from one-third of estate. If sick at vow: no fidya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللبث في المسجد، قاله الكمال. وقد لزمه بنذره وهو صحيح ولم يعتكف حتى مات، لزمه أو يوصي لصوم اعتكاف كل يوم بنصف صاع من ثلث ماله، وإن كان مريضاً وقت الإيجاب ولم يبرأ حتى مات فلا شيء عليه. فإذا لم يف الثلث توقف الزائد على الإجازة فيعطي الصوم كل يوم طعام مسكين لقوله : « مَنْ مَاتَ وَعَلَيْهِ صَوْمُ شَهْرٍ فَلْيُطْعَمْ عَنْهُ مَكَانَ كُلِّ يَوْمٍ مِسكين » قال القرطبي: إسناده حسن رواه ابن ماجه أيضاً، وكذا يخرج الصلاة كلّ وقت فروض اليوم والليلة، حتى الوتر لأنه فرض عملي عند الإمام الأعظم. وقد ورد النص في الصوم، والصلاة كالصوم باستحسان المشايخ لكونها أهم، واعتبار كل صلاة بصوم يوم هو الصحيح، وقيل فدية جميع صلوات اليوم الواحد كفدية صوم يوم، وعلى الصحيح أنه لكل صلاة فدية هي نصف صاع من بر أو دقيقه أو سويقه، أو صاع تمر أو زبيب أو شعير أو قيمته وهي أفضل عندنا لإسراعها بسد حاجة الفقير.

Key Terms
فدية (fidya): Compensation paid for missed religious obligations of a deceased person, in the form of feeding the poor.
اعتكاف (i'tikaf): Spiritual retreat in the mosque with intention, often accompanied by fasting.
نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship for Allah's sake, making it obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is no fidya required for a deceased person's vowed i'tikaf?
A) If the deceased did not make a will.
B) If the deceased was sick at the time of the vow and remained sick until death.
C) If the deceased was healthy at the time of the vow.
D) If the i'tikaf was for less than one day.

Key Takeaway
Fidya for vowed i'tikaf is wajib only if the deceased was healthy at the time of the vow; it is paid from one-third of the estate at half a sa' per day.

intermediate
8 min
Guardian's Donation for Deceased and Prohibition of Fasting/Praying
Explains permissibility of guardian donation and prohibition of fasting/praying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a guardian's voluntary donation without a will and explain the prohibition of fasting and praying on behalf of the deceased, citing the hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
A guardian may feed for the deceased from his own wealth, but no one can fast or pray for him.

This lesson addresses common questions about substituting acts of worship for the deceased. The Hanafi school distinguishes between financial acts—which can be performed by a guardian—and physical acts like fasting and prayer, which are strictly prohibited. The rulings are based on a clear hadith and the principle that worship requires personal intention, which the deceased cannot fulfill.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct rulings
The passage contains two separate rulings: (1) a guardian's voluntary donation (e.g., feeding) for the deceased without a will is permissible and valid; (2) fasting or praying for the deceased is invalid and prohibited. Note the difference in treatment.
2
Analyze the ruling on guardian's donation
The guardian (heir or executor) may donate from his own wealth or inherited wealth, but not from the deceased's estate without a will. Imam Muhammad considered it 'hopefully sufficient' (yujzihi in sha Allah), unlike when the deceased willed it, where certainty applies. The obligation drops because worship requires choice, which the deceased lacked.
Intra-Hanafi nuance: Imam Muhammad's uncertainty does not affect validity.
3
Understand the prohibition of fasting/praying
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'No one fasts for anyone, and no one prays for anyone, but one feeds on his behalf.' The text also uses qiyas: just as one cannot fast for a living person, so after death. Any contrary hadiths are considered abrogated (mansukh) according to scholars of al-Burhan.
The prohibition is absolute; no substitution with fasting or prayer.
4
Trace the logical distinction between financial and physical acts
Financial acts (feeding, clothing) can be performed by others because they are purely monetary and do not require the deceased's intention. Physical acts (fasting, prayer) require personal intention and are thus impossible to delegate. This explains why only financial substitutes are valid.
This distinction is key to understanding the Hanafi position.
5
Apply to practical scenarios
Example: If someone dies with missed Ramadan fasts, the guardian may feed a poor person for each missed day from his own wealth. He cannot fast on the deceased's behalf. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that the donation is valid whether the guardian uses inherited or personal wealth.
The ruling applies regardless of the guardian's relation to the deceased.

Source Text

وإن لم يُوصِ، وتبرع عنه وليه بغير الاعتكاف جاز، ويحكم بجوازه كما قال محمد في تبرع الوارث بالإطعام في الصوم يجزيه إن شاء الله تعالى من غير جزم كما قال في تبرع الوارث بالإطعام، بخلاف إيصائه به عن الصوم فإنه جزم بالإجزاء كما في «الفتح» وسواء تبرع الوارث بمال ورثه أو بغيره، أو الوصي بمال نفسه، إذ ليس له التبرع بمال الميت، ويكون لهم ثواب ذلك، كذا في « الاختيار»، ولا يلزم الولي الإخراج عنه بدون وصية لأنها عبادة، ولا بد فيها من الاختيار فإذا لم يوص فات الشرط، فيسقط في حق أحكام الدنيا للتعذر، بخلاف حق العباد فإن الواجب فيه وصوله إلى مستحقه لا غير، ولهذا لو ظفر به الغريم يأخذه بلا قضاء ولا رضا، ويبرأ من عليه الحق بذلك، ولـو تبرع عنه به أجنبي في حياته صح وبرئت ذمته، بخلاف حقوق الله تعالى، وقيـد صـحـة التبرع بـغـيـر الاعتاق فشمل الإطعام والكسوة، وإنما لا يصح بالإعتاق لما فيه من إلزام الولاء للميت بغير رضاه، وإذا أوصى بالحج يحج عنه من منزله بماله، وإن تبرع به وارثه أو غيره يصح. (ولا يصح أن يصوم) الولي ولا غيره عن الميت (ولا) يصح أن يصلّي أحد ( عَنْهُ) لقوله : « لا يَصُومُ أَحَدٌ عَنْ أَحَدٍ، ولا يُصَلِّي أَحَدٌ عَنْ أَحَدٍ وَلَكِنْ يُطْعَمُ عَنْهُ » ولأنه لا يصوم عنه في حال الحياة، فكذا بعد الموت كالصلاة، وما ورد من قوله : « فَصُومِي عَنْ أُمِّكِ » وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: « مَنْ مَاتَ وَعَلَيْهِ صِيَامٌ صَامَ عَنْهُ وَلِيُّهُ » فمنسوخ كما في « البرهان» وغيره.
And if he did not make a will, and his guardian donated on his behalf, without i'tikaf, it is permissible. Its permissibility is ruled as Muhammad said regarding the donor heirs' feeding for fasting: it suffices if Allah wills, without certainty, as he said regarding the heir donating food, unlike his will about fasting, for it is certain to suffice as in al-Fath. Whether the heir donates from inherited wealth or other, or the executor from his own wealth, since he cannot donate from the deceased's wealth, and they have reward for that, as in al-Ikhtiyar. The guardian is not obligated to pay on his behalf without a will because it is an act of worship and requires choice; if no will, the condition is missing, so it is dropped regarding worldly matters due to impossibility, unlike the right of servants, where the obligation is only to deliver to the entitled. Hence, if a creditor seizes it, he takes it without judgment or consent, and the debtor becomes free. If an outsider donates on his behalf during his life, it is valid and his liability is cleared, unlike the rights of Allah. The validity of donation is restricted to other than emancipation, so it includes feeding and clothing. Emancipation is invalid because it imposes clientage on the deceased without his consent. If he made a will for Hajj, it is performed from his home with his wealth; if his heir or another donates it, it is valid. And it is not valid for the guardian or anyone else to fast for the deceased, nor for anyone to pray for him, due to his saying: 'No one fasts for anyone, and no one prays for anyone, but one feeds on his behalf.' And because one does not fast for him during life, so likewise after death, like prayer. And what has been narrated: 'Fast on behalf of your mother' and 'Whoever dies with a fast, his guardian should fast for him' are abrogated, as in al-Burhan and others.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof addressing two issues: (1) the permissibility of a guardian (wali) donating on behalf of the deceased without a will, and (2) the absolute prohibition of fasting or praying on behalf of the deceased. The text begins by stating that if the deceased did not make a will, the guardian can voluntarily donate food (or clothing) for the missed obligations, and it is valid. Imam Muhammad considered this a hopeful (not certain) fulfillment, unlike when the deceased had explicitly willed it. The guardian can donate from his own wealth or inherited wealth, but not from the deceased's estate without will. The reason is that worship requires intention and choice; since the deceased did not choose, the obligation falls away regarding worldly legal consequences. This contrasts with financial debts to people, which must be paid. The text then strongly prohibits fasting or praying on behalf of the deceased, citing the hadith: 'No one fasts for anyone, and no one prays for anyone, but one feeds on his behalf.' A qiyas argument is given: just as one cannot fast for a living person, so too after death. The hadiths that seem to permit fasting for the deceased (e.g., the story of the mother's fast, and the hadith 'the guardian should fast for him') are considered abrogated (mansukh) by the scholars of al-Burhan. Thus, the only valid substitute is feeding (or its value).

Key Terms
ولي (wali): Guardian of the deceased, typically an heir or executor.
تبرع (tabarru'): Voluntary donation on behalf of another without obligation.
منسوخ (mansukh): Abrogated; the hadith permitting fasting for the deceased is superseded by later evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a guardian fasting on behalf of a deceased person?
A) It is recommended if the deceased made a will
B) It is valid but requires intention
C) It is prohibited and invalid
D) It is permissible only for the guardian

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits only financial substitutes (feeding, clothing) for missed acts of worship of the deceased; physical acts like fasting and prayer are prohibited due to the hadith and the requirement of personal intention.

Two Correct Opinions on Making Up Fasts from Two Ramadans
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the need for specification (ta'yin) in qada of fasts from two Ramadans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two correct Hanafi opinions on specification (ta'yin) when making up fasts from two different Ramadans and apply the chosen opinion to a given scenario.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, when making up missed fasts from two Ramadans, either general intention suffices or specification is required—both opinions are correct.

This passage delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when a person owes qada from two different Ramadans, must they specify which Ramadan they are making up, or is a general intention enough? The text presents two correct opinions (tas-hihan mukhtalifan) from authoritative Hanafi sources. By working through this lesson, you will learn to identify each opinion, trace its source, and apply the preferred ruling in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two opinions
The core issue is whether ta'yin (specification of the missed Ramadan) is required for the validity of qada fasts when the obligation spans two different Ramadans. Two opinions are presented: one requiring specification, one allowing general intention.
The passage uses 'al-Zayla'i' and 'al-Khulasa' as representatives of each view.
2
State the opinion of al-Zayla'i
Al-Zayla'i holds that specification is necessary. The person must intend whether they are fasting for the first or last Ramadan, similar to the requirement in making up missed prayers.
This is the stricter opinion.
3
State the opinion of al-Khulasa
Al-Khulasa holds that specification is not required. A general intention to make up what is owed from the two months suffices. This is extended to qada of prayers as well.
This is the more lenient opinion, and the passage indicates it is the chosen (al-mukhtar) opinion.
4
Identify the chosen opinion and its basis
The final quote from al-Khulasa explicitly states that for two days from two Ramadans, the chosen opinion (al-mukhtar) is that general intention suffices, and the same applies to prayers. This is the mufta bihi position.
The tarjih authority is al-Khulasa, and the passage does not present a decisive objection.
5
Extend the ruling to qada of prayers
The analogy with prayer qada is drawn: if a person owes multiple missed prayers, the two opinions apply similarly. The chosen opinion permits general intention without specifying which prayer.
This shows the scope of the dispute beyond fasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكَذَا الصّومُ الذي عليه من رمضانَيْنِ إذا أراد قضاءه يفعل مثل هذا على أحـد تصحيحيـنِ مُخْتَلِفَيْنِ، صحح الزيلعي كما سنذكره أنه لابد من التعيين فيخرج عنه بنية الأول أو الآخر، كما في الصلاة، وصحح في الخلاصة» في كتاب الصوم: أنه لا يحتاج لتعيين، فيكفيه أن ينوي من الليل صيام غد قضاء عما عليه من الشهرين، وإن كان عليه أيام من رمضان واحد، لا يحتاج لتعيين الأيام بأسمائها وعددها، وهذا الذي ذكرناه في الصلاة هو الأصح. وقال في ( الكنز » في مسائل شتى: لـو نوی قضاء رمضان ولم يعين اليوم [ صح ] ، ولو عن رمضانين، كقضاء الصلاة صح، وإن لم ينو أول صلاة أو آخر صلاة عليه. انتهى. قال شارحه الزيلعي هذا قول بعض المشايخ، والأصح أنه يجوز في رمضان واحد، ولا يجوز في رمضانين ما لم يعين أنه صائم عن رمضان سنة كذا، وكذا في قضاء الصلاة انتهى. وفي « الخلاصة : إذا وجب عليه قضاء يومين من رمضان واحد، ينبغي في القضاء أن ينوي أول يوم وجب عليه قضاؤه من هذا الرمضان، وإن لم يعين الأول يجوز، وكذا لـو كــان عـلـيـه قـضـاء يـومـيـن مـن رمضانين وهو المختار، ولو نوى القضاء لا غير يجوز ، وإن لم يعين، وكذا في قضاء الصلوات. انتهى.
Similarly, the fasting that is upon him from two Ramadans, if he wants to make it up, he does like this according to one of two different correct opinions. Al-Zayla'i corrected, as we will mention, that specification is necessary, so he can fulfill it by intending the first or last, as in prayer. And in al-Khulasa, in the book of fasting, it is corrected that he does not need specification; it suffices that he intends from the night to fast the next day as a make-up for what is upon him from the two months. And if upon him are days from one Ramadan, he does not need to specify the days by their names and number. And this which we mentioned in prayer is the more correct. And in al-Kanz, in miscellaneous issues: if he intended to make up Ramadan and did not specify the day, it is valid; and if from two Ramadans, like the make-up of prayer, it is valid, even if he did not intend the first prayer or the last prayer upon him. End. Its commentator al-Zayla'i said: this is the statement of some shaykhs, but the more correct is that it is permissible in one Ramadan, and not permissible in two Ramadans unless he specifies that he is fasting for Ramadan of such-and-such year, similarly in the make-up of prayer. End. And in al-Khulasa: if he has to make up two days from one Ramadan, it is proper that he intends the first day that became obligatory upon him to make up from this Ramadan; and if he does not specify the first, it is valid. Similarly, if he has to make up two days from two Ramadans, it is the chosen opinion, and if he intends the make-up only without specification, it is valid. Similarly in the make-up of prayers. End.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on making up missed fasts when a person owes qada from two different Ramadans (e.g., missed days from two consecutive Ramadans). It presents two correct opinions (tas-hihan mukhtalifan) transmitted in the Hanafi school. According to the first opinion (ascribed to al-Zayla'i), specification (ta'yin) is required: the person must specify whether the fast is for the first Ramadan or the second, and can simplify by intending the first or last missed fast, analogous to the prayer case. The second opinion (from al-Khulasa) holds that specification is not required; a general intention to make up the missed fasts from the two months suffices. The text then cites al-Kanz, which allows general intention for one or two Ramadans, but al-Zayla'i (in his commentary on al-Kanz) restricts this to one Ramadan only. The final quote from al-Khulasa states that for two days from one Ramadan, it is recommended to specify the first day, but it is valid without specification; for two days from two Ramadans, the chosen opinion (al-mukhtar) is that general intention suffices, and this extends to prayers as well. The passage illustrates the internal scholarly disagreement within the Hanafi school on the degree of specificity required in qada of missed fasts and prayers.

Key Terms
تصحيحان مختلفان (tas-hihan mukhtalifan): Two different correct opinions within the Hanafi school, both considered valid.
الخُلاصة (al-Khulasah): A Hanafi fiqh reference; its positions are authoritative.
التعيين (al-ta'yin): Specification; here, specifying which Ramadan (year) one is making up.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He must specify the exact year and day.
B) He must intend the first day from the earlier Ramadan.
C) A general intention to make up what he owes is valid.
D) He must intend the last day from the later Ramadan.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school offers two valid opinions on specifying the missed fasts from multiple Ramadans: one requires ta'yin, the other suffices with general intention; the chosen opinion permits general intention.

Definition of Sawm: Linguistic and Technical
Defines sawm linguistically and technically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of sawm (fasting) both linguistically and technically, and explain the key components of the technical definition.

Core Idea
Sawm is abstention from entering anything into the body and from sexual desire during daytime – understanding each term is key.

This lesson introduces the chapter on fasting (Kitab al-Sawm) by defining the term. The author first explains the linguistic meaning using Quranic and poetic evidence, then gives the precise technical definition used in Hanafi fiqh. Each element of the definition has legal significance, determining what breaks the fast and what does not.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic and technical definitions
Identify the broader linguistic meaning (absolute abstention from any action/speech) versus the restricted technical definition for fasting (abstention during daytime from specific acts).
The linguistic definition includes silence (not speaking), as in the Quranic verse.
2
Identify the core element and its temporal scope
The core is abstention (imsak). The temporal scope is daytime, defined from true dawn (fajr sadiq) to sunset.
The hadith and Quranic verse establish the boundaries of daytime.
3
Examine the scope of 'entering something'
Anything entering the body deliberately or mistakenly breaks the fast. This includes food, drink, medicine, or even unintended water during rinsing. However, forgetfulness and involuntary entry (e.g., dust) are excluded.
The definition explicitly says 'intentionally or mistakenly' to exclude forgetfulness.
4
Determine the routes of entry
The entry can be through the mouth/nose into the belly (batin) or through deep wounds (ja'ifah) that reach the body cavity, or into the brain (ammah) via head wounds. These are considered routes that break the fast if something enters.
The 'ruling of the belly' includes the brain, so injecting medicine into a head wound breaks the fast.
5
Note the exclusion of sexual desire
The definition includes abstention from sexual desire, primarily intercourse, which also breaks the fast if engaged during daytime.
This is a separate category from entry; it is specifically mentioned.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Book of Fasting. It is abstention during the day from entering anything intentionally or mistakenly into the belly or what has the ruling of the belly, and from sexual desire. The Book of Fasting. Fasting is mentioned after prayer as in the two Jami's, because each of them is a bodily worship. And in the second year of the Hijrah, the fast of Ramadan was made obligatory, and the sacrifice, and the zakat of fitr, and the mosque of Quba was built. Then it is necessary to know the meaning of fasting linguistically and legally, its cause, condition, ruling, pillar, wisdom of legislation, and description. Its linguistic meaning: absolute abstention from action and speech at any time. Allah Almighty said, relating Mary: 'Indeed, I have vowed to the Most Merciful a fast, so I will not speak today to any human' [Maryam: 26], i.e., silence and keeping quiet. And that was legislated. And it is said: the horse fasts from its manger if it stands and does not eat, and the manger is the feeding place. Its plural is araawi. And al-Nabigha said: 'Horses fasting and horses not fasting under the dust, and others chewing the bit.' i.e., refraining from running or from feed, and not refraining. And al-'ajaj is the dust raised by the hoofs of horses. And it is said: the sun fasts when it stands in the middle of the sky and refrains from moving at the time of zenith. Its legal meaning: (It is abstention during the day) daytime is a period from dawn (true dawn) to sunset, and this is the view of the scholars of fiqh and language. Hence the author of Diwan al-Adab said: Daytime is the opposite of night, and night ends with the rising of the true dawn, as in al-Hashiya. And abstention is specifically from entering something. The word 'thing' is general and includes food and other things. The restriction to entering excludes entering that is not a deliberate act, like dust entering with the breath. And the restriction to (intentionally or mistakenly) excludes entry due to forgetfulness. An example of one who errs is one in whom water precedes during rinsing the mouth or snuffing up water, and thereby the fast is broken, the same as intentionally. Whether he enters it into the belly through the mouth or nose, or through a wound in the interior, which is called ja'ifah, or enters it into what has the ruling of the belly, which is the brain, like medicine for a head wound (ammah). And abstention during the day from sexual desire includes intercourse.
Source pages p. 616

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on fasting (Kitab al-Sawm) with the technical definition of the term. Linguistically, 'sawm' means absolute abstention from any action or speech, as in the Quranic verse about Mary. Technically, it is defined as abstention from entering anything into the belly or what is analogous (like the brain) and from sexual desire, from true dawn to sunset. The commentary elaborates on each element: 'daytime' is from dawn to sunset; 'entering' excludes involuntary entry like dust; 'intentionally or mistakenly' excludes forgetfulness (accidental entry does not break the fast); 'belly' includes the mouth, nose, and deep wounds (ja'ifah); 'what has the ruling of the belly' includes the brain (e.g., medicine for a head wound). It also discusses the linguistic origins of the word, including examples from poetry and usage. The passage sets the foundation for understanding the act of fasting and its pillars.

Key Terms
الإمساك (al-im'sāk): Abstention; the central element of fasting, meaning to refrain from actions that break the fast.
النهار (al-nahār): Daytime, defined from the rising of the true dawn (fajr sadiq) until sunset.
الجائفة (al-jā'ifah): A deep wound that reaches the intestines or body cavity, through which something enters.
الآمة (al-āmmah): A head wound that reaches the brain membrane.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition of sawm in this passage, which of the following breaks the fast?
A) Forgetting to eat during the day.
B) Deliberately eating food during the day.
C) Involuntarily inhaling dust.
D) Having a wet dream during the day.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of sawm is abstention from entering anything into the belly (or analogous) and from sexual desire, intentionally or mistakenly, during daytime – forgetfulness and involuntary entry do not invalidate the fast.

Definition of Sawm (Fasting) Linguistically and Legally
Defines sawm linguistically and legally with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of sawm and identify which actions invalidate the fast.

Core Idea
Fasting is refraining from food, drink, and sexual desire with intention; forgetfulness does not break it.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah begins the Book of Fasting by defining sawm linguistically and legally. The Hanafi definition is precise: refraining during the daytime from intentionally or mistakenly introducing anything into the belly or its equivalent, and from sexual desire. Understanding each component is essential to determine what invalidates the fast and what does not, such as forgetfulness.

Guided Path
1
Separate linguistic from legal definition
Identify that linguistically, sawm means absolute abstention from action or speech, as shown by the verse of Maryam (AS) and poetic examples. Legally, it is a specific religious act with defined limits.
The linguistic definition is broader; the legal definition narrows it.
2
Parse the legal definition into components
Break down the definition: 'refraining during the daytime from introducing anything intentionally or mistakenly into the belly or what has its ruling, and from sexual desire.' Identify each element: time (nahar), action (imsak 'an idkhal), manner (amdan aw khata'an), locus (batn or ma lahu hukm al-batin), and additional element (shahwat al-farj).
Note that 'thing' (shay') includes all substances, not just food.
3
Identify what is excluded
Recognize that forgetfulness (nisyan) is excluded from invalidating the fast, as the definition says 'amdan aw khata'an' (intentionally or mistakenly) but not 'nisyan'. Also, accidental intake without intent (e.g., dust) is excluded because it does not involve 'idkhal' (introduction).
This is a key Hanafi distinction.
4
Understand the scope of 'belly' and its equivalent
The term 'batn' includes the mouth, nose, and internal wounds (ja'ifah). 'What has the ruling of the belly' includes the brain, so medicine applied to a head wound that reaches the brain invalidates the fast.
This broad interpretation covers all routes into the body.
5
Apply the definition to common scenarios
Test scenarios: swallowing water during rinsing intentionally? Mistakenly? Forgetfully? Know which invalidates and why. Also, consider sexual desire: intercourse or deliberate arousal leading to emission invalidates.
This step prepares for real-world application.

Book of Fasting
It is refraining during the daytime from introducing anything intentionally or mistakenly into the belly, or what has the ruling of the belly, and from sexual desire.
Book of Fasting
He mentioned fasting after prayer as in the two Jami', because each of them is a bodily worship. In the second year of the Hijra, the fast of Ramadan was made obligatory, and so were the sacrifice, zakat al-fitr, and the mosque of Quba was built. Then, it is necessary to know fasting linguistically and legally, its cause, condition, ruling, pillar, wisdom of its legislation, and its description. Its linguistic meaning: absolute refraining from action and speech at any time. Allah the Almighty said, relating from Maryam: 'Indeed, I have vowed to the Most Merciful abstention, so I will not speak today to any human' [Maryam:26], i.e., silence and quietness, and that was legislated. It is said: the horse fasted from its feeding trough if it stood and did not eat, and the feeding trough is the manger, its plural is araawi. An-Nabigha said: [from ar-rajaz meter] 'Horses fasting and horses not fasting beneath the dust, and others chewing the bit' i.e., refraining from running or fodder, and not refraining, and al-'ajaj is the dust raised by the horses' hooves. It is said: the sun fasts if it stands in the middle of the sky and refrains from motion at the time of noon. Its legal meaning: (It is refraining during the daytime) The daytime: an extended period from the rising of the true dawn to the setting of the sun, and this is the saying of the people of jurisprudence and language. For this reason, the author of Diwan al-Adab said: The daytime is the opposite of the night, and the night ends with the rising of the true dawn. So it is in al-Hashiyah. Refraining is specified to be (from introducing something), he generalized 'thing' so it includes what is usually eaten and other things, and he specified it by 'introducing' so it excludes entering without a deliberate act aimed at introduction, like dust entering with the breath. He specified the introduction by being (intentionally or mistakenly) so it excludes that which is from forgetfulness. An example of the mistaken: he is preceded by water during rinsing the mouth or sniffing water into the nose, and by it the fast is invalidated, just like the intentional, whether he introduces it into the belly from the mouth or nose, or a wound in the interior, which is called al-ja'ifah, or he introduces it into (what has the ruling of the belly) which is the brain, like a medicine for the head wound. (And) refraining during the daytime (from sexual desire) includes intercourse.

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah begins the Book of Fasting. It first defines fasting (sawm) linguistically and then legally. The author notes that fasting follows prayer in the book because both are bodily forms of worship. He then outlines the topics to be covered: linguistic and legal meaning, cause, condition, ruling, pillar, wisdom, and description.

Linguistically, sawm means absolute abstention from action or speech, as shown by the Qur'anic verse about Maryam's vow of silence and by poetic usage for a horse refraining from eating or running.

Legally, the definition is: 'refraining during the daytime from introducing anything intentionally or mistakenly into the belly, or what has the ruling of the belly, and from sexual desire.' Each part is carefully qualified:

- 'Daytime' (nahar) is from true dawn to sunset, as agreed by jurists and linguists.
- 'Introducing something' covers anything entering the body intentionally, not accidental dust.
- 'Intentionally or mistakenly' excludes forgetful eating or drinking, which do not break the fast according to the Hanafi school.
- 'Into the belly' includes the mouth, nose, and internal wounds (ja'ifah). 'What has the ruling of the belly' includes the brain, such that medicine reaching it breaks the fast.
- 'Sexual desire' covers intercourse and also deliberate arousal with emission (as noted in the margin? The text here just says 'includes intercourse' but the fuller meaning becomes clear later.

The definition emphasizes that intention (niyyah) is required to distinguish worship from habit, though the text mentions that intention will be discussed elsewhere.

This passage sets the foundational understanding of what constitutes the act of fasting and what invalidates it.

Key Terms
صوم (sawm): Linguistically: absolute abstention from action or speech. Legally: refraining during the daytime from introducing anything into the belly or what has its ruling, and from sexual desire, with intention.
إمساك (imsak): Refraining; the act of holding back from something.
نهار (nahar): The daytime period from true dawn (fajr sadiq) to sunset.
بطن (batn): The belly or internal cavity of the body; includes the mouth, nose, and internal wounds.
جوف (jawf): The interior of the body; what has the ruling of the belly, such as the brain.
شهوة الفرج (shahwat al-farj): Sexual desire; refraining from it includes intercourse and deliberate arousal leading to emission.
نية (niyyah): Intention; required for the fast to be valid and to distinguish worship from habit.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of sawm is a precise legal construct that distinguishes valid fasting from invalid by specifying time, the nature of intake, and the exclusion of forgetfulness.

Cause of Obligation of Ramadan and Each Day's Individual Cause
Explains the cause of obligation for Ramadan and each day's cause with an intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two causes (sabab) of fasting in Ramadan according to the Hanafi school and explain the practical consequence of the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding intermittent insanity.

Core Idea
The cause of Ramadan obligation is a part suitable for fasting, but each day is also an independent cause – and the dispute over nights has real legal consequences.

This passage explores the causation (sababiyyah) of fasting in Ramadan. It distinguishes between the cause that obligates the entire month and the cause that obligates each individual day. Additionally, there is an important intra-Hanafi dispute: does the sighting of any part of the month (including nights) suffice, or must the part be suitable for fasting (i.e., daytime)? This seemingly technical point affects real cases like intermittent insanity. We will unpack these layers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two levels of causation
Recognise that the passage discusses two distinct causes: one for the entire month (sighting of a suitable part) and one for each individual day (each day's existence).
These are not contradictory; they operate at different levels.
2
State the majority Hanafi position on the month's cause
The majority of Hanafis (Dabusi, Bazdawi, Abu al-Yusr) hold that the cause is the sighting of a part that is suitable for fasting, i.e., a daytime that can be intended for the fast.
The condition of suitability excludes nights and post-noon time.
3
Contrast Sarakhsi's position
Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi argues that the cause is the absolute sighting of any part of the month, including nights, because the entire month is witnessed.
This expands the cause to all moments of the month.
4
Examine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute appears in the case of intermittent insanity: if a person becomes sane on the first night, then insane before dawn and remains insane, Sarakhsi requires make-up (because the cause was already triggered), while the majority does not require make-up (because no suitable daytime was experienced).
The night cannot be a cause because it cannot be fasted.
5
Understand the reconciliation of the two causes
The month's cause (sighting a suitable part) does not negate each day's own cause. This allows, for example, a new Muslim to only fast remaining days, not past ones.
The two causes are compatible and both are operational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( وكل يوم منه) أي: من رمضان سبب لأدائِهِ) أي: لوجوب أداء ذلك اليوم، لأنَّ صيام الأيام عبادة متفرقة، كتفرق الصلاة في الأوقات، بل أشد لتخلل زمان لا يصلح للصوم أصلاً وهو الليل، حتى لو بلغ صبي أو أسلم كافر في أثناء الشهر يلزمه ما بقي لا ما مضى، كما في « الكافي»، وتبعنا « الهداية » ، في الجمع بين السببين لأنه لا منافاة، فشهود جزء مخصوص من الشهر سبب لكله، ثم كل يوم سبب لصومه، غاية الأمر: أنه تكرر سبب وجوب صوم اليوم باعتبار خصوصه ودخوله في ضمن غيره، قاله « الكمال»، ونقلت السببية في المجموع إلى الجزء الأول فيه رعاية للمعيارية، كما قلنا بمثله في الصلاة رعاية للظرفية، ولئلا يلزم تقديم الشيء على سببه، ولهذا يجوز نية أداء الفرض في الليلة الأولى مع عدم جواز النية قبل سبب الوجوب، كما إذا نوى قبل غروب الشمس صوم الغد، وسببية الليل لا تقتضي جواز الأداء فيه، كمن أسلم في آخر الوقت، كما أفاده شيخنا العلامة شمس الملة « محمد المحبي» رحمه الله تعالى عن حاشيته «المنار»، والمراد بآخر الوقت آخر اليوم على طريقة شمس الأئمة السرخسي، وأشرنا إلى اختلاف الأسباب، ففي الصوم الفرض وقد علمته، وفي المنذور النذر، وفي صوم الكفارات الحنث في اليمين والجناية في القتل والإحرام والإفطار، والعزم على الوطء القضاء في الظهار، والشروع في النفل، وسبب الأداء، وسبب وجوب
The cause of the obligation of Ramadan is the sighting of a part of it that is suitable for fasting. And we have restricted that the part be suitable for fasting, and that is by intention, as a precaution against what is not a place for it from the nights and what is after noon, as stated by Imam Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, Fakhr al-Islam Ali al-Bazdawi, and Shaykh al-Islam Abu al-Yusr, contrary to what Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: that the cause is the absolute sighting of the month, so that days and nights are equal in causality. The fruit of the dispute appears in the case of one who becomes conscious on the first night of the month, then becomes insane before dawn completely, then becomes conscious or becomes conscious in a night, or after noon of a day of it, then returns to insanity before dawn—he owes the make-up according to Shams al-A'imma, not according to others. Its verification is in its place. (And each day of it) i.e., of Ramadan, is a cause for its performance, i.e., for the obligation of performing that day, because the fasting of days is a separate worship, like the separation of prayer in times, rather more severe due to the interposition of a time that is not suitable for fasting at all, which is the night. So that if a boy reaches puberty or a disbeliever accepts Islam during the month, he is obliged to [fast] what remains, not what has passed, as in al-Kafi. And we followed al-Hidayah in combining the two causes because there is no contradiction. So the sighting of a specific part of the month is the cause for the whole [month], then each day is a cause for its fast. The utmost is that the cause of obligation of the day's fast is repeated considering its specificity and its inclusion within another. Al-Kamal said that. And the causality was transferred in the aggregate to the first part, taking into consideration the standard [principle], as we said similarly in prayer regarding the consideration of time, and to avoid advancing something before its cause. For this reason, it is permissible to intend the performance of the obligatory [fast] on the first night, while it is not permissible to intend before the cause of obligation, as if one intends before sunset the fast of the next day. And the causality of the night does not necessitate the permissibility of performance in it, like one who accepts Islam at the end of the time, as our teacher, the great scholar Shams al-Milla Muhammad al-Muhibbi—may Allah have mercy on him—stated in his gloss al-Manar. And what is meant by 'the end of time' is the end of the day according to the method of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. And we have referred to the difference of causes. So in the obligatory fast [of Ramadan], you have known it; in the vowed fast, [the cause is] the vow; in expiatory fasts, [the cause is] the breaking of an oath, the offense in killing, ihram, and breaking the fast, and the resolve to have intercourse requiring expiation in dhihar, and starting a supererogatory [fast]. And the cause of performance and the cause of obligation...
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This passage discusses two key points about the causation (sababiyyah) of fasting in Ramadan.

First, the cause (sabab) for the obligation of fasting the entire month of Ramadan is the sighting (shuhud) of a part (juz') of the month that is suitable for fasting, i.e., a day. This is the position of Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, and Abu al-Yusr, who argue that only parts that can be fasted (daytime) can be the cause, since the cause must be capable of bearing the effect. In contrast, Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi holds that the cause is the absolute sighting of any part of the month, including nights, because the entire month is witnessed. The practical consequence (thamarah) appears in cases involving intermittent insanity: if a person becomes sane on the first night, then insane before dawn, then sane again, etc., on Sarakhsi's view they must make up the fast because the cause (the first night's sighting) already obligated the whole month, while on the other view the cause is only the part suitable for fasting, so if they are insane at the time of fasting (daytime), no obligation arises for that day.

Second, the passage explains that each individual day of Ramadan is also a separate cause for the obligation of fasting that specific day. This is because each day's fast is an independent act of worship, like individual prayers are separate units. An evidence for this is that if a boy reaches puberty or an unbeliever accepts Islam during the month, they are only required to fast the remaining days, not the past ones. The author reconciles these two causes (the month's cause and the day's cause) by stating that they are not contradictory: the sighting of a specified part (the first day) is the cause for the whole month, but each day then has its own cause derived from its own existence. This allows, for example, the permissibility of intending the fast on the first night (since the cause of the month exists at night) while the actual performance of the fast must be during the day.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): Cause; that which triggers the obligation of an act of worship.
شهود (shuhud): Sighting or witnessing; the presence of a part of the month.
جزء (juz'): A part; here, a part of the month that is suitable for fasting (a daytime).
ثمرة الخلاف (thamarah al-khilaf): The practical consequence of a scholarly dispute.
نية (niyyah): Intention; here, the condition for the part to be suitable for fasting, as the cause must be something that can be intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The sighting of a part of the month that is suitable for fasting

Key Takeaway
The cause of obligation for Ramadan fasting is the sighting of a day part that can be fasted, and each day is its own cause; this affects cases like intermittent insanity.

The Legal Definition of Fasting in the Hanafi School
Definition of fasting with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fasting and identify the conditions required for its validity.

Core Idea
Fasting is valid only with intention, by a qualified person, at the proper time.

This lesson unpacks the precise legal definition of fasting (ṣawm) according to the Hanafi school. The passage from Imdad al-Fattah gives a concise yet comprehensive definition that emphasizes the necessity of intention (niyya) and the qualifications of the fasting person. Understanding this definition is essential for distinguishing valid fasting from mere abstention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitional elements
Locate the phrase 'abstention from things that break the fast with intention for Allah by His permission in its time'. Break it into components: the act (imsak), the subject (intention), the object (Allah), and the temporal condition (its time).
The term 'its people' refers to those qualified to fast.
2
List the conditions from the definition
The commentary explicitly excludes menstruating women, postpartum women, disbelievers, and the insane. Also, the time must be from dawn to sunset. List these as conditions (shurūṭ) for validity.
Condition: the person must be Muslim, sane, adult, and free from menstruation/postpartum bleeding.
3
Distinguish rukn from sharṭ
In Hanafi fiqh, the rukn (pillar) is the abstention with intention; the shurūṭ are external prerequisites. Identify which elements are integral to the act itself vs. prerequisites for its validity.
Intention is considered a rukn in fasting, not merely a condition, because the act itself is defined by it.

Source Text

وكل يوم منه سبب لأدائه.. الشهر، بنية من أهلِهِ. وسبب وجوبِ رمضانَ شُهُودُ جُزْءٍ منه، وكـ [؟] والإنزال بالعبث ولابد أن يكون الإمساك (بنيّة) لتمتاز العبادة عن العادة، وقدمنــا الـكـلام عـلـى النية ( من أهله)، احترازاً عن الحائض والنفساء والكافر والمجنون، وهذا الحد الصحيح اختصاره: إمساك عن المفطرات يُنوى الله تعالى بإذنه في وقته، كما في «الفتح»، و«المستصفى». (وسبب وجوب) يعني: افتراض الصوم رمضانَ شُهودُ جُزْءٍ) صالح للصوم (منه) أي: من رمضان، وقيدنــا يكون الجزء صالحاً للصوم، وهو بالنية احترازاً عما لم يكن محلاً له من الليالي، ومـا بـعـد الـزوال، كما قال الإمام أبو زيد الدبوسي»، وفخر الإسلام ( علي البزدوي ، وشيخ الإسلام «أبو اليسر»، خلافاً لما قاله شمس الأئمة ( السرخسي»: إن السبب مطلق شهود الشهر، حتى استوى في السببية الأيام والليالي، وثمرة الخلاف تظهر فيمن أفاق أول ليلة من الشهر، ثم جنَّ قبل الفجر جميع ثم أفاق أو أفاق في ليلة، أو فيما بعد الزوال من يوم منه، ثم عاد وطراً الجنون قبل الفجر، يلزمه القضاء على قول شمس الأئمة، لا على قول غيره، وتحقيقه في محله. ( وكل يوم منه) أي: من رمضان سبب لأدائِهِ) أي: لوجوب أداء ذلك اليوم، لأنَّ صيام الأيام عبادة متفرقة، كتفـرق الصلاة في الأوقات، بل أشد لتخلل زمان لا يصلح للصوم أصلاً وهو الليل، حتى لو بلغ صبي أو أسلم كافر في أثناء الشهر يلزمه ما بقي لا ما مضى، كما في « الكافي»، وتبعنا « الهداية » ، في الجمع بين السببين لأنه لا منافاة، فشهود جزء مخصوص من الشهر سبب لكله، ثم كل يوم سبب لصومه، غاية الأمر: أنه تكرر سبب وجوب صوم اليوم باعتبار خصوصه ودخوله في ضمن غيره، قاله « الكمال»، ونقلــت السببية في المجموع إلى الجزء الأول فيه رعاية للمعيارية، كما قلنا بمثله في الصلاة رعاية للظرفية، ولئلا يلزم تقديم الشيء على سببه، ولهذا يجوز نية أداء الفرض في الليلة الأولى مع عدم جواز النية قبل سبب الوجوب، كما إذا نوى قبل غروب الشمس صوم الغد، وسببية الليل لا تقتضي جواز الأداء فيه، كمن أسلم في آخر الوقت، كما أفاده شيخنا العلامة شمس الملة « محمد المحبي» رحمـه الله تعالى عن حاشيته «المنار»، والمراد بآخر الوقـت آخـر اليـوم على طريقة شمس الأئمة السرخسي»، وأشرنا إلى اختلاف الأسباب، ففي الصوم الفرض وقد علمته، وفي المنـذور النذر، وفي صوم الكفارات الحنث في اليمين والجناية في القتل والإحرام والإفطار، والعزم على الوطء القضاء في الظهار، والشروع في النفل، وسبب ) الأداء، و سبب وجوب ، وإذا نذر صوم يوم الخميس أو رجب فصام الاثنين أو ربيعاً الأول صح عن نذره لوجوب سببه، ولغي تـعـيـيـن الـيـوم والشهر، لأن صحة النذر ولزومه بما به يكون المنذور عبادة والمحقق لذلك الصوم لا خصوص الزمان، ولا باعتباره كما في «الفتح»
And every day of it is a cause for its performance... the month, with intention from its people. And the cause of obligation of Ramadan is witnessing a part of it, and [؟] and ejaculation by play, and it is necessary that the abstention be (with intention) to distinguish worship from habit, and we have previously spoken about intention (from its people), as a precaution against the menstruating woman, the postpartum woman, the disbeliever, and the insane. This is the correct definition, its summary: abstention from things that break the fast with intention for Allah Almighty by His permission in its time, as in al-Fath and al-Mustasfa. (And the cause of obligation) meaning: the obligation of fasting Ramadan is witnessing a part) suitable for fasting (of it) i.e., of Ramadan, and we qualified that the part be suitable for fasting, which is with intention, as a precaution against that which is not a place for it, like nights and after noon, as stated by Imam Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, Fakhr al-Islam Ali al-Bazdawi, and Shaykh al-Islam Abu al-Yusr, contrary to what Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: that the cause is absolute witnessing of the month, so that days and nights are equal in causation. The fruit of the disagreement appears in the case of one who regains consciousness on the first night of the month, then becomes insane before dawn altogether, then regains consciousness, or regains consciousness on a night, or after noon on a day of it, then insanity occurs again before dawn – he must make up on the saying of Shams al-A'imma, not on the saying of others, and its verification is in its place. (And every day of it) i.e., of Ramadan, is a cause for its performance) i.e., for the obligation of performing that day, because the fasting of days is a worship that is separate, like the separation of prayer in times, rather more so due to the interposition of a time not suitable for fasting at all, which is the night, such that if a boy reaches puberty or a disbeliever embraces Islam during the month, he must fast what remains, not what has passed, as in al-Kafi, and we followed al-Hidayah in combining the two causes because there is no contradiction; so witnessing a specific part of the month is a cause for all of it, then each day is a cause for its fast, the only point being that the cause of obligation of the fast of the day is repeated by virtue of its specificity and its inclusion within another, as stated by al-Kamal. And causation is transferred in the aggregate to the first part as a consideration of standard, as we said similarly in prayer as a consideration of time, and to avoid preceding the thing to its cause. Hence it is permissible to intend the performance of the obligatory fast on the first night while it is not permissible to intend before the cause of obligation, such as if one intended before sunset to fast the next day. The causation of the night does not necessitate the permissibility of performance in it, like one who embraces Islam at the end of the time, as our teacher, the noble scholar Shams al-Milla Muhammad al-Muhibbi, may Allah have mercy on him, conveyed from his annotation al-Manar. The meaning of 'end of time' is the end of the day according to the method of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. And we have indicated the difference of causes: in obligatory fast it is as you have learned, in vowed fast it is the vow, in expiatory fasts it is the breaking of an oath, the crime in killing, ihram, breaking the fast, and determination to have intercourse requiring make-up in zihar, and commencement in supererogatory, and cause of performance, and cause of obligation. And if one vows to fast Thursday or Rajab and fasts Monday or Rabi' al-Awwal, it is valid for his vow because the cause of obligation exists, and the specification of the day and month is disregarded, because the validity and binding of the vow is by that which makes the vowed thing an act of worship, and the actualizer of that is fasting, not the specificity of time, nor by considering it, as in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah is an expansion on the definition of fasting and the cause of its obligation for Ramadan. The passage begins by giving a precise definition of fasting: it is abstention from things that break the fast, accompanied by intention for Allah, at the proper time (before dawn until sunset). This definition distinguishes fasting as an act of worship from mere habit. The commentator emphasizes that intention is essential and that the fasting person must be from 'its people'—meaning a Muslim, sane, adult, and free from menstruation and postpartum bleeding. The definition is attributed to al-Fath and al-Mustasfa. Next, the passage discusses the cause (sabab) of the obligation of Ramadan fasting. The cause is witnessing a part of the month that is suitable for fasting, i.e., a part of the day (from dawn to sunset). This is qualified because nights and times after noon are not valid for beginning the fast. The commentator reports a disagreement among Hanafi scholars: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi holds that the cause is simply witnessing the month, making days and nights equal in causation, while other authorities (Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, Abu al-Yusr) say the cause is only witnessing a part suitable for fasting. The practical consequence (thamarah) is shown in cases where a person becomes insane or regains consciousness at various times—according to al-Sarakhsi, qada is required in more cases. The passage then explains that each day of Ramadan is itself a cause for performing the fast of that day, as fasting days are separate acts of worship. This dual causation (the month as a whole and each day individually) is harmonized by stating that witnessing a specific part causes the obligation for the entire month, and then each day causes its own fast. The discussion also touches on intention: one may intend the entire month's fast on the first night, but it is not permissible to intend before the cause (e.g., before sunset for the next day). Finally, the passage mentions different causes for different types of fast: for obligatory fasts (Ramadan) the cause is as above; for vowed fasts it is the vow itself; for expiatory fasts it is the triggering event; and for supererogatory fasts it is commencement. A point about vows: if one vows a specific day but fasts another, it can be valid if the month or day is not specified as a condition.

Key Terms
سبب وجوب (sabab wujūb): The cause that makes the act of worship obligatory. For Ramadan fasting, it is witnessing a part of the month suitable for fasting.
شهود جزء (shuhūd juz'): Witnessing a part of Ramadan. This is the cause of obligation, but it must be a part suitable for fasting (i.e., a day, not a night).
حد الصوم (ḥadd al-ṣawm): The legal definition of fasting: abstention from things that break the fast with intention for Allah at the proper time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, which of the following is a required component of a valid fast?
A) Abstention from food and drink
B) Intention (niyya)
C) Being male
D) Fasting from noon to sunset

Key Takeaway
A fast is only valid if accompanied by intention, performed by a qualified person, and during the proper time from dawn to sunset.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
10 min
The Cause of Obligation for Ramadan Fasting: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Cause of obligation with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the cause of obligation for Ramadan fasting and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute with its practical consequences.

Core Idea
The cause of obligation is witnessing a part of Ramadan suitable for fasting—not any part.

This lesson examines the sabab (cause) that triggers the obligation of fasting the entire month of Ramadan. The passage presents a significant intra-Hanafi dispute: is the cause simply witnessing any part of the month, or must that part be suitable for fasting (i.e., a day)? The answer affects cases where a person becomes insane or regains consciousness at various times.

Guided Path
1
Identify the majority view on the cause
Find the names: Imam Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, Shaykh al-Islam Abu al-Yusr. They hold that the cause is witnessing a part of Ramadan suitable for fasting, i.e., a day (from dawn to sunset).
Suitability means the time can accommodate the fasting act with intention.
2
Identify the opposing view (al-Sarakhsi)
Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi says the cause is absolute witnessing of the month, making days and nights equal in causation.
Al-Sarakhsi is a major Hanafi scholar but in this issue he differs.
3
Understand the thamarah (practical consequence)
The passage gives an example: someone regains consciousness on the first night of Ramadan, then becomes insane before dawn. According to al-Sarakhsi, qaḍā (make-up) is required for the whole month; according to the majority, no qaḍā is required because the cause was not fulfilled (the part witnessed was night, not suitable for fasting).
This shows how the legal dispute directly impacts rulings in similar cases.
4
Recognize the dual causation
The passage also explains that each day of Ramadan is a cause for its own fast, in addition to the overall cause: witnessing a part causes obligation for the entire month, and then each day triggers its own performance.
This harmonizes the two statements: the cause for obligation is the month, and the cause for performance is each day.

Source Text

Conditions and Pillars of Fasting in Ramadan
Memorization anchor for rules of fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions for obligation, the three conditions for validity, and the single rukn of fasting according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Fasting Ramadan is fard—its obligation requires four conditions; its validity requires three; its rukn is refraining from eating, drinking, and marital relations.

In this lesson, we dissect the concise ruling from Imdad al-Fattah on the pillars and conditions of fasting. Understanding these categories is essential: they tell you when fasting is obligatory, when it is valid, and what its core integral act is. This knowledge directly impacts your daily practice during Ramadan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall ruling
The passage begins by stating that fasting Ramadan is farḍ (obligatory) both as performance (ada') and make-up (qada'). Recognize that this is a definitive obligation.
Hanafis use farḍ for obligations proven by qat'i evidence.
2
Distinguish shurūṭ al-wujūb from shurūṭ al-ṣiḥḥa
The text lists four conditions for the obligation to fast (Islam, sanity, puberty, and knowledge/being in Dar al-Islam) and later three conditions for validity (intention, freedom from menstruation/postpartum bleeding, and freedom from invalidators). Note the difference: one can be liable but not validly fasting if a condition of validity is missing.
This distinction is key in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Examine the shurūṭ al-wujūb
List the four conditions for obligation and note the special cases: 'ilm bi al-wujūb for converts in dar al-harb, and simply being in dar al-Islam otherwise.
These are necessary for the duty to be incumbent.
4
Examine the shurūṭ al-ṣiḥḥa
List the three conditions for validity: intention, freedom from what contradicts the fast (menstruation/postpartum bleeding), and freedom from what invalidates it (e.g., deliberate eating). Note that janāba is NOT an invalidator.
This overrides a common misunderstanding.
5
Identify the rukn and its hukm
The rukn (pillar) is abstention from the desires of the stomach and private parts from dawn to sunset. The hukm of performing the fast is the discharge of the obligation (suqūṭ al-wājib).
If the rukn is omitted, the fast is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو فرض، أداءً وقضاء، على من اجتمع فيه أربعة أشياء: الإسلام، والعقل، والبلوغ، والعلم بالوجوب لمن أسلم بدار الحرب، أو الكون بدار الإسلام. ويشترط لوجوب أدائه: الصحـة مـن مـرض، وحيض، ونفاس. والإقامة ويُشترط لصحة أدائِهِ، ثلاثة: النية، والخُلُو عما يُنَافِيهِ، مِنْ حَيْضِ، وَبِفَاسِ، وَعَمَّا يُفْسِدُهُ ولا يُشترط الخُلُو عن الجنابة. وركتُهُ: الكف عن قضاء شهوتي البطن، والفرج، وما ألحق بهما، وحكمه: سقوط الواجب.
And it is an obligation, both performance and make-up, upon whom four things are combined: Islam, sanity, puberty, and knowledge of the obligation for one who embraces Islam in dar al-harb, or being in dar al-Islam. And for the obligation of its performance, health from illness, menstruation, and postpartum bleeding is required. And residence. And for the validity of its performance, three things are required: intention, freedom from what contradicts it, i.e., menstruation and postpartum bleeding, and from what invalidates it. And freedom from major ritual impurity is not required. And its pillar is: refraining from fulfilling the desires of the stomach and the private parts, and what is annexed to them. And its ruling is: discharge of the obligation.
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage from the sharh presents the core ruling and conditions of fasting in Ramadan in a concise, axiomatic style suitable for memorization. The passage begins by stating that fasting Ramadan is a fard (obligatory) duty, both as ada' (performance in time) and qada' (make-up). This obligation applies to a person who possesses four characteristics: Islam (being Muslim), 'aql (sanity), bulugh (puberty), and 'ilm bi al-wujub (knowledge of the obligation) for one who becomes Muslim in dar al-harb (non-Muslim territory). For one who is in dar al-Islam, simply being there as a Muslim entails obligation even without explicit knowledge. The passage then distinguishes between conditions for obligation (shurut wujub) and conditions for validity (shurut sihha). For obligation of performance, three conditions are mentioned: health (free from illness), freedom from menstruation and postpartum bleeding, and iqama (residence, i.e., not traveling). These excuse a person from fasting during Ramadan but require make-up later. For the validity of the fast itself, three conditions are listed: intention (niyya), freedom from what contradicts the fast (meaning menstruation and postpartum bleeding, which invalidate the fast if present during any part of the day), and freedom from what would invalidate it (such as intentional eating, drinking, or sexual intercourse). Importantly, the text explicitly states that being in a state of major ritual impurity (janaba) does not invalidate the fast; one can be junub and still have a valid fast, though one should not delay ghusl unnecessarily. The passage then gives the rukn (pillar) of fasting: refraining from fulfilling the desires of the stomach and the private parts (i.e., eating, drinking, and sexual activity) and anything analogous. The hukm (legal effect) of performing the fast is the discharge of the obligation (suqut al-wajib), meaning the duty is fulfilled and no longer due.

Key Terms
فرض (farḍ): An individually obligatory act established by definitive evidence (Quran or mutawatir Sunnah). Fasting Ramadan is fard.
ركن (rukn): The integral of an act without which it is not valid. For fasting, the rukn is refraining from the desires of the stomach and private parts.
شرط صحة (sharṭ ṣiḥḥa): A condition for the validity of the act. If missing, the act is invalid. Examples: intention, freedom from menstruation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT required for the validity (ṣiḥḥa) of fasting in Ramadan according to the Hanafi school?
A) Intention (niyya)
B) Freedom from menstruation and postpartum bleeding
C) Freedom from major ritual impurity (janāba)
D) Freedom from invalidating actions

Key Takeaway
Fasting Ramadan has its own distinct set of conditions and pillars; memorize the four for obligation, three for validity, and know that janāba does not invalidate.
