Prerequisites and Conditions for Obligatory Ramadan Fasting
Ruling on prerequisites and validity conditions for Ramadan fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions for the obligation of Ramadan fasting and explain the difference between conditions for obligation and conditions for validity.

Core Idea
Fasting Ramadan is fard 'ayn; its obligation depends on four conditions, but its validity requires separate conditions.

This passage from Nur al-Idah explains that Ramadan fasting is an individual obligation (fard 'ayn). However, it is only obligatory upon a person who meets specific prerequisites: Islam, sanity, puberty, and either knowledge of the obligation (for converts in dar al-harb) or residence in dar al-Islam. The passage also separates the conditions for valid performance, such as intention and freedom from menstruation. Understanding these layers is essential for correctly applying the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is fasting the month of Ramadan. The ruling is fard 'ayn (individual obligation) based on definitive evidence.
Fard 'ayn means every individual must perform it; no one else can fulfill it on their behalf.
2
Extract the four conditions for obligation (shurut al-wujub)
The text lists: Islam, sanity, puberty, and either knowledge of the obligation (for a convert in dar al-harb) or being a resident in dar al-Islam. These must be present for the fasting to be obligatory upon a person.
Knowledge of the obligation is a condition only for converts in dar al-harb; for Muslims in dar al-Islam, the obligation applies regardless of knowledge.
3
Locate the dalil for obligation
The author cites Quran 2:183: 'O you who have believed, decreed upon you is fasting.' The imperative 'kutiba' indicates obligation, and addressing the believers shows it is fard 'ayn.
The dalil is Quranic with qati' thubut and dalala.
4
Distinguish conditions for validity from conditions for obligation
Conditions for validity (shurut al-sihha) include intention, freedom from menstruation/postpartum bleeding, and avoidance of invalidators. Unlike conditions for obligation, these affect whether the act is valid once performed. For example, janabah does not invalidate fasting.
A person may be obligated to fast but if a condition for validity is missing (e.g., menstruation), the fast is invalid and must be made up.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the knowledge condition
Imam Abu Hanifa requires that the convert's knowledge be established through specific witnesses (two men, or one man and two trustworthy women, or one just person). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not require justice, puberty, or freedom in the witnesses.
The practical difference: according to Abu Hanifa, if the witnesses are insufficient, the obligation is not established, so no qada is required; according to the Sahibayn, the obligation may be established with less.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وهو) أي: صوم رمضان (فرض) عين ( أداءً وقضاء، على من اجتمع فيه أربعة أشياء) هي شروط لافتراضه و الخطاب ،به وتُسمى شروط وجوب، أحدها (الإسلام)، لأنه شرط للخطاب بفروع الشريعة، لقوله تعالى: ﴿ يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۸۳] (و) ثانيها: (العقلُ)، إذ لا خطاب بدونه. (وَ) ثالثها: (البلوغ) إذ لا تكليف إلا به. (و) رابعها: (الـعـلـم بالوجوب)، وهو شرط لمن أسلم بدار الحرب)، وإنما يحصل له العلم الموجب بإخبــار رجلين، أو رجل وامرأتين مستورين، أو واحد عدل، وعندهما: لا يشترط العدالة ولا البلوغ والحرية، وقوله: (أو الكونُ) عطف على العلم، أي: حلول المسلم ( بدار الإسلام) مكلفاً، فيلزمه الصيام أداء وقضاء علم بالوجوب أو لم يعلم، بخلاف من أسلم بدار الحرب، ولم يعلم، ثم علم بافتراض الصوم، ليس عليه قضاء ما مضى إذ لا تكليف بدون العلم ثمة للعذر، والجهل بدار الإسلام ليس عذراً. ويشترط لوجوب أدائِهِ ) الذي هو عبارة عن تفريغ الذمة في وقته (الصحة: من مرض) لقوله تعالى: أَيَّامًا مَعْدُودَاتٍ فَمَن كَانَ مِنكُم مَّرِيضًا أَوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَ [البقرة: ١٨٤] (و) الصحة أي: الخلـو عـن (حيض، ونفاس) لما قدمناه، والإقامة) لما تلوناه. ويُشترط لصحة أدائه) أي: فعله؛ ليكون أعـــم من الأداء والقضاء، (ثلاثة) شرائط (النيّةُ ) في وقتها لكل يوم كما سنذكره) (والخُلُو عَمَّا يُنَافِيهِ) أي: ينافي صحة فعله (منْ حَيْضِ ونفاس)، إذ لا يصح الصوم مع وجود أحدهما، (وَ) الخلو (عمّا يُفْسِدُهُ) بطروه عليه، وسنذكره ) إن شاء الله تعالى (ولا يُشترط لصحته (الخُلُو عن الجنابة)، فإنَّ الصائم إذا استمر جنباً أياماً صح صومه، وإن أثم بترك الصلاة لقوله تعالى : فَالْآنَ بَاشِرُوهُنَّ [البقرة: ١٨٧] ولم يجعل العقل والإقامة من شروط الصحة، فإنه لو نوى الصوم ليلاً ثم جن أو أغمي عليه صح صومه في ذلك اليوم، كما سنذكره (۳)
(It, i.e., fasting of Ramadan, is a fard 'ayn, performance and make-up, upon one in whom four things are combined) which are conditions for its being obligatory and addressing him, and they are called conditions of obligation. First: Islam, because it is a condition for addressing with branches of Sharia, due to His statement: 'O you who have believed, decreed upon you is fasting' [2:183]. Second: sanity, for there is no address without it. Third: puberty, for there is no responsibility except with it. Fourth: knowledge of the obligation, which is a condition for one who converts to Islam in dar al-harb. And he obtains the knowledge that obligates only by the report of two men, or one man and two trustworthy women, or one just person. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), justice, puberty, and freedom are not conditions. And his statement: 'or being in dar al-Islam' is coordinated with knowledge; i.e., the Muslim's residence in dar al-Islam as a responsible person, so fasting performance and make-up are incumbent upon him whether he knows the obligation or not, unlike one who converts in dar al-harb and does not know, then knows of the obligation of fasting: he is not required to make up what passed, because there is no responsibility without knowledge there due to the excuse, but ignorance in dar al-Islam is not an excuse. And it is required for the obligation of its performance (which is discharging the liability in its time): health from sickness (due to His statement: ' [Fasting for] a limited number of days. So whoever among you is ill or on a journey, then an equal number of other days' [2:184]), and health, i.e., freedom from menstruation and postpartum bleeding (as we have mentioned), and residence (as we have recited). And for the validity of its performance (i.e., the act, to be general for performance and make-up), three conditions are required: intention at its time each day (as we will mention), freedom from what contradicts it, i.e., contradicts the validity of the act (i.e., menstruation and postpartum bleeding), for fasting is not valid with their existence, and freedom from what invalidates it by its supervention (we will mention it, if Allah wills). And freedom from janabah is not a condition for its validity; for one who remains in a state of janabah for days, his fasting is valid, though he is sinful for leaving prayer, due to His statement: 'But now, be intimate with them' [2:187]. And sanity and residence are not considered conditions for validity; for if one intends fasting at night and then becomes insane or unconscious, his fasting that day is valid, as we will mention.
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the prerequisites for the obligation and validity of Ramadan fasting. The author first states the four conditions for fasting to be obligatory: Islam, sanity, puberty, and knowledge of the obligation for a person who accepts Islam in dar al-harb (abode of war). For a Muslim in dar al-Islam, knowledge of the obligation is not required for the duty to take effect; it applies regardless. The proof for the obligation is the Quranic verse 'O you who have believed, decreed upon you is fasting' (2:183). The author also mentions an intra-Hanafi difference: According to Imam Abu Hanifa, knowledge of the obligation for a convert in dar al-harb can only be established through the testimony of two men, or one man and two trustworthy women, or one just person. However, according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), justice, puberty, and freedom are not required for the witnesses. For validity of the fast, three conditions are needed: intention, freedom from menstruation and postpartum bleeding, and freedom from anything that invalidates it. Janabah (major ritual impurity) does not affect the validity of fasting, supported by the verse permitting marital relations at night (2:187). Also, if a person intends fasting at night and then becomes insane or unconscious, the fast remains valid. The passage ensures that students understand the precise conditions and the differences within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): An obligatory act that is established by definitive evidence, such that neglecting it incurs sin and denying it is disbelief (kufr).
علم بالوجوب ('ilm bil-wujub): Knowledge that the act (fasting) is obligatory; a condition for a convert in dar al-harb, but not for a Muslim in dar al-Islam.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory not under Islamic rule; converts there are excused for ignorance until informed.
صحة (sihha): Validity of the act; the act fulfills its conditions and pillars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ramadan fasting is fard 'ayn, but its obligation requires four conditions and its validity requires intention, freedom from menstruation, and avoidance of invalidators; janabah and unconsciousness do not invalidate.

The Pillar, Ruling, and Wisdom of Fasting
Defines rukn, hukm, hikmah, and riya' exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the pillar (rukn) of fasting, explain its ruling (suqut al-wajib and reward), list the wisdoms behind its legislation, and justify why riya' does not affect obligatory fasting.

Core Idea
Fasting's pillar is abstention; it discharges obligation and secures reward—and riya' does not taint that reward.

This lesson unpacks the foundational definition of fasting from Nur al-Idah. We will first identify its essential pillar (rukn) and the legal outcome (hukm). Then we explore the profound wisdoms that make fasting unique, and finally we examine a special benefit: why showing off (riya') cannot harm the reward of an obligatory fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rukn of fasting
Read the opening sentence: the rukn is 'al-kaff' (restraint). Determine what exactly must be restrained and note that analogies extend the scope.
The Hanafi position: intention is a shart, not a rukn; the rukn is the abstention itself.
2
Understand the hukm (ruling)
The text says the ruling is 'suqut al-wajib' (discharge of obligation) and 'al-thawab' (reward) in the Hereafter, provided the fast is not on a prohibited day. Grasp that this applies to both obligatory and supererogatory acts when initiated.
The phrase 'إن لم يكن منهياً' reminds us that reward is conditional on the act being permissible.
3
Explore the hikmah (wisdom)
The passage lists multiple benefits: subduing the commanding soul, purifying the heart, cultivating empathy for the poor, imitating the angels. Note how each benefit flows from the act of abstaining.
Hikmah is the benefit, not the legal cause ('illah).
4
Analyze the riya' exception
The fā'idah states that riya' does not enter obligatory fasting. Identify the proof: the hadith qudsī 'Fasting is for Me and I will reward it.' Explain why this makes fasting uniquely resistant to riya'.
This exception applies only to obligatory fasting; in other acts, riya' may nullify reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وركنُهُ) أي: الصيام، (الكف) أي: الإمساك عن قضاء شهوتي البطن، والفرج و ) عن ما أُلحق بهما مما سنذكره ) قريباً بفضل الله ( وحكمه: سُقوط الواجب) أي: اللازم فرضاً كان أو غيره ( عن الدّمة) بالإيجاب، أو الشروع في النفل (والثواب) تكرماً من الله (في الآخرة) إن لم يكن منهياً. وحكمة مشروعية الصوم: أن الله سبحانه وتعالى شرعه لفوائد أعظمها كونه موجباً لشيئين ينشأ أحدهما عن الآخر: سكون النفس الأمارة، وكسر ثورتها في الفضول المتعلقة بجميع الجوارح من العين واللسان والأذن والفرج، فإن الصوم به تضعف حركتها في محسوساتها، ولذا قيل: إذا جاعت النفس شبعت جميع الأعضاء، وإذا شبعت جاعت كلها، وبهذا صفاء القلب فإنَّ الموجب لكدوراته فضول الجوارح، فإذا حبست عنها وبه تبلغ الدرجات العلى، ومنها كون الصيام موجباً للرحمة والعطف على المساكين، فإنَّ الصائم لما ذاق ألم الجوع في بعض الأوقات تذكر مَنْ هذا حاله في عموم الأوقات، فتسارع إليـه بالرقة والرحمة، وحقيقتها في حق الإنسان نوع ألم باطن، فينال بذلـك مـا عنـد الله مـن حـسـن الجزاء، ومنها : موافقته الفقراء بتحمل ما يتحملون أحياناً، وفي ذلك رفـع حــال عند الله، وبهذا لا ينبغي الإفراط في السحور، فإنه يذهب معظم المقصود بالصوم لخلوه عن حكمته، ومنها: الاتصاف بصفة الملائكة الروحانية. فائدة: الرياء لا يدخل في صوم الفريضة، وفي سائر الطاعات يدخل لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: « يقول الله تعالى، الصوم لي وأنا أجزي به ) (۱) نفى شركة الغير، وهذا لم يذكر في سائر الطاعات كذا فـي التجنيس».
And its pillar (i.e., of fasting): restraint, i.e., abstaining from fulfilling the two appetites: the stomach and the private parts, and from what is joined to them as we will mention soon by Allah's grace. And its ruling: the discharge of the obligation, i.e., the required, whether fard or other, from the responsibility by making it due, or by beginning a supererogatory act, and reward as a favor from Allah in the Hereafter, if it is not prohibited. And the wisdom behind the legislation of fasting: that Allah, glorified and exalted, legislated it for benefits, the greatest of which is that it causes two things, one arising from the other: the calming of the commanding soul, and breaking its vigor in excess related to all limbs—the eye, tongue, ear, and private part—for fasting weakens their movement in their sensibilities. Therefore it is said: when the soul is hungry, all limbs become satiated; when it is satiated, all of them become hungry. Through this is the purity of the heart, for the cause of its turbidity is the excesses of the limbs; when they are restrained, one attains high ranks. And among them: fasting causes mercy and compassion towards the poor; for the fasting person, when he tastes the pain of hunger at some times, remembers those who are in this state all the time, so he hastens to them with softness and mercy. Its reality for humans is a kind of inner pain, so he attains through that what Allah has of good reward. And among them: his conformity with the poor in bearing what they bear sometimes, and in that is elevation of his status with Allah. Hence, one should not overdo the suhoor, for it removes most of the purpose of fasting, due to its being devoid of this wisdom. And among them: becoming characterized with the attribute of the spiritual angels. A benefit: Riya' (showing off) does not enter into obligatory fasting, whereas in other acts of worship it does enter, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Allah says: Fasting is for Me, and I will reward it' (Bukhārī, Muslim). He negated the partnership of others, and this is not mentioned in other acts of worship, as stated in al-Tajnīs.
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage defines the pillar (rukun) of fasting as the act of abstaining from fulfilling the appetites of the stomach and private parts, along with what is analogous. The hukm of fasting is that it discharges the obligation from one's responsibility and brings reward from Allah, provided the fast is not on a prohibited day such as Eid. The passage then delves into the wisdom (hikmah) behind fasting: it subdues the commanding soul (nafs ammārah), curbs the excesses of the limbs, purifies the heart, and softens one's heart towards the needy. It also mentions that overeating at suhoor diminishes these benefits. A notable benefit (fā’idah) is that riyā’ (showing off) does not enter into obligatory fasting, meaning that even if one engages in riyā’, the fast remains valid and rewarded, unlike other acts of worship where riyā’ can nullify the reward. This is supported by the hadith qudsī: 'Allah says: Fasting is for Me, and I will reward it,' implying the exclusivity of the reward from partners.

Key Terms
ركن (rukn): The integral part of fasting; without it, the act is invalid. Here it is the abstention itself.
سقوط الواجب (suqut al-wajib): The discharge of the obligation from one's responsibility, meaning the duty is fulfilled.
حكمة (hikmah): The divine wisdom and benefits behind the legislation of fasting.
الرياء (riya'): Showing off in worship; the text states it does not enter obligatory fasting, meaning it does not affect its reward.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the special status of obligatory fasting regarding showing off (riya')?
A) Riya' completely invalidates the fast.
B) Riya' does not enter into obligatory fasting, based on the hadith 'Fasting is for Me and I reward it.'
C) Riya' only affects supererogatory fasts, not obligatory ones.
D) Riya' reduces the reward but the fast is still valid.

Key Takeaway
Fasting's pillar is restraint; its ruling discharges obligation and brings reward, and riya' does not affect the reward of obligatory fasting due to the exclusive hadith qudsi.

Riya and Obligatory Fasting: The Hanafi Position
Explains wisdom of fasting and protection from riya in obligatory fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on riya in obligatory fasting and explain the proof from the hadith qudsi.

Core Idea
Riya does not invalidate obligatory fasting because the hadith qudsi states 'Fasting is for Me and I reward it'—excluding any share for others.

This lesson examines a unique feature of obligatory fasting: unlike other acts of worship, showing off (riya) does not nullify it. The Hanafi school grounds this position in a hadith qudsi that attributes fasting exclusively to Allah. We will explore the textual evidence and the legal reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific act being ruled upon? Obligatory fasting (fard). What is the ruling regarding riya? Riya does not affect its validity.
This is a ruling of non-invalidity, not a typical hukm level like fard/wajib.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith qudsi: 'Fasting is for Me and I will reward it.' Find it in the source text.
Narrated by Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'is for Me' (لي) indicates exclusive ownership and dedication. Therefore, any intention of showing off (for others) does not corrupt the fast because it remains for Allah alone.
This is a linguistic argument from the category of 'khass' (specific) indicating exclusivity.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars hold that riya invalidates all acts of worship, including obligatory fasting. Their evidence may be general verses condemning riya. How does the Hanafi school respond?
The Hanafi response is that fasting has an exclusive divine attribution not found in other acts, per the hadith.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi and based on a sound (sahih) hadith. It is not overridden by general principles because the hadith is specific (khass) to fasting.
This illustrates the Hanafi usul principle that a specific text (khass) takes precedence over a general one (amm).

(1) أخرجه البخاري في التوحيد باب قوله الله تعال يدُونَ أنْ يُبَدِّلُوا كَلامَ اللهِ )، (٧٤٩٢)، ومسلم في الصيام، باب فضل الصيام (١١٥١).
… concerning the debt, and the reward is in the Hereafter. It is not prohibited. Or other than it (concerning the debt) by obligation or commencing a supererogatory (act). And the reward is a favor from Allah in the Hereafter if not (neglected) from it. If it is prohibited like fasting on the day of Nahr, Fitr, and the days of Tashriq, its ruling is validity and release from liability, and sin for turning away from the hospitality of Allah. And the wisdom of legislating fasting: Allah the Exalted prescribed it for benefits, the greatest of which is that it causes two things that arise from each other: the calming of the commanding soul and breaking its rebelliousness in excesses related to all limbs – the eye, tongue, ear, and private part. Fasting weakens their movement in their sensations. Hence it is said: When the soul is hungry, all limbs are satisfied, and when it is full, all are hungry. Through this comes purity of the heart, for the cause of its turbidity is the excesses of the limbs. When restrained, high ranks are attained. Among them is that fasting causes mercy and compassion for the poor. When the fasting person tastes the pain of hunger at some times, he remembers those whose condition is like this all the time, so he hastens to them with gentleness and mercy. Its reality in humans is a kind of inner pain, by which he attains what is with Allah of good reward. Among them is that he agrees with the poor in bearing what they bear at times, and in that is elevation of his rank with Allah. Hence overeating at suhur is not proper, for it goes against most of the purpose of fasting, being devoid of its wisdom. Among them is being characterized with the spiritual attributes of angels. A benefit: Riya does not enter into obligatory fasting, but it enters into all other acts of worship, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: “Allah the Exalted says: ‘Fasting is for Me and I will reward it.’” This negates partnership of others, and this was not mentioned in other acts of worship, as recorded in al-Tajnis. (1) Narrated by al-Bukhari in al-Tawhid, section on His saying, “They want to change the words of Allah” (7492), and Muslim in Fasting, section on the virtue of fasting (1151).
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of Nur al-Idah first discusses rulings on prohibited fasts (like Eid days) – the fast is valid but sinful due to disregarding Allah's hospitality. Then it elaborates on the wisdom and benefits of fasting: calming the soul, restraining limbs, cultivating mercy, and attaining angelic qualities. The key legal benefit comes at the end: riya (showing off) does not affect the validity of obligatory fasting. The proof is a hadith qudsi: 'Fasting is for Me and I will reward it.' This exclusive attribution indicates that no partner (riya) can corrupt an obligatory fast, unlike other acts of worship where riya can nullify the reward. The Hanafi school holds that riya invalidates supererogatory acts but not obligatory ones, based on this hadith. The footnote cites Bukhari and Muslim.

Key Terms
الرياء (al-riya'): Showing off in worship; doing acts to be seen by others.
صوم الفريضة (sawm al-faridah): Obligatory fasting, such as Ramadan, kaffarat, and vowed fasts.
الحديث القدسي (hadith qudsi): A hadith in which the Prophet quotes Allah directly, but not part of the Quran.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fasting is the only act that Allah rewards directly without intermediary.
B) Fasting cannot be invalidated by riya because Allah says 'Fasting is for Me and I reward it.'
C) Fasting is more difficult than other acts.
D) Fasting has more conditions than other acts.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, riya does not nullify obligatory fasting due to the exclusive divine attribution in the hadith qudsi, distinguishing it from other acts of worship.

The Six Categories of Fasting and the Definition of Fard
Opens the chapter on fasting classification, defining fard and its evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six categories of fasting, identify which fasts are fard, and explain the Hanafi internal dispute on vowed fasting (nadhr).

Core Idea
Fard fasting rests on definitive evidence; vowed fasting is fard in the relied-upon view.

This lesson opens the chapter on fasting classification. The matn divides fasting into six types. Our focus is on the first: fard. The sharh expands on what constitutes fard and examines a key dispute: whether a vowed fast (nadhr) is fard or merely wajib. Understanding this distinction is crucial for appreciating the Hanafi reliance on evidence strength.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six categories
Read the matn's list: fard, wajib, masnun, mandub, nafl, makruh. Note that these are broad categories.
The sharh later details each; we focus on fard and wajib.
2
Define fard according to the passage
Fard is what is established by qat'i (definitive) evidence. The passage lists Ramadan (performance and make-up), expiations (for zihar, killing, oath, etc.), and vowed fasts.
Consensus (ijma) is also cited as definitive.
3
Analyze the evidence for vowed fasting
The text cites Quran 22:29 and the hadith to Prophet's command to fulfill vows. These are treated as qat'i in the relied-upon view.
4
Examine the alternative view
Some scholars argue the evidence is not qat'i (the verse is subject to interpretation, the hadith is ahad), so nadhr is only wajib. The text notes this and postpones full discussion.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Understand the practical consequence
Classifying nadhr as fard vs wajib affects the status of denial: denying fard is kufr, denying wajib is major sin but not kufr.
Also affects rulings on breaking the fast.

Source Text

Chapter on describing the nature of fasting and its classification. Fasting is divided into six categories: obligatory (fard), incumbent (wajib), customary (masnun), recommended (mandub), supererogatory (nafl), and disliked (makruh). As for the obligatory (fard), it is the fast of Ramadan, both performance and make-up, and the fast of expiations and the vowed (fast) in the apparent view. As for the incumbent (wajib), it is the make-up of what one has invalidated from a supererogatory (fast). Chapter on describing the nature of fasting and its classification. (1) Fasting is divided… Know that objects of different realities become one by consideration of a general attribute, like substance, blackness, and whiteness become one by consideration of existence, but multiply by consideration of specific attribute. Similarly, color variates into white, black, and green. Fasting is one in terms of devotion and abstention, but it varies by whether this fast is for him or against him. What is against him (obligatory) is by obligation of Allah or by obligation of the servant, as in al-Mustasfa. Hence we said: fasting is divided into six categories, mentioned summarily then in detail, for it is more effective in the soul: fard, wajib, masnun, mandub, nafl, and makruh. As for the first category, which is fard, it is the fast of the month of Ramadan, performance and make-up, and the fast of expiations (for zihar, killing, oath, hunting compensation, and the ransom for harm in ihram), because these are established by definitive evidence in both chain and text, and by consensus. Also included in this category is the vowed fast (al-mandhur), so it is fard in the apparent view, due to His saying: 'Let them fulfill their vows' [22:29] and the Prophet's saying to Umar: 'Fulfill your vow.' (3) It was said that it is wajib, because it is specified from among things that are not wajib in their kind, like visiting the sick, so it does not remain definitive and becomes like a solitary report, and due to the verse being subject to interpretation, by which only obligation (wujub) is established, not fard. There is a consideration we will mention in the chapter on vowed acts, as in al-Burhan. As for the second category, which is wajib, it is the make-up of what one has invalidated from a supererogatory fast, because it becomes obligatory by commencing, but it is not fard due to His saying 'Do not nullify your deeds' [47:33] and similar, because the evidence is not definitive in meaning. And from this category is the fast…
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This section from the sharh of Nur al-Idah begins a new chapter classifying fasting into six types. The matn gives a concise division: fard, wajib, masnun, mandub, nafl, and makruh. The sharh expands on the first two. Fard: established by qat'i (definitive) evidence – Quran, mutawatir hadith, ijma. It includes Ramadan (both adā' and qadā'), expiatory fasts (kaffarat for zihar, unintentional killing, broken oaths, hunting in ihram, and harm in ihram), and vowed fasts (nadhr) – the latter is considered fard in the apparent view, though a minority opinion says it is only wajib because the evidence (the verse 'Let them fulfill their vows' and the hadith 'Fulfill your vow') is not qat'i, making it similar to wujub established by solitary reports. The sharh mentions this ikhtilaf and postpones the discussion. Wajib: defined as making up a broken supererogatory fast. It becomes wajib by commencement (shurū'), but not fard, because the proof – the verse 'Do not nullify your deeds' and similar – is zannī al-dalāla (probable in meaning). Thus the Hanafi distinction between fard and wajib is based on the strength of evidence: fard rests on qat'i dalīl, wajib on zannī dalīl. This is a core Hanafi usul principle.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): A legally obligatory act established by definitive (qat'i) evidence, e.g., Quran, mutawatir hadith, or ijma.
المَنْذُور (al-mandhur): A fasting that a person vows to perform; according to the apparent view, it is fard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT included as a fard fast?
A) Fasting in Ramadan
B) Expiation for zihar
C) Vowed fasting (nadhr) in the apparent view
D) Making up a broken supererogatory fast

Key Takeaway
Fard fasting is defined by definitive evidence; vowed fasting is considered fard in the relied-upon Hanafi view, despite an internal dispute.

The Definition of Wajib Fasting and the Fard-Wajib Distinction
Defines wajib fasting as the make-up of a broken supererogatory fast, with evidence and distinction from fard.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define wajib fasting according to the Hanafi school and explain why it is classified as wajib rather than fard, citing the evidence.

Core Idea
Wajib fasting is binding but rests on probable evidence; the distinction from fard is the strength of the proof.

This lesson continues the classification of fasting, focusing on the second category: wajib. The passage defines wajib as the make-up of a broken supererogatory fast. It explains that such a fast becomes obligatory by commencement (shurū'), but the evidence is not definitive (qat'i), so it remains in the wajib category. Understanding this distinction is essential for recognizing the Hanafi hierarchy of obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of wajib
The passage states: 'wajib is the make-up of what one has invalidated from a supererogatory fast.' This is the only example given in this section.
2
Understand the reason for wujub
The fast becomes wajib because one has commenced it (shurū'). In Hanafi law, once a voluntary act of worship is started, it becomes binding to complete it (unless delayed by valid excuse).
This is based on the principle that a voluntary act becomes obligatory once commenced.
3
Analyze the evidence
The text cites the Quranic verse 'Do not nullify your deeds' (47:33) and similar proofs. This evidence is considered zannī al-dalāla (probable in meaning), not qat'i.
Because the verse is general and not specifically about fasting, it is not definitive.
4
Distinguish from fard
Fard is established by qat'i evidence; wajib by zannī evidence. The passage explicitly says that the ruling is not fard 'because the evidence is not definitive in meaning'.
This is a core Hanafi usul principle applied to fasting.
5
Apply the distinction
If someone denies the obligation to make up a broken nafl fast, it is a major sin but not kufr. Denying a fard (e.g., Ramadan) is kufr.

Source Text

This section from the sharh of Nur al-Idah begins a new chapter classifying fasting into six types. The matn gives a concise division: fard, wajib, masnun, mandub, nafl, and makruh. The sharh expands on the first two. Fard: established by qat'i (definitive) evidence – Quran, mutawatir hadith, ijma. It includes Ramadan (both adā' and qadā'), expiatory fasts (kaffarat for zihar, unintentional killing, broken oaths, hunting in ihram, and harm in ihram), and vowed fasts (nadhr) – the latter is considered fard in the apparent view, though a minority opinion says it is only wajib because the evidence (the verse 'Let them fulfill their vows' and the hadith 'Fulfill your vow') is not qat'i, making it similar to wujub established by solitary reports. The sharh mentions this ikhtilaf and postpones the discussion. Wajib: defined as making up a broken supererogatory fast. It becomes wajib by commencement (shurū'), but not fard, because the proof – the verse 'Do not nullify your deeds' and similar – is zannī al-dalāla (probable in meaning). Thus the Hanafi distinction between fard and wajib is based on the strength of evidence: fard rests on qat'i dalīl, wajib on zannī dalīl. This is a core Hanafi usul principle.

Key Terms
واجب (wajib): A legally obligatory act established by probable (zanni) evidence, e.g., solitary hadith or derived rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is making up a broken supererogatory fast classified as wajib rather than fard?
A) Because the fast was not originally obligatory.
B) Because the evidence (Quran 47:33) is not definitive in meaning.
C) Because it is only for women.
D) Because the fast was less than a day.

Key Takeaway
Wajib fasting is binding but based on probable evidence; the distinction from fard is the certainty of the proof, affecting the gravity of denial.

The Six Categories of Fasting in the Hanafi School
Classification of fasting into six categories with definitions and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six categories of fasting in the Hanafi school and identify which category a given type of fasting belongs to based on its evidentiary strength.

Core Idea
Hanafi fiqh classifies fasting into six categories with distinct evidentiary strength, from fard (qat'i) to makruh.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah, a commentary on Nur al-Idah, provides a systematic classification of fasting. The Hanafi school uniquely distinguishes between fard (obligatory with qat'i evidence) and wajib (necessary with zanni evidence), and further categorises sunnah, mandub, nafl, and makruh fasts. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for grasping the legal weight of different fasts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six categories
Read the initial sentence listing fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, nafl, and makruh. Note that these are the only categories for fasting in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Understand the fard category
Fard fasting includes Ramadan (on time and make-up), expiatory fasts (kaffarat), and vowed fasts (nadhr) according to the most apparent view. These are proven by qat'i (definitive) evidence.
Expiatory fasts are for zihar, accidental killing, broken oaths, hunting in ihram, and harm in ihram.
3
Understand the wajib category
Wajib fasting includes the make-up of a broken voluntary fast (nafl) and vowed fasts of i'tikaf. These are proven by zanni (non-definitive) evidence.
Breaking a nafl fast after commencement makes it wajib to make up.
4
Understand the sunnah, mandub, nafl categories
Sunnah: fasting Ashura with the 9th of Muharram. Mandub: three days a month (preferably the white days), Monday/Thursday, and six days of Shawwal. Nafl: any additional voluntary fasts.
Mandub is also called sunnah ghayr mu'akkadah in other contexts.
5
Note the makruh category
The passage mentions makruh but does not detail it. In Hanafi fiqh, certain days are makruh for fasting (e.g., the day of doubt).

Source Text

فصل في بيان صفة الصوم وتقسيمه

ينقسم الصوم إلى ستة أقسام: فرض، وواجب، ومسنون، ومندوب، ونفل، ومكروه. أما الفرض فهو: صوم شهر رمضان، أداءً وقضاء، وصوم الكفارات (الظهار، والقتل، واليمين، وجزاء الصيد، وفدية الأذى في الإحرام) والمنذور، في الأظهر. وأما الواجب فهو: قضاء ما أفسده من نفل، ومنه صوم الاعتكاف المنذور. وأما المسنون فهو: صوم عاشوراء مع التاسع. وأما المندوب فهو: صوم ثلاثة من كل شهر، ويندب كونها الأيام البيض، وهي الثالث عشر والرابع عشر والخامس عشر. وصوم الإثنين والخميس، وصوم ست من شوال، ثم قيل: الأفضل وصلها، وقيل: تفريقها.

اعلم أن الأشياء المختلفة الحقائق تصير واحداً باعتبار الأمر العام كالجوهر والسواد والبياض يصير واحداً باعتبار الوجود ويتكثر باعتبار الأمر الخاص كاللون يتنوع إلى البياض والسواد والخضرة، والصوم واحد باعتبار القربة والإمساك، لكنه يتنوع باعتبار أن هذا الصوم له أو عليه، فالذي عليه الواجب بإيجاب الله تعالى أو إيجاب العبد، كذا في المستصفى. فلذا قلنا: ينقسم الصوم إلى ستة أقسام، ذكرت مجملة ثم مفصلة، لكونه أوقع في النفس. فرض عين وواجب ومسنون ومندوب ونفل ومكروه. أما القسم الأول وهو الفرض فهو: صوم شهر رمضان، أداءً وقضاء، وصوم الكفارات الظهار والقتل واليمين وجزاء الصيد وفدية الأذى في الإحرام، لثبوت هذه بالقاطع من الأدلة سنداً ومتنا والإجماع عليها. ومن هذا القسم الصوم المنذور فهو فرض في الأظهر لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلْيُوفُوا نُذُورَهُمْ﴾ [الحج:29] وقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لعمر: «أوف بنذرك». قيل: إنه واجب؛ لأنه خص منها ما ليس من جنسه واجب كعيادة المريض، فلم يبق قطعياً وصار كخبر الواحد، وللآية المؤولة وبمثله يثبت الوجوب لا الفرض، وفيه نظر سنذكره في باب المنذورات، كما في البرهان.

وأما القسم الثاني وهو الواجب، فهو قضاء ما أفسده من الصوم نفل لوجوبه بالشروع، ولم يكن فرضاً لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تُبْطِلُوا أَعْمَالَكُمْ﴾ [محمد:33] ونحوه، لأن الدليل غير قطع الدلالة.

وأما القسم الثالث وهو المسنون، فهو صوم عاشوراء وأنه يكفر السنة الماضية مع صوم التاسع، لقول ابن عباس: «ما رأيت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يتحرى صيام يوم فضله على غيره إلا هذا اليوم يوم عاشوراء وهذا الشهر يعني رمضان» رواه الشيخان، وقال: «لئن بقيت إلى قابل لأصومن التاسع» رواه مسلم.

وأما القسم الرابع وهو المندوب فهو صوم ثلاثة أيام من كل شهر ليكون كصيام جميعه من جاء بالحسنة فله عشر أمثالها، وكان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يصوم ثلاثة أيام من الشهر الاثنين والخميس والاثنين من الجمعة الأخرى رواه أبو داود. ويندب كونها الأيام البيض، وهي الثالث عشر والرابع عشر والخامس عشر، سميت بذلك لتكامل ضوء الهلال وشدة البياض فيها، لما في أبي داود: كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يأمرنا أن نصوم البيض ثلاث عشرة وأربع عشرة وخمس عشرة، قال: وقال: هو كهيئة أي كصيام الدهر. وفي النسائي: كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يفطر الأيام البيض لا في حضر ولا في سفر. ومن هذا القسم: صوم يوم الإثنين ويوم الخميس لقوله: «تعرض الأعمال يوم الاثنين والخميس فأحب أن يعرض عملي وأنا صائم». ومنه صوم ست من شهر شوال لقوله: «من صام رمضان فأتبعه ستاً من شوال كان كصيام الدهر» رواه مسلم. ثم قيل: الأفضل وصلها الظاهر قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم ثم أتبعه، وقيل: تفريقها إظهاراً لمخالفة أهل الكتاب.
Chapter on explaining the description of fasting and its division. Fasting is divided into six categories: obligatory (fard), necessary (wajib), sunnah, recommended (mandub), supererogatory (nafl), and disliked (makruh). As for the obligatory (fard), it is: fasting Ramadan, whether on time or as make-up, and fasting of expiations and vowed fasts, in the most apparent view. As for the necessary (wajib), it is: making up what one corrupted from supererogatory fasts. As for the sunnah, it is: fasting Ashura with the ninth. As for the recommended (mandub), it is: fasting three days of every month, and it is recommended that they be the white days, which are the 13th, 14th, and 15th; fasting Monday and Thursday; and fasting six days of Shawwal. Then it is said: the best is to connect them (to Ramadan), and it is said: to separate them. Know that things of different realities become one by virtue of a general attribute... etc.
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah, a commentary on Nur al-Idah, provides a comprehensive classification of fasting in the Hanafi school. The author begins by stating that fasting divides into six categories: fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, nafl, and makruh. He then details each. Fard fasting includes Ramadan (both on time and make-up), expiatory fasts (kaffarat) such as those for zihar, accidental killing, broken oaths, hunting in ihram, and harm in ihram (fidyah), as well as vowed fasts (nadhr), though there is a difference of opinion on whether the latter is fard or wajib; the text says it is fard in the most apparent view (al-azhar), citing the Qur'anic command to fulfill vows and the hadith to 'Umar. The opposition says it is wajib because some obligations (like visiting the sick) are not qat'i, making vowed acts like khabar al-wahid in certainty; but the author notes this will be discussed later. Wajib fasting is the make-up of a broken voluntary fast (nafl), as it becomes obligatory by commencing it, but the evidence is not qat'i (hence wajib, not fard). This category also includes the vowed fast of i'tikaf. Sunnah fasting is Ashura (10th of Muharram) together with the 9th, based on hadith that the Prophet sought to fast it and planned to add the 9th. Mandub fasting includes three days of every month (preferably the white days, ayyam al-bid), Monday and Thursday, and six days of Shawwal. There is a minor difference on whether to connect the six days to Ramadan (preferred by the text) or separate them to distinguish from the People of the Book. The passage thus clarifies the Hanafi hierarchy of obligations and recommendations in fasting, with evidentiary support for each category.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): Obligatory fasting proven by qat'i evidence (Qur'an, mutawatir Sunnah, or ijma).
واجب (wajib): Necessary fasting proven by zanni evidence (e.g., ahad hadith or qiyas).
مسنون (sunnah): Regular practice of the Prophet, like fasting Ashura with the 9th.
مندوب (mandub): Recommended fasting that the Prophet encouraged, such as three days per month, Monday/Thursday, and six in Shawwal.
نفل (nafl): Voluntary fasting; if broken, its make-up becomes wajib (necessary) due to commencement.
مكروه (makruh): Disliked fasting; not detailed in this passage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is categorized as wajib fasting?
A) Fasting six days of Shawwal
B) Fasting the make-up of a broken voluntary (nafl) fast
C) Fasting Ramadan
D) Fasting Ashura

Key Takeaway
Fasting in Hanafi fiqh is classified into six categories with distinct evidentiary weight, and the fard/wajib distinction is unique to the school.

The Internal Hanafi Dispute on Vowed Fasts (Nadhr): Fard vs Wajib
Internal Hanafi dispute on whether vowed fasts (nadhr) are fard or wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the internal Hanafi dispute on whether a vowed fast (nadhr) is fard or wajib, and identify the evidence used by each side.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school is divided on whether vowed fasts are fard or wajib, a dispute rooted in the interpretation of Qur'anic and hadith evidence.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah focuses on the classification of vowed fasts (nadhr). While the majority position considers it fard, some Hanafi scholars argue it is wajib. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate conflicting arguments within the same school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The disputed ruling: whether a vowed fast is fard (obligatory with qat'i evidence) or wajib (necessary with zanni evidence).
This dispute is internal to the Hanafi school; other schools do not have this fard/wajib distinction.
2
Analyze the majority (fard) evidence
The text cites Qur'an (al-Hajj 29: 'Let them fulfill their vows') and the hadith where the Prophet told 'Umar: 'Fulfill your vow.' These are seen as explicit commands.
The majority considers these evidences qat'i in both transmission and meaning.
3
Analyze the minority (wajib) evidence
Some scholars argue that the Qur'anic verse is open to interpretation (mu'awwal) and the hadith is ahad, so they only give zanni proof. They draw an analogy to other obligations like visiting the sick, which are not qat'i.
4
Evaluate the tarjih (preference)
The author states that the most apparent view (al-azhar) is that nadhr is fard. The dispute will be revisited in the chapter on vows.
5
Understand the practical consequence
If fard, denying its obligation could be disbelief; if wajib, it is obligatory but denial is a lesser sin.
In practice, both require the fast to be kept; the difference is in the epistemological weight.

Source Text

نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship, such as fasting, as an obligation upon oneself.
أظهر (al-azhar): The most apparent or preferable view in the school.
مؤول (mu'awwal): A text that is open to interpretation, not literal.

The Masnun Fast: Ashura with the 9th
Ruling with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fasting Ashura and explain why it must be accompanied by the 9th or 11th.

Core Idea
Fasting Ashura alone is makruh tanzihi; the masnun is to fast it with the 9th.

This lesson focuses on the first category of non-obligatory fasts: the masnun (emphasized sunnah). The source passage explains that fasting Ashura (10th Muharram) is highly recommended but must be paired with the 9th (or 11th) to avoid imitation of the Jews. This ruling is based on hadith evidence and distinguishes the Hanafi position from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being discussed (fasting Ashura with/without the 9th) and the hukm level (masnun vs. makruh tanzihi).
Masnun is a category of sunnah muakkadah.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith cited: Ibn Abbas’ report about the Prophet’s eagerness for Ashura and his intention to fast the 9th.
Hadith from Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the masnun status? The Prophet specifically sought out Ashura and planned to add the 9th, indicating that the combination is the preferred practice.
4
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
The Hanafi school holds that fasting Ashura alone is makruh tanzihi, while the Shafi’i school considers it mustahabb (recommended).
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different interpretations of the hadith.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon Hanafi position is to fast Ashura with the 9th (or 11th) to avoid dislike.
As per the text, this is the masnun practice.

وأما المسنون فهو: صوم عاشوراء مع التاسع. وأما المندوب فهو: صوم ثلاثة من كل شهر، ويندب كونها الأيام البيض، وهي الثالث عشر، والرابع عشر والخامس عشر. وصوم الإثنين والخميس، وصوم ست من شوال، ثم قيل: الأفضل وصلها، وقيل: تفريقها. الاعتكاف المنذور. (وأما) القسم الثالث وهو (المسنون، فهو صوم عاشوراء) وأنه يكفر السنة الماضية (مع) صوم (التاسع)، لقول ابن عباس: «ما رأيت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يتحرى صيام يوم فضله على غيره إلا هذا اليوم يوم عاشوراء، وهذا الشهر يعني رمضان) رواه الشيخان، وقال: «لئن بقيت إلى قابل لأصومن التاسع» (٣) رواه مسلم. (وأما) القسم الرابع وهو المندوب فهو صوم ثلاثة) أيام (من كل شهر) ليكون كصيام جميعه من جاء بالحسنة فله عشر أمثالها، وكان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يصوم ثلاثة أيام من الشهر: الاثنين والخميس والاثنين من الجمعة الأخرى) رواه أبو داود (ويندب كونها: أي: الثلاثة (الأيام البيض، وهي الثالث عشر، والرابع عشر، والخامس عشر)، سميت بذلك لتكامل ضوء الهلال، وشدة البياض فيها، لما في أبي داود: كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: يأمرنا أن نصوم البيض: ثلاث عشرة، وأربع عشرة، وخمس عشرة، قال: وقال: هو كهيئة أي: كصيام الدهر»)، وفي (النسائي: كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يفطر الأيام البيض لا في حضر ولا في سفر»، (و) من هذا القسم: (صوم) يوم (الإثنين) ويوم (الخميس) لقوله: (تعرض الأعمال يوم الاثنين والخميس فأحب أن يعرض عملي وأنا صائم) (و) منه (صوم ست من) شهر (شوال) لقوله: (من صام رمضان ثم أتبعه ستا من شوال كان كصيام الدهر) رواه مسلم (ثم قيل: الأفضل وصلها الظاهر قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم ثم أتبعه، وقيل: تفريقها)؛ إظهارا لمخالفة أهل الكتاب في التشبه بالزيادة على المفروض، (و) منه (كل) صوم ثبت طلبه والوعد عليه بالسنة) الشريفة، (كصوم داود، عليه الصلاة و(السلام، كان: يصوم يوما، ويفطر يوما، وهو أفضل الصيام، وأحبه إلى الله تعالى) لقول النبي: (أحب الصيام إلى الله صيام داود، وأحب الصلاة إلى الله صلاة داود، كان ينام نصفه ويقوم ثلثه، وينام سدسه، وكان يفطر يوما ويصوم يوما) (٢) رواه أبو داود وغيره. (وأما) القسم الخامس: وهو (النفل فهو ما سوى ذلك الذي بيناه (مما) أي: صوم (لم يثبت عن الشارع (كراهيته) ولا تخصيصه بوقت (وأما) القسم السادس: وهو (المكروه فهو قسمان: مكروه تنزيها، ومكروه تحريما. الأول): الذي كره تنزيها (كصوم) يوم (عاشوراء، منفردا عن التاسع) (٣)، أو عن الحادي عشر، (والثاني) الذي كره تحريما (صوم العيدين: الفطر والنحر؛ للإعراض عن ضيافة الله تعالى ومخالفة الأمر، وهو لا يجوز، (و) منه صوم (أيام التشريق) لورود النهي عن صيامها)، وهذا التقسيم ذكره المحقق «الكمال بن الهمام رحمه الله تعالى، وقد صرح بحرمة صوم العيدين، وأيام التشريق في «البرهان»، (وكره: إفراد يوم الجمعة) بالصوم لقوله: (لا تخصوا ليلة الجمعة بقيام من بين الليالي، ولا تخصوا يوم الجمعة بصيام من بين الأيام، إلا أن يكون في صوم يصومه أحدكم) رواه مسلم، وقوله: (لا يصم أحدكم يوم الجمعة إلا أن يصوم قبله بيوم أو بعده) رواه أبو داود. (و) كره (إفراد يوم السبت) به لقوله: لا تصوموا يوم السبت إلا فيما افترض عليكم، فإن لم يجد (١) لما روي أن رمضان كتب على النصارى فوقع في برد، أو حر شديد، فحولوه إلى الربيع وزادوا عليه عشرين يوما كفارة لتحويله، وقيل: زادوا ذلك لموتان أصابهم تفسير البيضاوي (٤٦٢/١).
As for the recommended (masnun), it is: fasting Ashura with the ninth. As for the commendable (mandub), it is: fasting three days of every month, and it is commendable that they be the white days, which are the thirteenth, fourteenth, and fifteenth. And fasting Monday and Thursday, and fasting six days of Shawwal. Then it was said: the best is to connect them, and it was said: to separate them. The vowed retreat. (And as for) the third category which is (the masnun, it is fasting Ashura) and that it expiates the past year (with) fasting (the ninth), due to the saying of Ibn Abbas: 'I never saw the Prophet (peace be upon him) eagerly seeking to fast a day preferring it over others except this day, the day of Ashura, and this month meaning Ramadan' reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim, and he said: 'If I remain until next year, I will surely fast the ninth' (3) reported by Muslim. (And as for) the fourth category which is the mandub, it is fasting three days (from every month) so that it becomes like fasting all of it, because whoever does a good deed has ten times its like, and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to fast three days of the month: Monday and Thursday and Monday of the other Friday) reported by Abu Dawud. (And it is commendable that they be: i.e., the three (the white days, which are the thirteenth, fourteenth, and fifteenth), so named because of the completeness of the moonlight and the intensity of whiteness in them, as in Abu Dawud: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to command us to fast the white days: the thirteenth, fourteenth, and fifteenth, and he said: 'It is like the likeness of fasting forever'), and in al-Nasa'i: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) would not break the fast on the white days whether at home or traveling.' (And) from this category: (fasting) Monday (and) Thursday due to his saying: 'Deeds are presented on Monday and Thursday, so I love that my deeds be presented while I am fasting.' (And) from it (fasting six days of) Shawwal due to his saying: 'Whoever fasts Ramadan then follows it with six days of Shawwal, it is as if he fasted forever' reported by Muslim. (Then it was said: the best is to connect them, the apparent meaning of his saying (peace be upon him) 'then follows it', and it was said: to separate them) to show opposition to the People of the Book in imitating an increase upon the obligatory. (And) from it (every) fast whose seeking and promise are established by the noble Sunnah, (like the fast of Dawud, peace and blessings be upon him, he used to fast one day and break one day, and it is the best of fasting and most beloved to Allah Almighty) due to the saying of the Prophet: 'The most beloved fasting to Allah is the fasting of Dawud, and the most beloved prayer to Allah is the prayer of Dawud; he used to sleep half of it, stand a third, and sleep a sixth, and he would break one day and fast one day' (2) reported by Abu Dawud and others. (And as for) the fifth category: which is (the supererogatory (nafl) then it is other than that which we have explained, from that whose (dislike has not been established) from the Lawgiver, nor its specification to a time. (And as for) the sixth category: which is (the disliked (makruh) then it is of two types: disliked but not severely (tanzihi), and disliked prohibitively (tahrimi). The first): that which is disliked tanzihi (like fasting) the day of Ashura alone without the ninth (3), or without the eleventh. (And the second) that which is disliked tahrimi (fasting the two Eids: al-Fitr and al-Adha, to turn away from the hospitality of Allah Almighty and oppose the command, and it is not permissible. (And) from it is fasting (the days of Tashreeq) because of the prohibition of fasting them). This classification was mentioned by the verifier al-Kamal ibn al-Humam (may Allah have mercy on him), and he explicitly stated the prohibition of fasting the two Eids and the days of Tashreeq in al-Burhan. (And it is disliked: singling out Friday) for fasting due to his saying: 'Do not single out Friday night for standing in prayer among nights, nor single out Friday for fasting among days, unless it falls within a fast that one of you is observing' reported by Muslim, and his saying: 'None of you should fast on Friday unless he fasts a day before it or after it' reported by Abu Dawud. (And) it is disliked (singling out Saturday) for it due to his saying: 'Do not fast on Saturday except in what is prescribed for you, and if he does not find... (1) because it was related that Ramadan was prescribed for the Christians, and it fell in extreme cold or heat, so they moved it to spring and added twenty days as expiation for moving it, and it was said: they added that due to a plague that struck them. Tafsir al-Baydawi (1/462).
Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah is a commentary (sharh) on Nur al-Idah, where the author classifies non-obligatory fasts into distinct categories with their proofs. The author first defines the masnun (recommended) fast: fasting Ashura (10th of Muharram) along with the 9th, based on Ibn Abbas' report that the Prophet (peace be upon him) eagerly awaited this day and intended to fast the 9th if he lived. This fast expiates the previous year's minor sins. Next is the mandub (commendable) category: fasting three days each month, preferably the 'white days' (13th, 14th, 15th) due to their bright moonlight, as the Prophet commanded this and equated it to fasting all time. Also included are fasting Mondays and Thursdays, as deeds are presented on those days, and six days of Shawwal, which together with Ramadan equal a year's fasting. There is a difference of opinion on whether to fast them consecutively right after Eid or separately; the author notes that connecting them is better based on the apparent meaning of the hadith 'then follows it', while separating them shows opposition to the People of the Book who added to their prescribed fast. Then the author mentions every fast established by the Sunnah, like the fast of Prophet Dawud (alternate-day fasting), which is the best and most beloved to Allah. The fifth category is nafilah (supererogatory) – any fast that is not specifically encouraged nor disliked, essentially voluntary. The sixth is makruh (disliked), divided into tanzihi (lightly disliked) like fasting Ashura alone without the 9th or 11th, and tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) like fasting the two Eids and the days of Tashreeq (11th, 12th, 13th of Dhul Hijjah), which are forbidden. The author quotes the Hanafi jurist Kamal ibn al-Humam for this classification. Finally, it is disliked to single out Friday or Saturday for fasting without pairing with another day, as per hadith prohibitions, unless it coincides with a habitual fast. The footnote explains the Christian practice of moving Ramadan to spring. This passage provides a comprehensive Hanafi taxonomy of voluntary fasting.

Key Terms
المسنون (al-masnun): The fast that is established by consistent practice of the Prophet, emphasized but not obligatory; level between wajib and mandub.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on fasting the day of Ashura (10th Muharram) without the 9th or 11th?
A) It is haram
B) It is masnun
C) It is makruh tanzihi
D) It is wajib

Key Takeaway
The masnun fast of Ashura requires pairing with the 9th (or 11th); alone it is makruh tanzihi.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
7 min
Mandub Fasts: White Days, Monday/Thursday, and Six of Shawwal
Ruling with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the mandub fasts and apply the Hanafi recommendation to fast three white days, Mondays and Thursdays, and six days of Shawwal.

Core Idea
Mandub fasts are sunnah ghayr muakkadah; fasting three white days, Monday/Thursday, and six of Shawwal brings great reward.

This lesson covers the mandub (commendable) category of non-obligatory fasts. The source provides specific fasts that are recommended: three days each month, preferably the white days (13th, 14th, 15th), Mondays and Thursdays, and six days of Shawwal. The passage includes hadith evidence and a discussion on whether to connect the six days or separate them. Additionally, the fast of Prophet Dawud (alternate-day fasting) is mentioned as the best voluntary fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mandub acts
List the specific fasts mentioned: three days per month (white days), Monday and Thursday, six of Shawwal, and the fast of Dawud.
These are all sunnah ghayr muakkadah.
2
Examine the hadith evidence
For each, note the hadith: the white days equate to fasting a year, Monday/Thursday for presentation of deeds, six of Shawwal make a complete year with Ramadan, and Dawud fast is most beloved.
Hadith from Abu Dawud, Muslim, etc.
3
Understand the preference for connecting six of Shawwal
The text states it is better to fast them consecutively right after Eid, based on the word 'then follows' in the hadith, though separating is also allowed.
The difference is not a legal ikhtilaf but a preference.
4
Note the excellence of Dawud's fast
Fasting alternate days is the best voluntary fast, as confirmed by the Prophet.
This is included as a mandub fast.
5
Apply the ruling practically
A Muslim may choose to fast these days with the intention of attaining the rewards mentioned.
Omitting them has no sin.

(و) كره (إفراد يوم السبت) به لقوله: لا تصوموا يوم السبت إلا فيما افترض عليكم، فإن لم يجد (١) لما روي أن رمضان كتب على النصارى فوقع في برد، أو حر شديد، فحولوه إلى الربيع وزادوا عليه عشرين يوما كفارة لتحويله، وقيل: زادوا ذلك لموتان أصابهم تفسير البيضاوي (٤٦٢/١).
As for the recommended (masnun), it is: fasting Ashura with the ninth. As for the commendable (mandub), it is: fasting three days of every month, and it is commendable that they be the white days, which are the thirteenth, fourteenth, and fifteenth. And fasting Monday and Thursday, and fasting six days of Shawwal. Then it was said: the best is to connect them, and it was said: to separate them. The vowed retreat. (And as for) the third category which is (the masnun, it is fasting Ashura) and that it expiates the past year (with) fasting (the ninth), due to the saying of Ibn Abbas: 'I never saw the Prophet (peace be upon him) eagerly seeking to fast a day preferring it over others except this day, the day of Ashura, and this month meaning Ramadan' reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim, and he said: 'If I remain until next year, I will surely fast the ninth' (3) reported by Muslim. (And as for) the fourth category which is the mandub, it is fasting three days (from every month) so that it becomes like fasting all of it, because whoever does a good deed has ten times its like, and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to fast three days of the month: Monday and Thursday and Monday of the other Friday) reported by Abu Dawud. (And it is commendable that they be: i.e., the three (the white days, which are the thirteenth, fourteenth, and fifteenth), so named because of the completeness of the moonlight and the intensity of whiteness in them, as in Abu Dawud: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to command us to fast the white days: the thirteenth, fourteenth, and fifteenth, and he said: 'It is like the likeness of fasting forever'), and in al-Nasa'i: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) would not break the fast on the white days whether at home or traveling.' (And) from this category: (fasting) Monday (and) Thursday due to his saying: 'Deeds are presented on Monday and Thursday, so I love that my deeds be presented while I am fasting.' (And) from it (fasting six days of) Shawwal due to his saying: 'Whoever fasts Ramadan then follows it with six days of Shawwal, it is as if he fasted forever' reported by Muslim. (Then it was said: the best is to connect them, the apparent meaning of his saying (peace be upon him) 'then follows it', and it was said: to separate them) to show opposition to the People of the Book in imitating an increase upon the obligatory. (And) from it (every) fast whose seeking and promise are established by the noble Sunnah, (like the fast of Dawud, peace and blessings be upon him, he used to fast one day and break one day, and it is the best of fasting and most beloved to Allah Almighty) due to the saying of the Prophet: 'The most beloved fasting to Allah is the fasting of Dawud, and the most beloved prayer to Allah is the prayer of Dawud; he used to sleep half of it, stand a third, and sleep a sixth, and he would break one day and fast one day' (2) reported by Abu Dawud and others. (And as for) the fifth category: which is (the supererogatory (nafl) then it is other than that which we have explained, from that whose (dislike has not been established) from the Lawgiver, nor its specification to a time. (And as for) the sixth category: which is (the disliked (makruh) then it is of two types: disliked but not severely (tanzihi), and disliked prohibitively (tahrimi). The first): that which is disliked tanzihi (like fasting) the day of Ashura alone without the ninth (3), or without the eleventh. (And the second) that which is disliked tahrimi (fasting the two Eids: al-Fitr and al-Adha, to turn away from the hospitality of Allah Almighty and oppose the command, and it is not permissible. (And) from it is fasting (the days of Tashreeq) because of the prohibition of fasting them). This classification was mentioned by the verifier al-Kamal ibn al-Humam (may Allah have mercy on him), and he explicitly stated the prohibition of fasting the two Eids and the days of Tashreeq in al-Burhan. (And it is disliked: singling out Friday) for fasting due to his saying: 'Do not single out Friday night for standing in prayer among nights, nor single out Friday for fasting among days, unless it falls within a fast that one of you is observing' reported by Muslim, and his saying: 'None of you should fast on Friday unless he fasts a day before it or after it' reported by Abu Dawud. (And) it is disliked (singling out Saturday) for it due to his saying: 'Do not fast on Saturday except in what is prescribed for you, and if he does not find... (1) because it was related that Ramadan was prescribed for the Christians, and it fell in extreme cold or heat, so they moved it to spring and added twenty days as expiation for moving it, and it was said: they added that due to a plague that struck them. Tafsir al-Baydawi (1/462).
Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah is a commentary (sharh) on Nur al-Idah, where the author classifies non-obligatory fasts into distinct categories with their proofs. The author first defines the masnun (recommended) fast: fasting Ashura (10th of Muharram) along with the 9th, based on Ibn Abbas' report that the Prophet (peace be upon him) eagerly awaited this day and intended to fast the 9th if he lived. This fast expiates the previous year's minor sins. Next is the mandub (commendable) category: fasting three days each month, preferably the 'white days' (13th, 14th, 15th) due to their bright moonlight, as the Prophet commanded this and equated it to fasting all time. Also included are fasting Mondays and Thursdays, as deeds are presented on those days, and six days of Shawwal, which together with Ramadan equal a year's fasting. There is a difference of opinion on whether to fast them consecutively right after Eid or separately; the author notes that connecting them is better based on the apparent meaning of the hadith 'then follows it', while separating them shows opposition to the People of the Book who added to their prescribed fast. Then the author mentions every fast established by the Sunnah, like the fast of Prophet Dawud (alternate-day fasting), which is the best and most beloved to Allah. The fifth category is nafilah (supererogatory) – any fast that is not specifically encouraged nor disliked, essentially voluntary. The sixth is makruh (disliked), divided into tanzihi (lightly disliked) like fasting Ashura alone without the 9th or 11th, and tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) like fasting the two Eids and the days of Tashreeq (11th, 12th, 13th of Dhul Hijjah), which are forbidden. The author quotes the Hanafi jurist Kamal ibn al-Humam for this classification. Finally, it is disliked to single out Friday or Saturday for fasting without pairing with another day, as per hadith prohibitions, unless it coincides with a habitual fast. The footnote explains the Christian practice of moving Ramadan to spring. This passage provides a comprehensive Hanafi taxonomy of voluntary fasting.

Key Terms
المندوب (al-mandub): The fast that is recommended but not emphasized; if performed, reward, if omitted, no sin. Includes three white days, Monday/Thursday, six of Shawwal.
الأيام البيض (al-ayyam al-bid): The white days: 13th, 14th, 15th of each lunar month, named for the full moon's brightness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the best way to fast the six days of Shawwal?
A) They must be separated throughout the month
B) They must be connected immediately after Eid
C) Connecting them is better, but separating is also allowed
D) They are not recommended at all

Key Takeaway
Mandub fasts include white days, Monday/Thursday, six of Shawwal (preferably consecutive), and alternate-day fasting; all are sunnah ghayr muakkadah.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Nafil and Makruh Fasts: Voluntary and Disliked Categories
Ruling with proof from hadith and classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between nafil, makruh tanzihi, and makruh tahrimi fasts, and identify which fasts are prohibited or disliked in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Nafil is unrestricted voluntary fasting; makruh tanzihi includes Ashura alone; makruh tahrimi (prohibited) covers Eid and Tashreeq days.

This lesson covers the final two categories of non-obligatory fasts: nafil (supererogatory) and makruh (disliked). The source defines nafil as any fast not specifically encouraged or discouraged. Makruh is divided into tanzihi (lightly disliked) for actions like fasting Ashura alone or singling out Friday or Saturday, and tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) for fasting on the two Eids and the days of Tashreeq. The passage cites hadith for the prohibitions and notes the Hanafi position that the latter are haram (forbidden) though stated as makruh tahrimi.

Guided Path
1
Define nafil
Nafil is any voluntary fast that does not fit into the previous categories and has no specific disapproval or recommendation.
Example: random day fasting without intention of specific virtue.
2
Understand the two types of makruh
Makruh tanzihi: disliked but not sinful. Makruh tahrimi: prohibited, close to haram.
Hanafi distinguishes based on strength of evidence.
3
Identify makruh tanzihi cases
Fasting Ashura alone (without 9th/11th) and singling out Friday or Saturday for fasting.
Hadith forbid singling out Friday; Saturday is also disliked.
4
Identify makruh tahrimi cases
Fasting the two Eids and the days of Tashreeq (11th-13th Dhul Hijjah).
These are prohibited due to explicit hadith.
5
Apply the classification
A Muslim must avoid the tahrimi cases and be cautious with tanzihi cases.
The category of nafil allows flexibility.

(وأما) القسم الثالث وهو (المسنون، فهو صوم عاشوراء) وأنه يكفر السنة الماضية (مع) صوم (التاسع)، لقول ابن عباس: «ما رأيت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يتحرى صيام يوم فضله على غيره إلا هذا اليوم يوم عاشوراء، وهذا الشهر يعني رمضان) رواه الشيخان، وقال: «لئن بقيت إلى قابل لأصومن التاسع» (٣) رواه مسلم. (وأما) القسم الخامس: وهو (النفل فهو ما سوى ذلك الذي بيناه (مما) أي: صوم (لم يثبت عن الشارع (كراهيته) ولا تخصيصه بوقت (وأما) القسم السادس: وهو (المكروه فهو قسمان: مكروه تنزيها، ومكروه تحريما. الأول): الذي كره تنزيها (كصوم) يوم (عاشوراء، منفردا عن التاسع) (٣)، أو عن الحادي عشر، (والثاني) الذي كره تحريما (صوم العيدين: الفطر والنحر؛ للإعراض عن ضيافة الله تعالى ومخالفة الأمر، وهو لا يجوز، (و) منه صوم (أيام التشريق) لورود النهي عن صيامها)، وهذا التقسيم ذكره المحقق «الكمال بن الهمام رحمه الله تعالى، وقد صرح بحرمة صوم العيدين، وأيام التشريق في «البرهان»، (وكره: إفراد يوم الجمعة) بالصوم لقوله: (لا تخصوا ليلة الجمعة بقيام من بين الليالي، ولا تخصوا يوم الجمعة بصيام من بين الأيام، إلا أن يكون في صوم يصومه أحدكم) رواه مسلم، وقوله: (لا يصم أحدكم يوم الجمعة إلا أن يصوم قبله بيوم أو بعده) رواه أبو داود. (و) كره (إفراد يوم السبت) به لقوله: لا تصوموا يوم السبت إلا فيما افترض عليكم، فإن لم يجد (١) لما روي أن رمضان كتب على النصارى فوقع في برد، أو حر شديد، فحولوه إلى الربيع وزادوا عليه عشرين يوما كفارة لتحويله، وقيل: زادوا ذلك لموتان أصابهم تفسير البيضاوي (٤٦٢/١).
As for the recommended (masnun), it is: fasting Ashura with the ninth. As for the commendable (mandub), it is: fasting three days of every month, and it is commendable that they be the white days, which are the thirteenth, fourteenth, and fifteenth. And fasting Monday and Thursday, and fasting six days of Shawwal. Then it was said: the best is to connect them, and it was said: to separate them. The vowed retreat. (And as for) the third category which is (the masnun, it is fasting Ashura) and that it expiates the past year (with) fasting (the ninth), due to the saying of Ibn Abbas: 'I never saw the Prophet (peace be upon him) eagerly seeking to fast a day preferring it over others except this day, the day of Ashura, and this month meaning Ramadan' reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim, and he said: 'If I remain until next year, I will surely fast the ninth' (3) reported by Muslim. (And as for) the fourth category which is the mandub, it is fasting three days (from every month) so that it becomes like fasting all of it, because whoever does a good deed has ten times its like, and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to fast three days of the month: Monday and Thursday and Monday of the other Friday) reported by Abu Dawud. (And it is commendable that they be: i.e., the three (the white days, which are the thirteenth, fourteenth, and fifteenth), so named because of the completeness of the moonlight and the intensity of whiteness in them, as in Abu Dawud: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to command us to fast the white days: the thirteenth, fourteenth, and fifteenth, and he said: 'It is like the likeness of fasting forever'), and in al-Nasa'i: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) would not break the fast on the white days whether at home or traveling.' (And) from this category: (fasting) Monday (and) Thursday due to his saying: 'Deeds are presented on Monday and Thursday, so I love that my deeds be presented while I am fasting.' (And) from it (fasting six days of) Shawwal due to his saying: 'Whoever fasts Ramadan then follows it with six days of Shawwal, it is as if he fasted forever' reported by Muslim. (Then it was said: the best is to connect them, the apparent meaning of his saying (peace be upon him) 'then follows it', and it was said: to separate them) to show opposition to the People of the Book in imitating an increase upon the obligatory. (And) from it (every) fast whose seeking and promise are established by the noble Sunnah, (like the fast of Dawud, peace and blessings be upon him, he used to fast one day and break one day, and it is the best of fasting and most beloved to Allah Almighty) due to the saying of the Prophet: 'The most beloved fasting to Allah is the fasting of Dawud, and the most beloved prayer to Allah is the prayer of Dawud; he used to sleep half of it, stand a third, and sleep a sixth, and he would break one day and fast one day' (2) reported by Abu Dawud and others. (And as for) the fifth category: which is (the supererogatory (nafl) then it is other than that which we have explained, from that whose (dislike has not been established) from the Lawgiver, nor its specification to a time. (And as for) the sixth category: which is (the disliked (makruh) then it is of two types: disliked but not severely (tanzihi), and disliked prohibitively (tahrimi). The first): that which is disliked tanzihi (like fasting) the day of Ashura alone without the ninth (3), or without the eleventh. (And the second) that which is disliked tahrimi (fasting the two Eids: al-Fitr and al-Adha, to turn away from the hospitality of Allah Almighty and oppose the command, and it is not permissible. (And) from it is fasting (the days of Tashreeq) because of the prohibition of fasting them). This classification was mentioned by the verifier al-Kamal ibn al-Humam (may Allah have mercy on him), and he explicitly stated the prohibition of fasting the two Eids and the days of Tashreeq in al-Burhan. (And it is disliked: singling out Friday) for fasting due to his saying: 'Do not single out Friday night for standing in prayer among nights, nor single out Friday for fasting among days, unless it falls within a fast that one of you is observing' reported by Muslim, and his saying: 'None of you should fast on Friday unless he fasts a day before it or after it' reported by Abu Dawud. (And) it is disliked (singling out Saturday) for it due to his saying: 'Do not fast on Saturday except in what is prescribed for you, and if he does not find... Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah is a commentary (sharh) on Nur al-Idah, where the author classifies non-obligatory fasts into distinct categories with their proofs. The author first defines the masnun (recommended) fast: fasting Ashura (10th of Muharram) along with the 9th, based on Ibn Abbas' report that the Prophet (peace be upon him) eagerly awaited this day and intended to fast the 9th if he lived. This fast expiates the previous year's minor sins. Next is the mandub (commendable) category: fasting three days each month, preferably the 'white days' (13th, 14th, 15th) due to their bright moonlight, as the Prophet commanded this and equated it to fasting all time. Also included are fasting Mondays and Thursdays, as deeds are presented on those days, and six days of Shawwal, which together with Ramadan equal a year's fasting. There is a difference of opinion on whether to fast them consecutively right after Eid or separately; the author notes that connecting them is better based on the apparent meaning of the hadith 'then follows it', while separating them shows opposition to the People of the Book who added to their prescribed fast. Then the author mentions every fast established by the Sunnah, like the fast of Prophet Dawud (alternate-day fasting), which is the best and most beloved to Allah. The fifth category is nafilah (supererogatory) – any fast that is not specifically encouraged nor disliked, essentially voluntary. The sixth is makruh (disliked), divided into tanzihi (lightly disliked) like fasting Ashura alone without the 9th or 11th, and tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) like fasting the two Eids and the days of Tashreeq (11th, 12th, 13th of Dhul Hijjah), which are forbidden. The author quotes the Hanafi jurist Kamal ibn al-Humam for this classification. The footnote explains the Christian practice of moving Ramadan to spring. This passage provides a comprehensive Hanafi taxonomy of voluntary fasting.

Key Terms
المكروه تحريماً (al-makruh tahriman): A category of disliked that is prohibitively so; avoided to avoid sin, though not at the level of haram such that one becomes a fasiq. Includes fasting Eid days and Tashreeq.
النفل (al-nafl): Voluntary fasts beyond the categories of masnun and mandub; includes any fast not specifically encouraged or discouraged.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is considered makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) in the Hanafi school?
A) Fasting Ashura without the 9th
B) Fasting on the day of Eid al-Fitr
C) Fasting on Friday alone
D) Fasting the white days

Key Takeaway
Nafil is unrestricted voluntary fasting; makruh tanzihi includes Ashura alone and singling out Friday/Saturday; makruh tahrimi includes Eid and Tashreeq days.
