Sawm — Fasting
beginner
6 min
Sunnah Fasting: The Fast of Dawud
Defines sunnah fast and proves its excellence with hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of sunnah fasting, identify the fast of Dawud as the most beloved fast, and cite the hadith proof.

Core Idea
The best fast is the fast of Dawud – fasting every other day – as established by the Prophet's command.

The Hanafi school classifies voluntary fasts into sunnah, nafl, and makruh. The highest voluntary fast is the sunnah fast, exemplified by Prophet Dawud's pattern of fasting alternate days. This lesson examines the proof and excellence of this fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
Read the opening lines: 'وكل صومِ ثَبَتَ طَلَبُهُ والوعد عليه بالسُّنَّةِ'. The act is the sunnah fast, specifically the fast of Dawud.
Sunnah here means recommended (mandub).
2
Extract the specific ruling
The text says 'وهو أفضل الصيام وأحبه إلى الله تعالى' – it is the best fast and most beloved.
This is a station of virtue, not obligation.
3
Analyze the dalil
The hadith from Abu Dawud: 'أحب الصيام إلى الله صيام داود...' serves as the proof. Identify the chain and meaning.
The hadith is sahih, establishing the recommendation.
4
Understand the wisdom
Fasting every other day balances worship and worldly needs, as Dawud would fast a day and break a day.
This pattern avoids extremism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل صومِ ثَبَتَ طَلَبُهُ والوعد عليه بالسُّنَّةِ، كصوم داود عليه السَّلامُ، كان يَصُوْمُ يَوْمَا، وَيُفْطِرُ يَوْمَاً، وهو أفضل الصيام وأحَبَّهُ إلى الله تعالى، وأما النفل فهو ما سوى ذلك، مما لم يَثْبُتْ كَرَاهِيَتُهُ وأما المكروه فهو قسمان: مكروه تنزيهاً، ومكروه تحريماً. الأوَّلُ: كَصَوْمِ عاشوراء، منفرداً عن التاسع. والثاني: صوم العيدين، وأيام التشريق. وكَرِهَ: إفراد يوم الجمعة. وإفراد يومِ السَّبْتِ. التشبه بالزيادة) على المفروض، (وَ) منه (كل) صوم ثَبَتَ طَلَبُهُ والوعد عليه بالسُّنَّةِ) الشريفة، (كصوم داود، عليه الصلاة والسلام، كان : يَصُومُ يَوْمَا، وَيُفْطِرُ يَوْمَاً، وهو أفضلُ الصِّيامِ، وَأَحَبُّهُ إلى الله تعالى) لقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: ( أحَبُّ الصَّيَامَ إِلَى اللَّه صَيَام دَاودَ ، وأَحَبُّ الصَّلاةِ إِلَى اللَّه صَلَاةُ دَاوِدَ، كان يَنَامُ نِصْفَهُ وَيَقُومُ ثُلْثَهُ، وَيَنَامُ سُدسَهُ، وَكان يُفْطِرُ يَوْمَاً وَيَصُومُ يَوْمَاً ) رواه أبو داود وغيره. (وأما) القسم الخامس: وهو (النفلُ فهو ما سوى ذلك الذي بيناه ( مِمَّا ) أي: صوم (لم يثبت عن الشارع (كَرَاهِيتُهُ) ولا تخصيصه بوقت ( وأما ) القسم السادس: وهو ( المكروه فهو قسمان: مكروه تنزيهاً، ومكروه تحريماً الأوَّلُ ): الذي كره تنزيهاً (كَصَوْمِ ) يوم (عاشوراء، منفرداً عن التاسع) أو عن الحادي عشر، (والثاني) الذي كره تحريماً (صوم العيدين الفطر والنحر؛ للإعراض عن ضيافة الله تعالى ومخالفة الأمر، وهو لا يجوز، (وَ) منه صوم (أيَّامِ التشريق) لورود النهي عن صيامها)، وهذا التقسيم ذكره المحقق الكمال بن الهمام رحمه الله تعالى، وقد صرح بحرمة صوم العيدين، وأيام التشريق في « البرهان»، (وكره: إفراد يوم الجمعة) بالصوم لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : ( لا تخصوا ليلة الجمعة بقيام من بين الليالي، ولا تخصوا يوم الجمعة بصيام من بين الأيام، إلا أن يكون في صوم يصومه أحدكم ) رواه مسلم، وقوله : ( لا يصم أحدكم يوم الجمعة إلا أن يصوم قبله بيوم أو بعده " رواه أبو داود. (و) كره ( إفراد يوم السبت) به لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا تصوموا يوم السبت إلا فيما افترض عليكم، فإن لم يجد أحدكم إلا لحاء عنبة، أو عود شجرة فليمضغه ) رواه أحمد وأصحاب السنن إلا النسائي. (وَ) كره إفراد (يومِ النَّيْرُوزِ ) أصله النوروز، لكن لما لم يكن في أوزان العرب فوعول أبدلوا الواو ياء، وهو : يوم في طرف الربيع، (أو) إفراد يوم (المَهْرَجَانِ) معرب مهركان، وهو : يوم في طرف الخريف، وقال في ريح كوشياء: النيروز أول يوم في فروردين ماه، والمهرجان هو: اليوم السادس عشر من مهرماه، قلت: فروردين ماه أول أشهر الفرس، ومهرماه هو الشهر السابع من السنة عندهم، كذا في «شرح الهداية للعيني رحمه الله تعالى، وكره ذلك لأن فيه تعظيم أيام نهينا عن تعظيمها، (إلا أن يوافق) ذلك اليوم (عادته) في الصوم لفوت علة الكراهة. وكره صوم الوصال، ولو) واصل بين (يومين) فقط للنهي عنه) (وهو) أي: الوصال أن لا يفطر بعد الغروب أصلاً حتى يتصل صوم الغد بالأمس). وكره صوم الصمت، وهو أن يصوم ولا يتكلم بشيء، فعليه أن يتكلم بخير وبحاجة دعت إليه. (وكره صوم الدهر ) لأنه يضعفه أو يصير طبعاً له، ومبنى العبادة على مخالفة العادة، ولا تصوم المرأة نفلاً إلا بإذن زوجها، وله أن يفطرها لقيام حقه، واحتياجه، كما في « البرهان» والله أعلم.
And every fast whose seeking and promise is established by the Sunnah, like the fast of Dawud, peace be upon him, he used to fast a day and break a day, and it is the best of fasts and most beloved to Allah Almighty. As for nafl, it is other than that, which no dislike has been established. As for makruh, it is two types: makruh tanzihi and makruh tahrimi. The first: like fasting Ashura alone without the ninth. The second: fasting the two Eids and the days of Tashriq. And it is disliked to single out Friday and to single out Saturday. [...] And it is disliked to fast on Nawruz or Mihrajan, unless it coincides with his habit. And the fast of wisal is disliked, even if only two days. And the fast of silence is disliked. And the fast of the entire year is disliked. And a woman should not fast nafl except with her husband's permission.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah provides a detailed classification of voluntary fasts in the Hanafi school. It begins by defining sunnah fasting as that which is specifically recommended by the Prophet ﷺ, citing the best example: the fast of Prophet Dawud, fasting every other day. This is the most beloved fast to Allah. Then it defines nafl (supererogatory) as any fast not established as disliked or specifically recommended. The passage then turns to makruh (disliked) fasts, which are divided into two categories: makruh tanzihi (slightly disliked) and makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). Examples of makruh tanzihi include fasting on Ashura alone (10th Muharram without the 9th or 11th) and singling out Friday or Saturday for fasting. Makruh tahrimi includes fasting on the two Eids and the days of Tashriq (11th, 12th, 13th of Dhul Hijjah), which is actually haram (forbidden) as stated by the commentator Kamal ibn al-Humam. The passage also covers fasting on Persian festivals (Nawruz and Mihrajan), noting that it is disliked unless it coincides with one's habit. Further, it mentions the fast of wisal (continuous fasting without breaking at sunset) and the fast of silence, both disliked. Fasting every day of the year (sawm al-dahr) is also disliked due to potential harm or making it a habit. Finally, a woman may not observe voluntary fasts without her husband's permission, as his rights take precedence. Each ruling is supported by hadith evidence, demonstrating the Hanafi method of deriving rulings.

Key Terms
صوم السنة (sawm al-sunnah): A fast whose seeking and promise is established by the Sunnah, i.e., specifically recommended.
صوم داود (sawm Dawud): Fasting every other day, the best voluntary fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on fasting every other day (the fast of Dawud)?
A) Wajib
B) Sunnah (mandub)
C) Mubah
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
The fast of Dawud is the best voluntary fast because the Prophet ﷺ declared it the most beloved to Allah, and it represents the balance of worship and worldly life.

Sawm — Fasting

Makruh Fasts: Distinguishing Tanzihi and Tahrimi
Classifies disliked fasts with examples and hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between makruh tanzihi and makruh tahrimi in fasting, list specific examples, and cite the evidence for each.

Core Idea
Makruh fasts divide into tanzihi (slightly disliked) and tahrimi (prohibitively disliked), with examples like fasting Ashura alone and fasting on Eids.

The Hanafi school divides disliked fasts into two categories: makruh tanzihi, which is better to avoid but not sinful, and makruh tahrimi, which is sinful to perform. This lesson explains these categories with their evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The text states: 'المكروه فهو قسمان: مكروه تنزيهاً، ومكروه تحريماً'. Note the classification.
2
List examples for each
For tanzihi: fasting Ashura alone (10th without 9th or 11th), singling out Friday or Saturday. For tahrimi: fasting the two Eids and days of Tashriq.
The text also mentions Nawruz and Mihrajan as tanzihi.
3
Analyze the proofs
For Friday: hadith 'لا تخصوا يوم الجمعة بصيام'. For Saturday: hadith 'لا تصوموا يوم السبت'. For Eids: based on absence of permission and disobedience.
The tahrimi proofs are stronger, sometimes using the word 'لا يجوز'.
4
Understand the consequences
Fasting makruh tanzihi is allowed but disliked; leaving it is better. Fasting makruh tahrimi is sinful and should be abandoned.
The commentator Kamal ibn al-Humam considers Eids and Tashriq as haram.

Source Text

Key Terms
مكروه تنزيهاً (makruh tanzihan): Disliked but not sinful; leaving it is better than doing it.
مكروه تحريماً (makruh tahriman): Prohibitively disliked; doing it is sinful but not as severe as haram.
صوم عاشوراء (sawm 'Ashura): Fasting on the 10th of Muharram.

Which of the following is an example of makruh tahrimi fasting?
A) Fasting Friday alone

Key Takeaway
Makruh tahrimi fasts (Eids and Tashriq) must be avoided; makruh tanzihi fasts (like Friday alone) are better not done but are not sinful.

Additional Disliked Fasts: Wisal, Silence, Dahr, and Woman's Permission
Lists additional disliked fasts and the rule for woman's nafl fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on sawm al-wisal, sawm al-samt, sawm al-dahr, and explain why a woman needs her husband's permission for nafl fasting.

Core Idea
Continuous fasting, silence fasting, and perpetual fasting are disliked; a woman's nafl fast requires the husband's permission.

Beyond the major categories, the Hanafi school also addresses other forms of fasting that are disliked due to harm, habit, or neglect of rights. This lesson covers these and the important condition of a wife's nafl fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the additional disliked fasts
The text lists: صوم الوصال (continuous fast), صوم الصمت (silence fast), and صوم الدهر (perpetual fast).
2
Understand the proofs and reasons
Wisal is disliked due to prohibition in hadith. Samt is disliked because it resembles pre-Islamic practices. Dahr is disliked because it becomes a habit or harms the body.
The text also says 'مبنى العبادة على مخالفة العادة'.
3
Analyze the ruling on woman's nafl
The text states: 'لا تصوم المرأة نفلاً إلا بإذن زوجها'. The husband has a right that may be affected by her fasting.
The husband can break her fast if he needs her.
4
Derive the underlying principle
These rulings protect against extremism, harm, and neglect of obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وأما) القسم الخامس: وهو (النفلُ فهو ما سوى ذلك الذي بيناه ( مِمَّا ) أي: صوم (لم يثبت عن الشارع (كَرَاهِيتُهُ) ولا تخصيصه بوقت ( وأما ) القسم السادس: وهو ( المكروه فهو قسمان: مكروه تنزيهاً، ومكروه تحريماً الأوَّلُ ): الذي كره تنزيهاً (كَصَوْمِ ) يوم (عاشوراء، منفرداً عن التاسع) أو عن الحادي عشر، (والثاني) الذي كره تحريماً (صوم العيدين الفطر والنحر؛ للإعراض عن ضيافة الله تعالى ومخالفة الأمر، وهو لا يجوز، (وَ) منه صوم (أيَّامِ التشريق) لورود النهي عن صيامها)، وهذا التقسيم ذكره المحقق الكمال بن الهمام رحمه الله تعالى، وقد صرح بحرمة صوم العيدين، وأيام التشريق في « البرهان»، (وكره: إفراد يوم الجمعة) بالصوم لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : ( لا تخصوا ليلة الجمعة بقيام من بين الليالي، ولا تخصوا يوم الجمعة بصيام من بين الأيام، إلا أن يكون في صوم يصومه أحدكم ) رواه مسلم، وقوله : ( لا يصم أحدكم يوم الجمعة إلا أن يصوم قبله بيوم أو بعده " رواه أبو داود. (و) كره ( إفراد يوم السبت) به لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا تصوموا يوم السبت إلا فيما افترض عليكم، فإن لم يجد أحدكم إلا لحاء عنبة، أو عود شجرة فليمضغه ) رواه أحمد وأصحاب السنن إلا النسائي. (وَ) كره إفراد (يومِ النَّيْرُوزِ ) أصله النوروز، لكن لما لم يكن في أوزان العرب فوعول أبدلوا الواو ياء، وهو : يوم في طرف الربيع، (أو) إفراد يوم (المَهْرَجَانِ) معرب مهركان، وهو : يوم في طرف الخريف، وقال في ريح كوشياء: النيروز أول يوم في فروردين ماه، والمهرجان هو: اليوم السادس عشر من مهرماه، قلت: فروردين ماه أول أشهر الفرس، ومهرماه هو الشهر السابع من السنة عندهم، كذا في «شرح الهداية للعيني رحمه الله تعالى، وكره ذلك لأن فيه تعظيم أيام نهينا عن تعظيمها، (إلا أن يوافق) ذلك اليوم (عادته) في الصوم لفوت علة الكراهة. وكره صوم الوصال، ولو) واصل بين (يومين) فقط للنهي عنه) (وهو) أي: الوصال أن لا يفطر بعد الغروب أصلاً حتى يتصل صوم الغد بالأمس). وكره صوم الصمت، وهو أن يصوم ولا يتكلم بشيء، فعليه أن يتكلم بخير وبحاجة دعت إليه. (وكره صوم الدهر ) لأنه يضعفه أو يصير طبعاً له، ومبنى العبادة على مخالفة العادة، ولا تصوم المرأة نفلاً إلا بإذن زوجها، وله أن يفطرها لقيام حقه، واحتياجه، كما في « البرهان» والله أعلم.
And every fast whose seeking and promise is established by the Sunnah, like the fast of Dawud, peace be upon him, he used to fast a day and break a day, and it is the best of fasts and most beloved to Allah Almighty. As for nafl, it is other than that, which no dislike has been established. As for makruh, it is two types: makruh tanzihi and makruh tahrimi. The first: like fasting Ashura alone without the ninth. The second: fasting the two Eids and the days of Tashriq. And it is disliked to single out Friday and to single out Saturday. [...] And it is disliked to fast on Nawruz or Mihrajan, unless it coincides with his habit. And the fast of wisal is disliked, even if only two days. And the fast of silence is disliked. And the fast of the entire year is disliked. And a woman should not fast nafl except with her husband's permission.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah provides a detailed classification of voluntary fasts in the Hanafi school. It begins by defining sunnah fasting as that which is specifically recommended by the Prophet ﷺ, citing the best example: the fast of Prophet Dawud, fasting every other day. This is the most beloved fast to Allah. Then it defines nafl (supererogatory) as any fast not established as disliked or specifically recommended. The passage then turns to makruh (disliked) fasts, which are divided into two categories: makruh tanzihi (slightly disliked) and makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). Examples of makruh tanzihi include fasting on Ashura alone (10th Muharram without the 9th or 11th) and singling out Friday or Saturday for fasting. Makruh tahrimi includes fasting on the two Eids and the days of Tashriq (11th, 12th, 13th of Dhul Hijjah), which is actually haram (forbidden) as stated by the commentator Kamal ibn al-Humam. The passage also covers fasting on Persian festivals (Nawruz and Mihrajan), noting that it is disliked unless it coincides with one's habit. Further, it mentions the fast of wisal (continuous fasting without breaking at sunset) and the fast of silence, both disliked. Fasting every day of the year (sawm al-dahr) is also disliked due to potential harm or making it a habit. Finally, a woman may not observe voluntary fasts without her husband's permission, as his rights take precedence. Each ruling is supported by hadith evidence, demonstrating the Hanafi method of deriving rulings.

Key Terms
صوم الوصال (sawm al-wisal): Fasting continuously without breaking at sunset for two or more days.
صوم الصمت (sawm al-samt): Fasting while remaining silent and not speaking.
صوم الدهر (sawm al-dahr): Fasting every day of the year without exception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a disliked fast according to the Hanafi school?
A) Fast of silence
B) Fast of Dawud
C) Continuous fast (wisal)
D) Perpetual fast (dahr)

Key Takeaway
Disliked fasts include those that cause harm, imitate non-Islamic practices, or infringe on the rights of others; a woman’s nafl fast requires her husband’s permission.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner
8 min
Disliked Fasts: Nayruz, Mihrajan, Wisal, Samt, and Dahr
Lists disliked fasts with reasons and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five types of disliked fasts in Hanafi fiqh, explain the reason for each, and identify when the dislike is lifted.

Core Idea
Fasting is makruh when it imitates non-Muslims, weakens the body, or violates rights; necessity or habit can remove the dislike.

This passage from the commentary on Nur al-Idah lists several types of fasting that are considered makruh tanzihi (disliked) in the Hanafi school. You will learn the specific rulings for Nayruz, Mihrajan, Wisal, Sawm al-Samt, and Sawm al-Dahr, along with the conditions that can remove the dislike. Understanding these rulings helps avoid acts that may detract from the spiritual benefits of fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify each act and its ruling level
For each type of fast (Nayruz, Mihrajan, Wisal, Samt, Dahr), confirm that the ruling is makruh tanzihi (disliked but not sinful). Note that singling out these days or practices is the basis of dislike.
The passage also includes a separate ruling: a woman's voluntary fast requires her husband's permission.
2
Understand the reason for dislike
For Nayruz and Mihrajan, the reason is glorifying non-Muslim festivals. For Wisal, it is the prohibition of continuous fasting without breaking. For Sawm al-Samt, silence is not worship; speaking good is encouraged. For Sawm al-Dahr, it weakens the body and becomes a habit, contradicting the spirit of worship.
The passage cites a hadith for Friday fasting as analogy, but not for others; reasons are given contextually.
3
Identify exceptions that remove dislike
For Nayruz and Mihrajan, if the day coincides with one's habitual fast (e.g., regular Monday fast), the dislike is lifted because the intention is not to glorify the festival. For other types, no exceptions are mentioned except that a woman's voluntary fast is permitted with her husband's permission.
The exception for habit applies only to Nayruz and Mihrajan in this passage.
4
Apply the woman's additional ruling
A woman may not fast voluntarily without her husband's permission, and he has the right to break her fast to fulfill his marital needs. This ruling does not apply to obligatory fasts.
This is included in the passage as a separate point.

And (it is disliked to fast) on the day of Nayruz or Mihrajan, unless it coincides with his habit. And it is disliked to fast Wisal, even for two days. And it is: that he does not break fast after sunset at all, so that the fast of the next day connects with yesterday. And it is disliked to fast forever. (Due to the hadith of Abu Hurairah, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'Let none of you fast on Friday unless he fasts before it or after it.') Reported by Ahmad and the authors of the Sunan except al-Nasa'i. And it is disliked to single out the day of Nayruz. Its origin is al-Nawruz, but since it was not in the meters of the Arabs as Fu'ul, they substituted the waw with ya, and it is: a day at the edge of spring. Or to single out the day of Mihrajan, which is a Persian word Mihrakan, and it is: a day at the edge of autumn. And he said in Rayh Kushya: Nayruz is the first day of Farwardin Mah, and Mihrajan is the sixteenth day of Mehr Mah. I said: Farwardin Mah is the first month of the Persians, and Mehr Mah is the seventh month of the year according to them. Thus in Sharh al-Hidayah by al-Ayni, may Allah have mercy on him. This is disliked because it involves glorifying days which we have been forbidden to glorify. (Unless) that day (coincides with his habit) in fasting, because the cause of dislike is absent. And it is disliked to fast Wisal, even if he connects (only) two days due to the prohibition. And it (i.e., Wisal) is: that he does not break fast after sunset at all, so that the fast of the next day connects with yesterday. And it is disliked to fast (the silence fast), which is that he fasts and does not speak at all; rather, he should speak good and about a need that arises. (And it is disliked to fast the entire year) because it weakens him or becomes a habit for him, and worship is based on opposing habit. And a woman should not fast voluntarily except with her husband's permission, and he may break her fast to fulfill his right and need, as in al-Burhan. And Allah knows best.
(1) Reported by Abu Dawud in Fasting, Chapter: The prohibition of singling out Saturday for fasting (2421), and al-Tirmidhi in Fasting, Chapter: What has been narrated about fasting on Saturday (744), and Ahmad in his Musnad (4/189).
(2) Reported by Muslim in Fasting, Chapter: The prohibition of Wisal in fasting (1104), and al-Tirmidhi in Fasting, Chapter: What has been narrated about the dislike of Wisal for the fasting person (778).
(3) As for the obligatory fast, even if it is a specific obligation, it does not depend on his pleasure because abandoning it is disobedience, and there is no obedience to a creature in disobeying the Creator. End.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Nur al-Idah, listing several types of disliked fasts in the Hanafi school. The author explains each ruling with its rationale. 

First, it is makruh tanzihi (disliked) to fast specifically on the days of Nayruz (Persian New Year) and Mihrajan (a Persian autumn festival), because these are festivals of disbelievers and singling them out for fasting shows reverence for them. However, if one habitually fasts on that day (e.g., because it coincides with a regular Monday or Thursday fast), the dislike is lifted as the intention is not to glorify the festival.

Second, Wisal (continuous fasting from sunset to the next night without breaking fast) is makruh even if only for two days, due to the prophetic prohibition.

Third, fasting while observing silence (not speaking at all) is makruh; one should speak good or when necessary, as silence is not part of worship.

Fourth, fasting every day (Sawm al-Dahr) is makruh because it weakens the body and makes fasting a habit rather than an act of worship built on opposing one's desires.

Finally, a woman may not fast voluntary fasts without her husband's permission because he has a right to intimacy. He may even break her voluntary fast to fulfill his right. This does not apply to obligatory fasts.

Key Terms
النَّيْرُوزِ (al-Nayruz): A Persian festival at the beginning of spring (New Year's day).
المَهْرَجَانِ (al-Mihrajan): A Persian festival in autumn, specifically the 16th day of Mehr Mah.
الوِصَال (al-Wisal): Continuous fasting from sunset to the next day without breaking fast at all.
صوم الصمت (Sawm al-Samt): Fasting while refraining from speaking entirely.
صوم الدَّهرِ (Sawm al-Dahr): Fasting every day of the year without exception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what is the ruling on fasting the entire year (Sawm al-Dahr)?
A) It is wajib for those able.
B) It is mustahabb (recommended).
C) It is makruh (disliked).
D) It is haram (forbidden).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies five types of fasting as makruh tanzihi, primarily due to imitation of non-Muslims, physical harm, or violation of rights, but these dislikes can be overridden by habit or necessity.

Sawm — Fasting

When Intention for Fasting Does Not Require Pre-Dawn Specification
Explains categories exempt from pre-dawn intention and specification, with proof and intra-school disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three types of fasts that do not require pre-dawn intention and specification, and apply the correct time limit for making the intention according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
For Ramadan, vowed fasts with fixed time, and voluntary fasts, intention can be made up to just before half the day—no need for pre-dawn specification.

In this passage, the commentator explains a key relaxation in the rules of intention for fasting. While other fasts require the intention to be made before dawn and to specify the type of fast, three categories are granted leniency. Understanding this dispensation is essential for correct practice, especially for those who may wake up late or forget to make intention at night.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three exempted fasts
From the passage, list the three types of fasts for which specification of intention and pre-dawn timing are not required: Ramadan, a vowed fast with a specified time, and voluntary fasts.
2
Determine the valid time window
According to the preferred Hanafi view (from al-Jami' al-Saghir), intention can be made from the night up to just before half the day. Half the day is the midpoint between dawn and sunset, not zawal.
3
Analyze the hadith proof
The hadith 'La siyama liman lam yubayyit' is interpreted as negation of perfection, not obligation, similar to 'no prayer for the neighbor of the mosque except in the mosque'. Voluntary fasts are explicitly excepted.
4
Compare intra-Hanafi disagreement
Al-Quduri held intention valid until zawal (midday), but the author prefers the view before half the day because at zawal most of the day has not remained, undermining the precautionary condition of intention in most of the day.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a specific scenario (e.g., on a Ramadan day, dawn at 4 AM, sunset at 8 PM, half-day at 12 PM), determine if intention made at 11 AM is valid.

فصل فيما لا يشترط تبييت النية وتعيينها فيه وما يشترط ذلك أما القسم الذي لا يُشترط فيه تعيين النية، ولا تبيتُها، فهو: أداء رمضان، والنَّدْرُ المُعَيَّنُ زمانُهُ، والنقل. فيصح بنيةٍ منَ اللّيل إلى ما قبل نصف النَّهارِ، على الأصحُ ونصفُ النَّهارِ مِنْ طلوع الفجر إلى . فصل فيما لا يتشرط تبييت النية وتعيينها فيه وما يشترط ذلك أما القسمُ الَّذي لا يُشترط فيه تعيينُ النّيّة) لما يصومه (ولا) يشترط فيه أيضاً (تببيتها ) أي: النية فيه ( فهو : أداء رمضانَ وَ( أداء (النَّدْرُ المُعَيَّنُ (زمانُهُ) كقوله: الله علي صوم يوم الخميس من هذه الجمعة، فإذا نوى مطلق الصوم من ليلته إلى ما قبل نصف النهار صح، وخرج به عن عهدة المنذور، (و) أداء ( النَّفْلُ. فيصحُ) كل من هذه الثلاثة (بنية) معينة مبيتة (من الليل) وهو الأفضل، وحقيقـة النية: قصده بقلبه أنه يصوم غداً، ولا يخلو مسلم عن هذا في ليالي شهر رمضان، إلا ما نذر، وليست النية باللسان شرطاً، كذا في ( الاختيار»، ومحمل ما روي من قوله : « لا صِيَامَ لِمَنْ لَمْ يُبيت الصَّيَامَ مِنْ اللَّيلِ » ) ويعزم على نفي الكمال كقوله : ( لا صَلاةَ لِجَارِ المَسْجِدِ إِلا فِي المَسْجِدِ » (۳) وحمل على غير ذلك، وقد خص منه النفل، فلم يكن قطعياً في اشتراط التبييت، وكذا يصح كل منها بنية من ابتداء اليوم ) إلى ما قبل نصفِ النَّهارِ)، لأن الشرط وجود النية في أكثر النهار احتياطاً، وبـه توجد في كله حكماً، إذ لأكثر الشيء حكم كله في كثير من موارد الفقه، وخص هذا بالصوم فخــرج الحج والصلاة؛ لأنَّ الصوم ركن واحد [ ممتد ]، فبالوجود في أكثره يعتبر قيامها في كله بخلافهما فإنهما أركان، فيشترط قرانها بالعقد على أدائها، وإلا [ خلت ] بعض الأركان عنها، فلم يقع ذلك الركن عبادة، وإنما قلنا: إلى ما قبل نصف النهار تبعاً للجامع الصغير » لا كمـا ذكــر « القـدوري » بقوله : ما بَيْنَهُ أي طلوع الفجر وبين الزوال، إذ عند الزوال لم يبق أكثر الـيـوم، فكان قولنـا ( عـلـى الأصح) احترازاً عنه، وإنما قال ( ونصفُ النَّهارِ مِنْ) ابتداء (طلوع الفجر إلى) قبيــل
Section: Regarding what does not require pre-dawn intention and specification of intention, and what requires that. As for the category for which specification of intention is not required, nor its pre-dawn timing, it is: the performance of Ramadan, a vowed fast whose time is specified, and voluntary fast. So each is valid with an intention from the night up to before half of the day, according to the most correct view. And half of the day is from the break of dawn to... Section: Regarding what does not require pre-dawn intention and specification of intention, and what requires that. As for the category for which specification of intention is not required for what he fasts, nor is its pre-dawn timing required, that is: the performance of Ramadan, and the performance of a vowed fast whose time is specified, like saying: 'By Allah, upon me is the fast of Thursday of this week.' So if he intends an absolute fast from its night up to before half of the day, it is valid and he fulfills the vow. And the performance of voluntary fast. So each of these three is valid with a specific intention made at night, which is best. The reality of intention is his resolve in his heart to fast tomorrow, and a Muslim is not devoid of this on the nights of Ramadan, except what is vowed. And intention by tongue is not a condition, as in al-Ikhtiyar. And the interpretation of what is reported from his saying: 'There is no fast for one who does not pre-dawn the intention from the night' is carried on the negation of perfection, like his saying: 'There is no prayer for the neighbor of the mosque except in the mosque,' and it is interpreted otherwise. And voluntary fast is excepted from it, so it is not categorical in requiring pre-dawn intention. Likewise, each of them is valid with an intention from the beginning of the day to before half of the day, because the condition is the existence of intention during most of the day as a precaution, and by that it exists in all of it juridically, since for most things the ruling of the whole applies in many issues of fiqh. And this is specific to fasting, excluding Hajj and prayer, because fasting is a single [extended] pillar, so its existence in most of it makes it considered as existing in all of it, unlike them because they are multiple pillars, so it is necessary for intention to accompany the commencement of performance, otherwise some pillars would be devoid of it, so that pillar would not be an act of worship. And we only said: until before half of the day following al-Jami' al-Saghir, not as al-Quduri mentioned by his saying: 'what is between' i.e., dawn and midday, because at midday most of the day has not remained. So our saying 'on the most correct' is a precaution against it. And he only said: 'and half of the day from the beginning of dawn to just before'
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a new section (fasl) of Nur al-Idah regarding the requirements of intention (niyyah) for fasting. The author distinguishes between fasts that require specification (ta'yin) of the type of fast and pre-dawn intention (tabyit), and those that do not.

According to Hanafi fiqh, for three types of fasts – Ramadan, a vowed fast with a specified time (e.g., 'I vow to fast on Thursday of this week'), and voluntary fasts – it is not required to specify the exact type of fast in the intention, nor is it required to make the intention before dawn. The intention can be made from the night up to just before half the day (i.e., before the midpoint between dawn and sunset). The best time is at night, but it is also valid if made before half the day.

The author explains the hadith, 'There is no fast for one who does not pre-dawn the intention from the night,' is interpreted as referring to perfection, not obligation, similar to the phrase 'no prayer for the neighbor of the mosque except in the mosque.' Voluntary fasts are explicitly excepted. The rationale is that fasting is a single extended pillar, so having intention for most of the day suffices, unlike prayer and Hajj which have multiple parts.

The author also notes a disagreement within the Hanafi school: al-Quduri (in Mukhtasar al-Quduri) said intention is valid until zawal (midday), but the correct view (from al-Jami' al-Saghir) is that it is valid until just before half the day, because after half the day, most of the day has passed. This is the mufta bihi position.

The passage is incomplete, breaking off at 'just before' (qabīl), likely continuing on the next page.

Key Terms
تببيت النية (tabyit al-niyyah): Making the intention for fasting before dawn; i.e., from the night before.
تعيين النية (ta'yin al-niyyah): Specifying the type of fast in the intention (e.g., 'I intend to fast Ramadan').
نصف النهار (nisf al-nahar): Half of the day, counted from dawn (fajr) to sunset; the midpoint between them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct Hanafi view, until what time can intention be made for a Ramadan fast?
A) Only before Fajr.
B) Until zawal (midday).
C) Until before half the day (midpoint between dawn and sunset).
D) Until sunset.

Key Takeaway
For Ramadan, vowed fasts with fixed time, and voluntary fasts, intention is valid from night up to before half the day; specification is not required.

Ruling on Intention for Fasting: Time and Specification
Explains when intention for fasting need not be made at night nor specified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three types of fasts that do not require intention at night nor specification and explain the time limit for making intention, including the Hanafi interpretation of the hadith and the dispute regarding travelers and the sick.

Core Idea
For Ramadan, specified vows, and nafl, intention can be made until before midday—the Hanafi school does not require intention from the night.

Intention (niyyah) is a condition for the validity of fasting, but when exactly must it be made? The Hanafi school makes a key distinction: some fasts require intention from the night, while others allow it to be made during the day. This lesson examines the latter category, covering the three types of fasts exempted from tabyit and ta'yin, the time window for intention, and the intra-madhhab differences concerning travelers and the sick.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three exempted fasts
Find in the text which types of fasts do not require intention at night (tabyit) nor specification (ta'yin). List them: Ramadan, specified vow, nafl.
A specified vow is a particular day, e.g., 'I will fast Thursday.'
2
Determine the time window for intention
The text states intention can be made 'from the night to before half the day'. Clarify what half the day means: from dawn to just before the great awakening (close to noon).
The best time is from night, but permissibility extends until before half the day.
3
Analyze the hadith proof
The hadith 'No fast for one who does not intend from the night' appears to require tabyit. The Hanafis interpret it as denial of perfection, not validity, analogous to 'No prayer for the neighbor of the mosque except in the mosque.'
Nafl is exempted, showing the hadith is not absolute.
4
Apply the ruling to absolute intention
Understand that for these three fasts, absolute intention (without specifying the type) suffices, and even intention of nafl counts as Ramadan or the specified vow.
This applies even for travelers and sick, with a dispute detailed later.
5
Evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute
For a traveler intending another wajib, Abu Hanifa says it counts as that wajib; Sahibayn say it counts as Ramadan. For the sick, two narrations from Abu Hanifa. The mufta bihi is that absolute intention counts as Ramadan.
The practical difference concerns whether qada is required.

Section on what does not require intention to be made at night and specified, and what requires that. As for the category that does not require specification of intention, nor does it require tabyit (making intention at night), it is: performing Ramadan, specified vow whose time is specified, and supererogatory fasts. So it is valid with intention from the night to before half the day, according to the most correct view. Half of the day is from the dawn until... Section: on what does not require intention at night and specification. As for the category that does not require specification of intention for what he fasts, nor does it require tabyit (i.e., the intention) for it, it is: performing Ramadan, and (performing) the specified vow whose time is specified, like saying: I owe Allah the fast of Thursday of this week. So if he intends absolute fasting from its night to before half of the day, it is valid and he fulfills the vow. And (performing) supererogatory fasting. So each of these three is valid with a specified intention made at night, which is superior. The reality of intention is his resolve in his heart that he will fast tomorrow. No Muslim is devoid of this on the nights of Ramadan, except what he vowed. Intention by the tongue is not a condition, as in 'al-Ikhtiyar'. The interpretation of what is narrated from his saying: 'No fast for one who does not intend fasting from the night' is taken as denial of perfection, like his saying: 'No prayer for the neighbor of the mosque except in the mosque' and it is interpreted as other than that. Supererogatory fasting has been exempted from it, so it is not definitive in requiring tabyit. Similarly, each of these is valid with intention from the beginning of the day to before half of the day, because the condition is the existence of intention in most of the day as a precaution, and by that it exists in all of it legally, since the majority of a thing has the ruling of all of it in many jurisprudential contexts. This is specific to fasting, excluding Hajj and prayer, because fasting is a single extended pillar, so its existence in most of it is considered its existence in all of it, unlike them, for they are multiple pillars, so their conjunction with the contract at the time of performing is required; otherwise, some pillars would be devoid of it, and that pillar would not occur as an act of worship. We said: until before half of the day, following al-Jami' al-Saghir, not as al-Quduri mentioned by his saying: what is between dawn and noon, because at noon most of the day has not remained, so our saying 'according to the most correct view' is a reservation against his view. He said: half of the day from the beginning of dawn until just before the time of al-sahwa al-kubra (the great awakening), not at it, as in al-Tatarkhaniyya from al-Nihaya, because the day may be linguistically applied to what is from sunrise to sunset, and at noon it is half, so the condition of validity of intention would be lost if it occurs just before noon. Also, each of Ramadan, specified vow, and supererogatory fasting is valid with absolute intention, i.e., intending fasting without specifying whether it is obligatory, mandatory, or sunnah, because Ramadan is a standard where no other fast is prescribed, so it is specifically for the obligatory act; and the specified matter needs no specification, and the specified vow is considered by Allah's command, so each is hit by absolute intention, and also by intention of supererogatory fast, even if he is a traveler or sick, according to the most correct of the two narrations from Imam (Abu Hanifa). This is the choice of Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma and a group, because the foundation of intention suffices, and the additional specification is ignored, because the concession was legislated out of kindness to the traveler and sick; so if they undertake hardship, they join the non-excused, considering them by waiving what would be required to make up and the punishment for leaving it when they are able to make up later. So its occurrence as obligatory is better. In another narration: it occurs as supererogatory for him, because since it is permissible for him to leave fasting, it is permissible for him to occupy himself with what is more preferable in his sight, like a day outside Ramadan. This is chosen by a group. When the sick or traveler makes an absolute intention, it occurs for Ramadan, mentioned in al-Muhit without relating any disagreement. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: if the traveler makes an absolute intention, the correct view is that it occurs for Ramadan, as in al-Kashf, i.e., according to all narrations. It is said: if absolute, it does not occur for the obligatory. End. (Performing Ramadan with intention of another obligatory) is valid for one who is healthy, resident, because it is a standard, so it is hit by mistake in the description, like absolute intention, i.e., mistake legally; even if he intended it, he is mistaken, like the unique one in the house, he is hit by his generic name, and with mistake in his name, if called, he responds. Unlike the traveler, for if he intends another obligatory, it occurs for what he intended of that obligatory, as one narration from Abu Hanifa, because he occupied the time with the more important. Ramadan in his case is like Sha'ban for the resident, so he directs it to what is upon him. His concession is attached to absolute travel, which exists. Even if he does not make up later, he does not have to make up Ramadan and is not sinful, unlike what was upon him before. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it occurs for Ramadan. And the matter of preponderance is disputed regarding the sick if he intends another obligatory in Ramadan, because there are two narrations from the Imam.
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the conditions of intention (niyyah) for fasting. It divides fasts into three categories that do not require the intention to be made at night (tabyit) nor specify which fast is intended: the obligatory Ramadan fast, a specified vow (e.g., fasting a specific day), and supererogatory (nafl) fasts. For these, it is valid to make the intention from the night until just before midday (nisf al-nahar). The author clarifies that the best time is from the night, but the permissibility extends into the day. The ruling is based on a hadith that seems to require intention from the night, but the Hanafi school interprets it as indicating perfection, not obligation, and exempts nafl from it. The time limit is set at half the day because fasting is a single continuous act; having intention in most of the day suffices. The passage then discusses validity with absolute intention (not specifying the type of fast) and with intention for nafl, even for a traveler or sick person, according to Abu Hanifa's preferred view. A dispute follows: if a healthy resident intends another obligatory fast in Ramadan, it counts as Ramadan. For a traveler, Abu Hanifa says it counts as the other obligatory (one narration), while the Sahibayn say it counts as Ramadan. For the sick, there are two narrations. The commentary emphasizes the Hanafi position that intention is a heart act and that the ruling is based on istihsan and flexibility for the fasting person.

Key Terms
تَبْيِيت النية (tabyīt al-niyyah): Making the intention for fasting during the night before the day of fasting.
تَعْيِين النية (ta'yīn al-niyyah): Specifying which fast one intends (e.g., Ramadan, vow, etc.) when making the intention.
نصف النهار (niṣf al-nahār): Midday; but here defined as the time from dawn until just before the 'great awakening' (al-ṣaḥwa al-kubrā), which is close to noon but not exactly noon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Until what time can a person make intention for fasting Ramadan according to the Hanafi school?
A) Until sunset
B) Until just before midnight of the previous night
C) Until before half of the day (before midday)
D) Only from the night

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits delaying intention for Ramadan, specified vows, and nafl until before midday, and absolute intention suffices due to the nature of these fasts.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
6 min
Rulings on Intention for Ramadan Fast: Traveler, Sick, and Resident
Explains validity of various intentions for Ramadan fast for different persons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of mutlaq niyyah and niyyah naql for Ramadan fast for a traveler or sick person, and distinguish the intra-madhhab dispute regarding intending another wajib fast during Ramadan.

Core Idea
Mutlaq niyyah suffices for Ramadan even for traveler and sick; but intending another wajib during Ramadan triggers an intra-Hanafi dispute.

The intention (niyyah) for fasting Ramadan has specific conditions. This passage from Imdad al-Fattah expands on the rulings for different persons: healthy residents, travelers, and the sick. You will learn when a general intention suffices and when specifying the type matters, as well as the internal Hanafi differences on what happens if one intends another obligatory fast during Ramadan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
We are looking at the validity of intention (niyyah) for the fast of Ramadan. The passage rules that mutlaq niyyah and niyyah naql are valid for all persons, and that intending another wajib is valid for a healthy resident but disputed for traveler and sick.
Focus on the categories: resident, traveler, sick.
2
Locate the proof
The proof for the validity of mutlaq niyyah for traveler/sick is that the basic intention suffices and specifying direction is waived due to the license (rukhsah). The proof for the healthy resident's validity of intending another wajib is that Ramadan is a fixed measure (mi'yar) so it is hit by error in description.
The reasoning uses qiyas and the concept of 'error in description'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
For traveler/sick, the reasoning is that if they bear the hardship, they are treated like non-excused and the fast becomes for Ramadan. For resident, the fast is valid for Ramadan even if another wajib is intended because the time is fixed.
Understand the analogy: traveler bearing hardship ⟷ resident, making the fast count for Ramadan.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds that a traveler's intention of another wajib falls on that wajib, while the Sahibayn hold it falls on Ramadan. For the sick, there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa.
This is a dispute within the Hanafi school; the Sahibayn are Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
For the sick, the mufta bihi is that the fast falls for Ramadan according to al-Burhan and the choice of Fakhr al-Islam. For the traveler, the text does not give a clear tarjih.
The mufta bihi is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقت الصحوة الكبرى، ويصح أيضاً مُطلَق النّيّة، وبنية النقل، ولو كان مسافراً، أو مريضاً في الأصح ويصح أداء رمضانَ بنية واجب آخر ، لمن كان صحيحاً، مُقيماً بخلاف ا المسافرِ، فَإِنَّهُ يقعُ عمَّـا نـواهُ مَنَ الواجب واختُلِفَ التَّرْجِيحُ في المريض إذا نوى واجباً آخر في رمضان.. ( وقتِ الصَّحْوَةِ الكُبرى لا عندها، كما في ( التاتارخانية ) عن ( النهاية»، لأن النهار قد يطلق علـى ما عند طلوع الشمس إلى غروبها لغة، وعند الزوال نصفه فيفوت شرط صحة النية لوجودها قبيل الزوال. ( ويصح أيضاً كل من أداء رمضان والنذر المعين، والنفل بمُطْلَقِ النِّيَّةِ) بأن ينوي الصوم من غير تقييد بوصف الفرض، أو الواجب، أو السنة؛ لأن رمضان معيار لم يشرع فيـه صــوم آخر، فكان متعيناً للفرض، والمتعين لا يحتاج إلى التعيين والنذر المعين معتبر بإيجاب الله تعالى، فيصاب كل بمطلق النية، وبنيّة النَّفْلِ) أيضاً (ولوْ كانَ) الذي قد نوى (مسافراً، أو) كــان (مريـضـاً في الأصح من الروايتين عند الإمام، وهو اختيار «فخر الإسلام»، و«شمس الأئمة وجمع، لأن أصل النية كاف، ويلغي زيادة الجهة، لأن الرخصة شرعت رفقاً بالمسافر والمريض، فإذا تحملا المشقة التحقا بغير المعذور، نظراً لهما بإسقاط ما يلزم قضاؤه والعقوبة بتركه عند إدراك عدة من أيام أُخر ، فكان وقوعه عن الفرض أولى، وفي رواية: يقع نفلاً له، لأنه لما جاز إخلاؤه عــن الـصـوم جاز له الشغل بالراجح في نظره، كاليوم الخارج عن رمضان واختاره جمع، وإذا أطلق المريض أو المسافر النية فإنه يقع عن رمضان ذكره في ( المحيط ) من غير حكاية خلاف، وفي « التاتارخانية » : إذا أطلق المسافر النية فالصحيح أن يقع عن رمضان، كما في ( الكشف ) أي: على جميع الروايات، وقيل: إذا أطلق لا يقع عن الفرض انتهى . ( ويصح أداء رمضان بنية واجـب آخر) هذا (لمن كان صحيحاً، مُقيماً) لما أنَّه معيار (۱) فيصاب بالخطأ في الوصف، كمطلق النية أي: الخطأ شــرعـاً، ولـو قصده فهو مخطئ، كالمتوحد في الدار يصاب باسم جنسه، ومع الخطأ في اسمه إذا نودي أجاب، (بخلاف المسافر ، فإنَّهُ إذا نوى واجباً آخر (يقعُ عمَّا نواهُ منَ ذلك (الواجب) رواية واحــدة عــن أبي حنيفة»، لأنَّه شغل الوقت بالأهم، ورمضان في حقه كشعبان في حق المقيم، فيصـرفـه إلـى مـا عليه، ورخصته متعلقة بمطلق السفر وقد وجد، حتى لو لم يدرك عدة من أيــام أُخر لا يلزمه قضـاء رمضان فلا يأثم، بخلاف ما كان عليه قبله، وقالا : يقع عن رمضان (واخْتُلِفَ التَّرْجِيحُ في) صوم ( المريض إذا نوى واجباً آخر ) بصومه (في) شهر (رمضان)، لأنه روي عن الإمام فيه روايتان، روى الحسن» عنه أنه كالمسافر يقع عما نوى، وهو اختيار صاحب ( الهداية»، وأكثر مشايخ بخارى؛ لأن رخصته متعلقة بخوف ازدياد المرض لا بحقيقة العجز ، فكان كالمسافر في تعلـق الرخصة في حقه بعجز مقدر، كذا في فتح القدير » ، وقال الشيخ أكمل الدين في ( العناية » : هذا الذي اختاره المصنف [ من التسوية بين المسافر والمريض مخالف لما ذكره العلمـان في التحقيق ] فخــر الإسلام وشمس الأئمة فإنهما قالا : إذا نوى المريض عن واجب آخر، فالصحيح أنه يقع صومه عــــن رمضان انتهى. وفي ( البرهان » : وهو الأصح انتهى.
At the time of full awakening, and also the absolute intention and the intention of transfer are valid even if one is traveling or sick in the most correct view. And performing Ramadan with the intention of another obligatory act is valid for one who is healthy and resident, unlike the traveler, for it falls on what he intended of the obligatory act. And there is a difference of opinion on the preponderance in the case of the sick person if he intends another obligatory act in Ramadan... The time of full awakening, not before it, as in al-Tatarkhaniyya from al-Nihaya, because the day linguistically may be applied from sunrise to sunset, and at midday it is half, so the condition for the validity of intention is missed due to its occurrence just before midday. (And also valid) each of performing Ramadan and the specific vow and supererogatory with absolute intention: by intending fasting without specifying whether obligatory, compulsory, or sunnah, because Ramadan is a fixed measure in which no other fast is legislated, so it is determined for the obligatory, and what is determined does not need specification; and the specific vow is considered by Allah's obligation, so each is achieved by absolute intention, and also by the intention of supererogatory. (Even if the one who intended is) traveling or sick in the most correct of the two narrations from the Imam, and this is the choice of Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma and a group, because the basic intention suffices, and specifying the direction is waived, because the license is legislated as a concession for the traveler and sick, so if they bear hardship they join the non-excused, considering them by dropping what would be required to make up and the punishment for leaving it when they catch up with other days, so its occurrence for the obligatory is more appropriate. In another narration: it falls as supererogatory for him, because since it is permissible to leave it empty of fasting, it is permissible for him to occupy himself with what is more preferable in his view, like a day outside Ramadan, and a group chose this. And if the sick or traveler makes an absolute intention, it falls for Ramadan; mentioned in al-Muhit without reporting a dispute. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: if the traveler makes an absolute intention, the correct view is that it falls for Ramadan, as in al-Kashf, i.e., according to all narrations. It is said: if absolute, it does not fall for the obligatory. End. (And performing Ramadan with the intention of another obligatory act) is valid (for one who is healthy and resident) because it is a fixed measure, so it is hit by an error in description, like absolute intention, i.e., an error legally; even if he intended it, he is mistaken, like an exclusive one in a house hit by the name of his genus, and with an error in his name when called, he responds. (Unlike the traveler, for if he intends another obligatory act, it falls on what he intended of that obligatory act) in one narration from Abu Hanifa, because he occupies the time with the more important, and Ramadan in his case is like Sha'ban for the resident, so it diverts to what is upon him, and his license is attached to the mere travel and it exists, such that if he does not catch up with other days, he is not required to make up Ramadan, so he does not sin, unlike what was upon him before. And the two said: it falls for Ramadan. (And there is a difference of opinion on the preponderance in) the fast of (the sick person if he intends another obligatory act) by his fast (in) the month of (Ramadan), because two narrations are reported from the Imam: Al-Hasan reported from him that he is like the traveler, it falls on what he intended, and this is the choice of the author of al-Hidaya and most scholars of Bukhara, because his license is attached to the fear of increase of illness not to actual incapacity, so he is like the traveler in the attachment of license in his case to estimated incapacity. As in Fath al-Qadir. And Shaykh Akmal al-Din said in al-Inaya: This which the author chose (of equating the traveler and sick) is contrary to what the two scholars (Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma) mentioned in the investigation, for they said: if the sick intends another obligatory act, the correct view is that his fast falls for Ramadan. End. And in al-Burhan: it is the most correct. End.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of rulings on intention (niyyah) for the fast of Ramadan, particularly concerning the validity of various forms of intention for different categories of persons: healthy residents, travelers, and the sick. The author begins by stating that intention at the time of 'full awakening' (al-sahwah al-kubra) is valid, but not before it, as per the Tatarkhaniyya. He then explains that mutlaq (absolute) intention and niyyah of naql (transfer) are valid for the fast of Ramadan, a specific vow (nadhr mu'ayyan), and supererogatory fasts, even if the person is traveling or sick in the most correct view (the choice of Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma). The reasoning is that the essential intention suffices and specifying the direction is waived due to the license (rukhsah) granted to travelers and the sick; if they bear the hardship, they are treated like non-excused persons, making the fast count for the obligatory Ramadan. Another narration states it falls as supererogatory. For a healthy resident, intending another wajib while fasting Ramadan is valid because Ramadan is a fixed measure (mi'yar) and the intention is considered erroneous in description. For a traveler, however, if he intends another wajib, it falls on that wajib according to one narration from Abu Hanifa, because he occupies the time with the more important, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it falls for Ramadan. For the sick, there is a dispute: some equate him with the traveler (making it fall on the intended wajib), while the more correct view according to Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma is that it falls for Ramadan. The passage concludes by stating the latter is the most correct.

Key Terms
مُطلَق النّيّة (mutlaq al-niyyah): An intention to fast without specifying the type (obligatory, vow, etc.).
الصحوة الكبرى (al-sahwah al-kubra): The time of full awakening after the middle of the day, considered the end of the time for making intention for fasting.
نقل (naql): Transfer of intention from one fast to another, e.g., intending a supererogatory fast while fasting Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct Hanafi view, if a sick person intends another obligatory fast during Ramadan, what is the ruling?
A) It falls for the intended wajib.
B) It falls for Ramadan.
C) It is invalid.
D) It is considered supererogatory.

Key Takeaway
Mutlaq niyyah suffices for Ramadan for all persons, but the ruling on intending another wajib differs between healthy residents (valid for Ramadan), travelers (disputed), and sick (mufta bihi: falls for Ramadan).

Ruling on Specified Vowed Fast (Nadhr Mu'ayyan)
States the ruling on nadhr mu'ayyan and introduces fasts requiring tabyit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a specific vowed fast (nadhr mu'ayyan) when the intention is for another wajib, and identify which fasts require specific and night intention (tabyit).

Core Idea
A specified vowed fast cannot be fulfilled by intending another wajib; it must be intended specifically for the nadhr.

This passage clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh regarding vowed fasts. When you have made a vow to fast on a specific day, intending a different obligatory fast (like qada or kaffara) on that day does not fulfill the vow; instead, the fast counts for what you intended, and the vow remains to be fulfilled. The passage also begins to list fasts that require specific intention and intention from the night (tabyit), which will be explored further.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the first sentence: 'ولا يصح المنذور المعين زمانه بنية واجب غيره'. Understand that the specific vowed fast is not valid with the intention of another wajib. The result: the fast falls for the intended wajib, not the nadhr.
The nadhr remains unfulfilled and must be made up.
2
Parse the second category
Notice the phrase 'وأما القسم الثاني' indicating a new category. Identify the fasts listed: qada of Ramadan, qada of supererogatory fasts that were invalidated, expiatory fasts (kaffarat), and absolute vows (nadhr mutlaq). These all require specific intention and night intention.
This is a preview; the full conditions will be detailed later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يصح المنذور المعيَّنُ زمانُهُ بنيّة واجب غيره، بل يقعُ عما نواه من الواجب فيه. وأما القسم الثاني، وهو ما يُشترط له تعيين النية، وتبييتها فهو قضاء رمضان، وقضاء ما أفسدَهُ منْ نَفْلِ. وَصَوْمُ الكفَّارَاتِ بأنواعها. والنَّدْرُ المُطلَقُ، كقوله: إن شفى الله مريضي فعلي صوم يوم، فحصل الشفاء.
And the specific vowed (fast) whose time is determined is not valid with the intention of another wajib, rather it falls on what he intended of the wajib in it. As for the second category, which requires specific intention and night intention: it is qada of Ramadan, qada of what he corrupted from supererogatory, and fasts of expiations of all types, and the absolute vow, such as his saying: if Allah cures my sick, upon me is a fast of a day, then cure occurs.
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct rulings. First, the ruling on a specific vowed fast (nadhr mu'ayyan): if someone intends a different obligatory fast while fasting on that day, the fast does not count for the nadhr; instead, it falls for the intended wajib, and the nadhr must be made up later. This is because the nadhr is determined by the vower's specification, unlike Ramadan which is determined by the Lawgiver. Second, the author begins introducing the second category of fasts that require specific intention and tabyit (intention from the night). This category includes: qada of Ramadan, qada of supererogatory fasts that were invalidated, all types of expiatory fasts (kaffarat), and absolute vows (nadhr mutlaq) that are not tied to a specific time.

Key Terms
المنذور المعين زمانه (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan zamanuhu): A vow where the vower specifies a particular day or time period for the fast.
تبييت النية (tabyit al-niyyah): Intention made during the night before dawn, required for certain fasts like qada and kaffarat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The nadhr is fulfilled.
B) The fast counts for the intended wajib, and the nadhr remains.
C) The fast is invalid.
D) Both are fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
A specified vowed fast (nadhr mu'ayyan) requires its own specific intention; intending another wajib on that day validly discharges that wajib but leaves the vow outstanding.

Fasts Requiring Specific and Night Intention
Lists fasts requiring specific intention and tabyit, defines niyyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the fasts that require specific intention and tabyit, and apply the definition of niyyah to determine valid intention.

Core Idea
Intention must be specific and from the night for fasts without fixed time.

We now explore the second category of fasts, which demand precise intention made during the night. Understanding which fasts require this and the definition of niyyah itself is crucial for valid fasting. This lesson will clarify the categories, the role of intention, and the condition of persistence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of the second category
The passage distinguishes fasts that require both specific intention (ta'yin) and night intention (tabyit). These are fasts that have no fixed time.
2
List the fasts in this category
The author enumerates: qada of Ramadan, qada of supererogatory fasts that were broken, expiatory fasts (kaffarat for oath, zihar, killing, breaking Ramadan fast, hunting penalty, ransom in ihram, tamattu' and qiran), and absolute vows (nadhr mutlaq).
Nadhr mu'ayyan (specific time vow) is not included; it belongs to the first category (not requiring tabyit).
3
Understand the rationale for tabyit
Since these fasts lack a specific time, they can only be determined by an intention made during the night (or at the break of dawn). The obligation is to join intention with the fast; preceding is allowed out of necessity.
4
Define niyyah and its conditions
Niyyah is a firm resolve in the heart to perform the specific fast. Verbalizing is recommended (sunnah). A key condition is persistence (baqa') on the intention from night until fajr.
If one retracts the intention at night, the fast is invalid. Renewal of intention is possible before fajr.
5
Apply the consequence of retraction
If one retracts the intention and then breaks the fast, only qada is required (for Ramadan and nadhr). No kaffarah because the original intention was nullified by retraction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني، وهو ما يُشترط له تعيينُ النِّيِّةِ، وتَبيِيتُهَا) ليتأدى به، ويسقط عن المكلف بـه (فـهـو : قضـاءُ رمضان. وقضاءُ ما أفسدَهُ منْ نَفْلِ. وصَوْمُ الكفارات بأنواعها)، ككفـارة الـيـمـيـن والـظـهـار والـقتــل والإفطار في رمضان، وجزاء الصيد، والفدية في الإحرام، وصوم التمتع والقران)، (والنَّدْرُ المُطْلَقُ) عن تقييده بزمان، وهو إما معلق بشرط وَوُجِدَ (كقوله: إن شفى الله مريضي فعليَّ صَوْمُ يومٍ، فحصل الشفاء) أو مطلق كقوله: الله تعالى علي صوم يوم، فعليه الوفاء به، وإنما اشترط التعيين والتبييت فيها لأنها ليس لها وقت معين فلم يتعين لها إلا بنية من الليل، أو بنية مقارنة لطلوع الفجر وهو لأن الواجب قران النية بالصوم لا تقديمها، وإنما جاز التقديم للضرورة، ثم اعلم بأن النيـة جـزم بالقلب على ما يريد الإتيان به من الصوم [ واستحب المشايخ التلفظ بها، ويشترط في النية البقاء عليها، فلو رجع عما نوى ليلاً ] لم يصر صائماً، ولو أفطر لا شيء عليه إلا القضاء في رمضان والمنذور، لأن تلك النية انتقضت بالرجوع عنها، إلا أن يعود إلى تجديد النية، ويجعل مضيه فيه في
As for the second category, which requires specific intention and night intention, so that it may be performed and the obligation discharged: it is qada of Ramadan, qada of what he corrupted from supererogatory, and fasts of expiations of all types, such as expiation of oath, zihar, killing, breaking fast in Ramadan, penalty of hunting, ransom in ihram, and fast of tamattu' and qiran, and the absolute vow (nadhr mutlaq) not tied to a time. It is either conditional upon a condition that occurs (like his saying: if Allah cures my sick, upon me is a fast of a day, then cure occurs) or absolute like his saying: upon me by Allah is a fast of a day; he must fulfill it. And specific intention and tabyit are required for these because they have no specific time, so they become specific only by intention from the night, or by intention concurrent with the break of dawn, because the obligation is to join intention with the fast, not to precede it; preceding is only allowed out of necessity. Then know that intention is a resolve of the heart upon what one intends to perform of fasting. And the scholars recommend verbalizing it. And it is required to persist in the intention; if one retracts what he intended at night, he does not become a faster, and if he breaks his fast, nothing is upon him except qada in Ramadan and the vowed (fast), because that intention is invalidated by retraction, unless he returns to renew the intention, and he makes his passing in it in the
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage defines the second category of fasts that require both specific intention (ta'yin al-niyyah) and night intention (tabyit). These include: qada of Ramadan, qada of supererogatory fasts that one invalidated, all types of expiatory fasts (kaffarat: for oath, zihar, killing, breaking Ramadan fast, hunting penalty, ransom in ihram, and tamattu' and qiran), and absolute vows (nadhr mutlaq) that are not tied to a specific time. The reason is that these fasts have no fixed time, so they can only be determined by an intention made during the night or exactly at the break of dawn. The passage then provides a definition of intention (niyyah): it is a firm resolve in the heart to perform the specific fast. Scholars recommend verbalizing it. An important condition is persistence (baqa') on the intention; if one retracts the intention at night, the fast is not valid. If he then breaks the fast, no penalty is due except to make up the fast in the case of Ramadan or vowed fasts, because the original intention was nullified. However, one can renew the intention.

Key Terms
تبييت النية (tabyit al-niyyah): Making intention from the night before, i.e., before the break of dawn.
النذر المطلق (al-nadhr al-mutlaq): A vow not tied to a specific time, e.g., 'I owe Allah a fast'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following fasts does NOT require tabyit (night intention) according to this passage?
A) Qada of Ramadan
B) Kaffarat of oath
C) Nadhr mu'ayyan
D) Nadhr mutlaq

Key Takeaway
Fasts without fixed time (qada, kaffarat, nadhr mutlaq) require a specific intention made from the night, and the intention is a firm heart-resolve with an obligation of persistence.

Ruling on Specified Vow (Nadhr Mu'ayyan) and the Sick Person's Fast Analogy
Explains the ruling on specified vow with an analogy to the sick person's fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a specified vow fast when a different wajib is intended, and distinguish the position of Fakhr al-Islam from that of the author of al-Hidayah regarding the sick person's fast.

Core Idea
A specified vow fast can be overridden by another wajib intention, but the vow remains obligatory to make up.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh: if you have a vowed fast fixed to a specific day and you intend a different obligatory fast (like qada of Ramadan) on that day, what happens? The source passage clarifies that the fast counts for the intended wajib, not the vow, and the vow must be made up later. It also opens a digression on whether a sick person fasting Ramadan can similarly intend another wajib, revealing an intra-Hanafi dispute. We will walk through the ruling, its proof, the analogy with the traveler, and the different positions of later Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its conditions
The passage begins by stating that a specified vow fast is not valid with the intention of another wajib. Instead, the fast falls as the intended wajib. Identify the act (vowed fast) and the condition (intending a different wajib).
The ruling is conditional: it applies only when another wajib is intended, not nafl.
2
Locate the dalil (proof) and its structure
The proof is talil (reasoning) based on analogy (qiyas). The sick person is compared to the traveler, whose concession allows substitution. The author cites al-Hasan's narration and the choice of al-Hidayah. Trace the wajh al-dalalah: the sick person's license is based on fear of increased illness, not actual inability, similar to the traveler's presumed inability.
The analogy is contested within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the sick person
The commentator notes that Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma disagree with equating the sick to the traveler. They hold that the sick person's fast of Ramadan counts for Ramadan even if another wajib is intended. Identify their proof: the obligation of Ramadan remains binding and the license does not extend to overriding the original obligation.
The passage states that al-Burhan considers the view of Fakhr al-Islam as 'more correct' (asahh).
4
Evaluate the tarjih and the mufta bihi
Despite the dispute, the passage confirms that for the vowed fast, all narrations agree that the fast falls as the intended wajib and qada of the vow is required. For the sick person, the mufta bihi according to later authorities is the view of Fakhr al-Islam (counts for Ramadan).
The distinction between Ramadan and a vow is key: Ramadan's time is set by Allah and cannot be overridden; a vow's time is set by the individual and can.
5
Note the exception and the ruling on nafl intention
The passage adds: if the person intended nafl instead of wajib, the fast counts as the vow. Also, there is a narration from Abu Hanifa that the fast counts as the intended wajib within the specified time. Understanding this exception completes the picture.
This shows the authority of the vow-maker over voluntary acts but not over obligatory ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يصح المنذور المعيَّنُ زمانُهُ بنيّة واجب غيره، بل يقعُ عما نواه من الواجب فيه. الحسن عنه أنه كالمسافر يقع عما نوى، وهو اختيار صاحب الهداية، وأكثر مشايخ بخارى؛ لأن رخصته متعلقة بخوف ازدياد المرض لا بحقيقة العجز، فكان كالمسافر في تعلق الرخصة في حقه بعجز مقدر، كذا في فتح القدير، وقال الشيخ أكمل الدين في العناية: هذا الذي اختاره المصنف من التسوية بين المسافر والمريض مخالف لما ذكره العلمان في التحقيق فخر الإسلام وشمس الأئمة فإنهما قالا: إذا نوى المريض عن واجب آخر، فالصحيح أنه يقع صومه عن رمضان انتهى. وفي البرهان: وهو الأصح انتهى. ولا يصح أي لا يسقط المنذور المعين زمانه بصومه بنية واجب غيره، بل يقع عما نواه الناذر من الواجب المغاير للمنذور في الروايات كلها، ويلزمه قضاء ما نذره، كما في التاتارخانية، وقيدنا بصوم واجب لأنه لو نى نفلاً وقع عن المنذور المعين كإطلاق النية، وروي عن أبي حنيفة أنه يكون عما نواه فيه أي في الزمن الذي عينه للمنذور، والفرق بينه وبين رمضان أن رمضان تعين بتعيين الشارع، وله ولاية إبطال صلاحيته لغيره من الصيام فلا يقع لغيره مع تعيين ذلك الغير، والمنذور تعين بتعيين الناذر وله إبطال صلاحية ما له وهو النفل لا ما عليه، وهو القضاء، ونحوه كذا في التبيين.
And the specified vowed fast whose time is specified is not valid with the intention of another obligatory fast, rather it falls on what he intended of the obligatory [fast] in it. Al-Hasan narrated from him that he is like the traveler, it falls on what he intended, and this is the choice of the author of al-Hidayah and most scholars of Bukhara, because his concession is related to fear of increase of illness not to actual inability, so he is like the traveler in the attachment of the concession in his case to a presumed inability, as in Fath al-Qadir. And Shaykh Akmal al-Din said in al-Inayah: This which the author chose of equating the traveler and the sick person is contrary to what the two great scholars, Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma, mentioned in verification, for they said: When a sick person intends for another wajib, the correct view is that his fast falls for Ramadan, end. And in al-Burhan: it is the more correct, end. And it is not valid, meaning it does not count, the specified vowed fast of its specified time by fasting it with the intention of another wajib, rather it falls as what the vow-maker intended of the wajib different from the vowed in all the narrations, and he must make up what he vowed, as in al-Tatarakhaniyyah. And we restricted [the ruling] to fasting a wajib because if he intended a nafl, it falls as the specified vow like the absolute intention. And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that it is what he intended in it, i.e., in the time he specified for the vow. And the difference between it and Ramadan is that Ramadan is determined by the determination of the Lawgiver, and it has the authority to render itself unsuitable for other fasts, so it does not fall for another with the determination of that other. And the vow is determined by the determination of the vow-maker, and he has the authority to render unsuitable what he has, which is the nafl, not what is upon him, which is the qada, and the like, as in al-Tabyin.
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and includes an intra-Hanafi dispute. It opens by stating the core ruling: if someone has a specified vowed fast (e.g., I must fast on a specific day for a vow) and intends a different wajib fast on that day (e.g., qada of Ramadan), the fast does not count for the vow but rather counts as the different wajib he intended. However, he must still make up the original vow later. The author then enters a digression about the sick person fasting Ramadan and intending another wajib. Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrates from Abu Hanifa that the sick person is like the traveler: his fast counts for what he intended (the other wajib). This is the choice of the author of al-Hidayah and many Bukhara scholars, reasoning that the sick person's license is based on fear of increased illness, not actual inability, so he resembles the traveler. Shaykh Akmal al-Din objects that this equating is contrary to the position of Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma, who hold that a sick person's fast of Ramadan counts for Ramadan even if he intended another wajib, and this is the more correct view according to al-Burhan. Returning to the vow ruling, the text elaborates that if he intended a nafl instead of a wajib, the fast counts as the vow. There is also a narration from Abu Hanifa that the fast counts as the intended wajib in the specified time. The difference between Ramadan and a vow is explained: Ramadan's time is set by Allah, and it has the authority to invalidate other intentions, so it cannot be overridden. A vow's time is set by the person himself, and he can invalidate his own right (the nafl) but not his obligation (the qada). This ruling is consistent across all narrations.

Key Terms
المنذور المعين (al-mandhūr al-muʿayyan): A vowed fast that is specified with a particular time, e.g., 'I must fast on Friday if Allah heals my sick.'
نية واجب غيره (niyyatu wājib ghayrihi): Intending a different obligatory fast (e.g., qada of Ramadan or a kaffarah) while fasting on the day of a specified vow.
المسافر (al-musāfir): A traveler who is permitted to break the fast of Ramadan. In analogy, the sick person is compared to him regarding the validity of intending another wajib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone has a specified vow to fast on a particular day but intends qada of Ramadan on that day, what happens according to the relied-upon Hanafi view?
A) The fast counts for the vow, and qada of Ramadan is missed.
B) The fast counts for qada of Ramadan, and the vow must be made up later.
C) The fast is invalid, and both must be made up.
D) The fast counts for both simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a specified vow fast is overridden by the intention of a different wajib, but the vow obligation persists and must be fulfilled later; this contrasts with the Shafi'i position and has an intra-Hanafi parallel in the ruling on the sick person's fast.

Sawm — Fasting
